The Australian Play Competition
The Red Page
Some 30 one-act plays were submitted for
this competition, but none of outstanding
dramatic quality. The chief faults disclosed
were: the excessive use of soliloquy, a con-
ventional plot, long-windedness of dialogue,
and speech that was literary rather than
natural. But the art of the drama has been
but newly born here, and the competition
has proved that, with proper nourishment,
the infant will survive and grow to a fine
manhood.
Evidence of the newness of the task im-
posed was found in the fact that many of
the one-act plays submitted had two or more
scenes —'‘Diamond Cut Diamond” had a tre-
mendous cast and six scenes. The essence
of the one-acter, as contrasted with the play,
is that the scene must not change. If the
dramatist cannot tell his story without
shifting the stage-fittings, he has failed. Like
the short story the one-act play must have
no interval in its action: it must embody
one idea and present that idea in the brief-
est possible compass.
&
Let us dismiss some of the failures.
“De Profundis” is a riot of soliloquy.
Each character in turn nervously waits till
the other characters get off the stage in order
to unburden his or her soul to the furniture.
In the midst of his long speech to the atmo-
sphere one character naively asks the car-
pet, “But why soliloquise?” and, as the
carpet maintains a stubborn silence, goes on
cheerfully. soliloquising. “The Girl from
Boggabri,” a poor farce, opens with Sarah
Jane dusting the furniture and telling the
couch large chunks of family history. Mr.
Soliloquy is also the chief character in
“Love's Progress.” This play hat. an impos-
sible poet, and the commonplace dialogue
has not the faintest semblance of dramatic
interest. There is a tiny surprise in the
climax, but for it the audience would not
have waited. “The Marquis,” a foolish
farce* begins,' too, with the time-honored
chat between a girl and the chairs. “The
Black Bag,” a quite conventional comedy, is
bespattered with “asides.”
The opening of “He Did It” arouses the
audiences’s interest. “An ugly affair!” one
character remarks; but the affair turns out
to be a tame plot developed by long-winded
dialogue, xllso in the ranks of the conven-
tional melodramatic plot, unlit by any dra-
matic interest, is “The Elder Son.” “The
Breakaway” has not even a plot; it is
merely an uninteresting conversation about
a youth who wants to leave home. “The
Old Trail” is a literary thing that is neither
realistic nor dramatic, disfigured by the in-
troduction of characters who do not contri-
bute either to the atmosphere or to the
action. In a one-act play there must be no
character introduced upon whom some part
of the.plot does not pivot.
So much for the kindergarten class. “The
Defender” is a patriotic play. In his intro-
duction the author remarks: “The absence
of action and the general talkiness are in-
tentional —and, I hope, in a small way sym-
bolic.” The play deals with, Australian
“territorials” of 1913, and the threatened in-
vasion from China. It is, unfortunately,
dreadful talky. but its clever climax might,
with a drastically cut dialogue, have -a
chance on the stage. Its characters are
crudely drawn Australian types. The author
has symbolically typified the Yellow inva-
sion by the descent of Chinese burglars upon
the undefended household of unpatriotic
Australians. The moral thus neatly indi-
cated is that Australians will talk but do
nothing for defence.
The title, “The Lepers,” would not do for
this play, as it discloses on the programme
the secret the author wishes to keep, for his
“curtain.” The scene is set in the cabin of
a private vacht, in which the mysterious
lady is vainly seeking to escape from the
pursuing yacht of the man who loves her.
The dialogue is good, but the end conven-
tional. The author shirked the “necessary
scene” between the lover and the lady when
he discovers that she is a leper. That was
the scene the audience had been led to ex-
pect; and in a matter like this an audience
must not be “had.” In “Mozart’s Requiem
the pathos is too obvious, though the sad
thing is well atmosphered. In “Chatterton,
by the same author, {here is the same too
palpable pathos—added to a tremendous
death-soliloquy by the poet. There is. how-
ever. a prettv little conventional plot, and
the plav would act in a well-mannered sen-
timental way. Leprosy is again the subject
of “The Remedy,” which opens with one
character “discovered in an attitude of
thought,” the necessary preliminary to a
soliloquy. There is a good dramatic interest
in the main theme. A man knows that he
has leprosy, and. to deceive his wife, pretends
that he is a drunkard. The emergence of a
hero out of a drunken sot is an interesting
dramatic motive; but the author has added
unnecessary sub-plots that fail to heighten
the pathos* of the main theme, and are, in
fact, quite unconnected with the develop-
ment of the chief action.
“Diamond Cut Diamond, a compressed
three-act play in six scenes, has plenty of
Australian atmosphere and vigorous action;
but most of the characters have an annoy-
ing habit of literary quotation. The char-
acter drawing is convincing; but the whole
thing is merely a good cattlerstealing cine-
matograph drama. A pleasant farce-
comedy. written in good Australian, but
with a conventional plot, is The Scare-
crow ” “Ruth Hillman.” labelled a tragedy,
shows us the happily engaged couple m the
.backblocks, to whom enters the woman of
tlu 1 man’s past. TLlie talk is the talk of
books, the situation is tarnished with age,
and the play is extended to two acts when
one should have sufficed.
We are now left with- five competitors for
the prize. ‘‘ln the Days of the Strike”
shows us all the stock characters —the man,
the woman, the factory proprietor, the
strike-leader, the children ; and the problem
set is whether the man will blackleg under
pressure from his sttfrving wife and hungry
children, not forgetting the “unborn babe."
The types are neatly portrayed; but the
play is vitiated by the fact that, as a rule,
women do not starve in Australia when
their husbands are oHt on strike. If it
comes to the point that strike-pay is no
longer available, the strike collapses. The
play is machine-made, with imported
machinery, and the moral sticks too prom-
inently out.
“At the Slip Rails” has more humanity
in it. It is the only comedy that is written
with real Australian humor, but the thing
is too slight in conception, and possesses no
element of dramatic surprise. The soliloquy
of the girl “waiting for the male” at the slip-
rails is justified; for in the back-blocks the
slip-rails are' usually the only things for a
girl to speak to. Thus the soliloquy, ban-
ished from the modern theatre overseas, may,
for years to come, find a comfortable home
in the Slip-Rail Drama of Australia. Her
soliloquy is interrupted by the arrival of
Dad with his gun, also waiting for the male.
The dialogue is delightfully natural; but
the climax belongs to the world of the novel.
The author is advised to keep on.
The three plays left in all deal with the
same subject—probably because it is the
stock subject for drama —the lonely wife
who is about to go off to civilisation with the
other bloke. In “Anoli the Blind,” c\
tragedy in a remote homestead in N.Q., the
woman is not even a wife. All the chief
characters are Italians, and passion and
jealousy and hatred and murder make the
plot. Anoli has been blinded by the woman
in an attempt to murder him, and he moves,
a sinister figure, through this sinister atmo-
phere. The play is tensely written, but the
ending is arbitrary, though dramatically
effective. It is a slice of life that leaves a
disagreeable taste.
“The Climax,” bv Edward Dyson, 164
Barkley-street, St. Ivilda, is more like the
real thing. The action takes place in,front
of a small bush hut; and with an expert
hand Dyson draws the brooding wife, ob-
sessed by the desolate loneliness of the bush,
and the husband unable to understand that
the silence is almost driving her mad. The
opening, where the wife spiritlessly washes
the clothes, and the husband fusses over his
new gun, has the well-known quality of
Dyson humor and pathos. The husband
goes off for the day, and the lover appears.
Her longing to escape from the bush, not
her love for him, is her weakness; but she
has resolution enough to refuse the ’over.
And during this scene we catch a glimpse of
the husband, unexpectedly returned, reach-
ing out of the window and taking what he
thinks is his gun, but is a similar gun
brought in by the lover. The scene ends
with the lover going out to shoot himself in
the saplings if the woman does not follow
him within five minutes. She refuses to go.
A shot is heard —but it would be unfair to
detail the unexpected climax here. The
play is realistic, lit with understanding of
humanity, and crudely dramatic.
“The Kid’s Fingers,” by J. Stanley
Hughes, “Wynella,” Fletcher-street, Bondi,
Sydney, is an equally admirable play, set in
a less melodramatic key. Into the selector’s
hut the husband comes, happily home to find
the one-year-old kid sleeping in its cot and a
letter from his wife telling him she has gone.
He takes the shock with fine bravery. On
his departure into the next room, the wife
and lover appear. She has made the lover
drive back, for she cannot go without her
child. The man stays outside ; and while she
is going to pick the kid up, she is startled to
find her husband confronting her. To her
consternation her husband assumes that she
has come back to him, and takes the blame
upon himself for her dreary life. He speaks
hopefully of a brighter future. .. .with a
gramophone and a visit for her and the kid
to Sydney. Then, dead tired, he lies down
and falls asleep—a theatric convention that
the audience will easily forgive. Here is
the opportunity for the woman again. But,
with the child in her arms, she is suddenly
overcome, and slams the door in her lover’s
face. Then, with the noise of wheels and
galloping hoofs dying away, she confesses.
It was the kid’s fingers holding her face
that had kept her. When woman has
sinned or is going to sin, she says, it is not
pity nor kisses she needs, but understanding.
The ending is charming, and the whole thing
a delightfully human play.
The two-guinea “author’s fee” is divided
between the authors of “The Kid’s Fingers”
and the poorly titled “The Climax.” The
last five plays mentioned will be submitted
to the repertory theatres mentioned.

Science Progress, the one outstanding
scientific quarterly. (Murray; 55.). has.in its
latest issue several articles of extreriie in-
terest to the ordinary bloke. “The Planet
Mars” sums fip the knowledge we have of our
neighbor in terms corroborative of the asser-
tions of Prof. Lowell. The author backs
up that observer by showing that there is
ample evidence on that planet of the exist-
ence of “a highly developed and intelligent
race making a last stand against the increas-
ing deserts of its world” —and finds on Mars
a forecast of the ultimate future of life on
STANZAS FROM "THE BUSH."
Yea, Mother Bush, in your deep dreams
abide
Cupids alert for man and maid unborn,
Apprentice Bucks amid, your saplings hide.
And wistful gorges wait a Roland horn:
Wallet of Sigurd shall this sicag replace,
And centaurs curvet where those brumbies
race.
That drover's tale of love shall greaten duly
Through magic prisms of a myriad years,
Till burns Isolde to Tristram’s fervor newly,
Or Launcelot to golden Guinevere's....
Where is Australia, singer, do you know?
These sordid farms, and joyless factories,
Mephitic mines and lanes of pallid woe?
These ugly towns and * cities such as these
With incense sick to all unworthy power.
And all old sin in full malignant flower?
No! to her bourn her children still are far-
ing :
She is a temple that We are to build :
For her the ages have been long preparing:
She is a prophecy to be fulfilled!. .
Yet she shall be as tee, the Potter, mould:
Altar Or tomb, as we aspire, despair:
What wine we bring shall she, the chalice,
hold:
What word we write shall she, the script,
declare:
Bandage our eyes, she shall be Memphis,
Spain:
Barter our souls, she shall be Tyre again:
And if ice pour on her the red oblation
All o’er the world shall Asshur’s buzzards
throng:
Love-lit, her Chaos shall become Creation:
And dewed with dream, her silence flower
in song.
From “The Bush,”
by Bernard O’Dowd.
Bernard O’Dowd
The most interesting development in Aus-
tralian poetry of recent years is the almost
dramatic awakening of Australian bards to
the need of immigration. This continent
must be populated or abandoned to others
who will populate it. The poets have had
little to say, except.a bland approval, to the
policy of the Government of introducing agri-
cultural laborers from overseas, and the
recent threatened stoppage of that immigra-
tion has, so far, aroused no frenzy of poetic
indignation. But in their own department
of immigration there is a recent access of
zeal. This continent lacks, not only
sufficient agricultural laborers, but suffi-
cient gods. The gods of the aborigines
cannot be adapted for white Australian pur-
poses, and compared with’the gods of myth-
ology they are mere stone-age savages.
Hence the clamant need to import, for
poetic purposes, a consignment of seasoned
and well-authenticated gods. Here is a
bare patch of land, eminently suitable for
gods, that has been left uncultivated since
the beginning of time. The bush must be
populated with imported mythological
beings; our nation is ready to welcome
them ; and there seems no reasonable doubt
but that the gods of antiquity can be suc-
cessfully acclimatised, while in due time
there is every possibility that, in the favor-
able environment of Australia, they will pro-
duce new gods, true Australian gods, who
who will no longer speak of Greece and
Norway as “Home.”
This patriotic experiment has already
been tried by poets like Hugh MeCrae and
Frank Morton (and championed in art by
Norman Lindsay, and. in his etchings, by
Lionel Lindsay), while Bernard O'Dowd
has always kept the project in view. Now.
in The Bush (Lothian. Melb., 2s. 6d.) he
puts forward the complete prospectus.
The poem is, despite the inevitable
o’dowdisms, and a plethora of colons, a
fine piece of work, as solid a bit of metrical
architecture as has been produced in Aus-.
tralia. The theme of the long poem is ad-
mirably portrayed, with sudden touches of
true poetry, and many a pregnant phrase.
Its argument is that the bush, which is
the soul of Australia, is the matrix of-the
Australia of the future, that we are but the
forerunners, the bushwhackers of the Ilium
of the south, that all the heroic—and non-
heroic—incidents of these pioneering days
will form the groundwork of the legend that
will, for our descendants, be Australia.
The miner cradling icashdirt by the creek,
Or pulled through darkness dripping to
the plat:
The navvy boring tunnels through the peak:
The farmer grubbing box-trees on the
flat:
The hawker camping by the roadside spring:
The hodman on the giddy scaffolding:
Even
Moths that around the fashion windows
flutfer:
The racecourse spider and the betting fly:
The children romping by the city gutter
are but the rough blocks from which sculp-
tors shall chisel brownie, fairy and fawn.
And myrmidons of some Homeric dream
From Melbourne mob and Sydney push
be drawn.
The humdrum lives that now we tire of,
then ,
Romance shall be. and we heroic men
Treading the vestibule of Golden Ages.
Here the poet drops into liis o’dowdisms:
Scholars to be our prehistoric drama
From Esson’s “Woman lamer shall
restore. ... ,
Our Gilbert Murray uirites ‘‘Euripides” :
Pirani merges in Pythagoras... v
While Benvenuto Morton, drunk with sing-
Sees salamanders in a bush-fire's bed,
And Spencer sails from Alchennga bringing
Intaglios, totems, and Books of the Dead.
■By Way was Homer into Greek trans-
lated. ...
Bowie's entwines with Cagliostro’s name...
Tillett and Mann are Tyler then and Cade. .
And Archibald begets Australia's Muse
Upon an undine red of Chowder Bay
Rose Scott refutes dogmatic Cyril gaily
A hotch-potch of contemporary names and
prose statements, the purposely common-
place emphasised in order to enforce the
poet’s view that Romance is now ; but these
are polemics, not poetry. They are, how-
ever, but the necessary blemishes m the de-
lineation of a patriotic and poetic idea.
Finely, in isolateu stapzas has O Dowd
emphasised the romance around us: —
And, mid the censers of the mountain
musk.
Acolyte bell-birds the Angelas are pealing.
And boobooks moan lone yespers in the
* dusk
but much of his imagery is too new, though
time will soften its glaring tints: —■
The yang-yang screams o'er cactus wilder-
nesses ....
‘ For a complete understanding of this
poem a dictionary of mythology is neces-
sary. But we shall need all our mythology
when the immigrant gods arrive. It
is impossible. in a poem of this
length, to keep up the necessary head of
poetic steam all the way, but there are iso-
lated lines that show the poet’s quality.
O'Dowd is a poet that Australia must take
account of: he has something to say, and
must be allowed to choose his own words,
even though, in so doing, he decimates the
dictionary.
The English Language
In his valuable book, The Task of Social
Hygiene (Constable, per Dymock’s Library),
Havelock Ellis discusses the question of the
world-language of the future. The claims
of English are ably tabulated :
“English is the most concise and laconic
of the great languages. Greek, French and
German are all more expansive, more syl-
labically copious. Latin alone may be said
to equal or surpass English in concentration.
This power of English to attain expression
with a minimum expenditure of energy....
furnishes no claim to preference. for actual
speaking, in which this economy of energy
ceases to be a supreme virtue, since here we
have also to admit the virtues of easy intelli-
gibility and of persuasiveness.... The inapti-
tude of English for the purposes of speech
is well illustrated in our oratory. .. .The
English intellect, with all its fine qualities,
is not sufficiently nimble for either speaker
or hearer to keep up with the swift brevity
of the English tongue. It is a curious fact
that Great Britain takeg the lead in Europe
in the prevalence of stuttering; the language
is probably a factor in this evil pre-eminence,
for it appears that the Chinese, whose lan-
guage is powerfully rhythmic, never stut-
ter.... While an admirably strong and con-
cise language, English is by no means so
adequate in actual speech ; it is not one of-
the languages which can be heard at a long
distance, and. moreover, it lends itself in
speaking to so many contractions that are
not used in writing—so many “can’ts” and
“won’ts” and “don’ts,” which suit English
taciturnity, but slur and ruin English
speech—that English, as spoken, is almost a
different language from that which excites
admiration when written.
"Moreover, it must tte remembered that
English is not a democratic language. It is
not, like the chief Romance languages and
the chief Teutonic languages, practically
homogeneous, made out of one block. It is
formed by the mixture of two utterly unlike
elements, one aristocratic, the other plebeian.
Ever since the Norman lord came over to
England a profound social inequality has
become rooted in the very language. The
exclusive use of English as an international
language would be a misfortune for the na-
tions already using it. since they would be
deprived of that .mental flexibility and emo-
tional sympathy which no discipline can
give so well as the knowledge of a living
foreign tongue.”
The claims of other living languages are
similarly ruled out by the author; and he
suggests thai a language called Ido—an im-
proved language based on Esperanto-—upon
the compilation of which an international
delegation of scientific men is now at work,
furnishes the only solution of an auxiliary
language for the world.

THE Australian Magazine—THE

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Away from the World.
Away from the world,
Where no picture shows are,
Nor mummers to jar
On lite’s ideality,
Wrapped in reality,
Such as you seek
At the end of a week,
Or here,
In a year,
In an island away from the rest
Of the world you’ve exchanged, for repose,
You may dream you have bucked all the
woes
Of unrest from their seat on your chest.
Oh yes, you may dream
That repose is supreme,
And that all the conditions are just what
they seem.
Alone, you may think as you’re gadding
Away in the woodlands apart,
Afar from the crowd that is madding,
With a book or the girl of your heart.
But things are not so, and the crowd that
can “mad,”
In mode most efficient, is that which is sad,
By units and twos,
Alone, on the loose,
And loves to amuse,
With yabber profuse,
The stranger, whose prayer
Is the cry of despair—-
Ah, where
Can I fare
Away from the world?
MAJORCA.
WAIF.
Non-Progressive Joy.
The modern poet should not sing
Of gods who made the old world fair,
Of knights who tilted in the ring,
Of maids with pale and twisted hair;
These things to-day are out of date,
Like sandals, daggers and the like ;
The toiling bard must learn to make
A hymn to match the motor-bike,
The trust, the combine and the strike.
An aeroplane against the sun
Is dropping bird-men to the ground;
Excited people madly run
To catch their limbs on the rebound ;
A phonograph is singing loud,
Its Yankee accent shrill with pain;
While politicians to the crowd
Roar strips of language loud and vain
Their broken pledges to explain.
The trams are shrieking down the wire;
Wild motor-cars, with stench and horn,
Speed on the large, inflated tyre,
Raising a dust of pride and scorn;
Without a pause the message speeds
Through copper net-works spread on high,
While one the trembling ether reads
Across the Telefunken sky,
Making all distances a lie.
These are the things that should engage
The soul of the uplifted bard ;
His hand should smite in splendid rage
The type upon his key-board hard;
His mind should see the years unfurled,
With greater wonders yet to rise,
When new inventions lift the world
To bump its forehead on the skies,
And fact triumphant scorns surprise.
But strangely his perverted mind
Wheels back upon the ancient things ;
A maiden’s dimple he can find
More tuneful than the cashful strings
Loud progress lifts her hands to pull;
Two lips that hold one rosy kiss
Are more to the unseeing fool
Than all the hints of golden bliss
That mining speculators miss.
For thus the cranky ages turn,
And while invention grows apace
The world has incense still to burn
Before a young and smiling face;
For all the splendid wheels that spin,
The fact is patent still and clear,
This planet sways to elder sin
That swathed in joy its youngest year—
New petrol cannot match old beer.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
Pierrot and Pierrette.
“Funiculi ! Funicula!” The lilting chorus
breaks and dies. .
Pierrot, he smiles. Pierrette, she sighs.
Beyond., the river waters lie
Gleaming beneath a starry sky;
And where the shore is swept with light,
The jesters flame their red and white.
All life is love and melody,
A gay-capped feast of revelry.
“Funiculi! Funicula!” A ripple of the
harpist’s strings
A voice that floats like silver wings
While o’er the dreamy, tropic night
Hot, yellow flowers breathe- delight.
Life’s but an hour of revelry ;
A puff-ball cup of melody.
Pierrot, he laughs; Pierrette, she lies —
The truth is written in her eyes.
QUEENSLAND.
A. GORE-JONES.
Love v. Death.
If I were dead,
I’d have no debts to pay;
No thoughts of “might” or may ;
No sorrow, no dismay.
The grass o’erspread
Upon my grave would be;
The wind would croon to me,
There for Eternity,
O’er my lone bed.
Yet, were I dead,
I’d lose the night’s soft skies;
The sweet breath of your sighs;
The love fires in your eyes,
With passion fed.
Life may be sad awhile •
We’ll make it reconcile
Love me, my love, and I’ll
Take Death as read.
N.s.w.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tin: Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to ver-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official alivays is.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 16, 1913.
THE LIBERAL “MACHINE” AND ITS
DEPLORABLE TENDENCY TO
TURN TORIES INTO ANAR-
CHISTS.
There is some ground for thinking that proposals
for a comprehensive scheme of national insurance
against sickness and unemployment will be a matter
of practical politics in the Federal arena before very
long. It is believed that it will be advocated as part
of the policy of the Liberal party if it should be re-
turned to power at the next general election.
—Great Liberal daily.
The repeal of the land tax is no part of the
Federal Liberal party’s policy. Apart from other
considerations such a step would be impracticable on
financial grounds.— lbid.
The Commonwealth Navy has come to stay, and the
sooner disgruntled critics realise that fact the better.
—lbid.
It is the ceaseless dropping of such intima-
tions that wears away the trusting Liberal’s
patience. “What is truth?” asked a prom-
inent anti-Socialist helplessly 1900 years ago.
His modern prototype has taken to inquiring,
with a similar air of stupefaction, “What is
anti-Socialism?” For years and years the
Liberal or Tory or Conservative or Reac-
tionary, or whatever he may be called or mis-
called, has been voting for candidates whom
he had every reason to regard as anti-Social-
ists. Also, he has been subscribing to their
campaign funds, orating on their behalf, en-
tertaining the electors at vast At Homes in
their interest, and writing more or less
incoherent letters in praise of their efforts —
all in the cause of Individualism and the
Empire. During this glad and hopeful
period, the persons in whose interests he was
spending his time and money and energy
used to tell him that Socialism was a raven-
ing tiger, and that it was their aim in life
—to the exclusion of fiscalism. Defence and
all else—to slay the brute utterly. Whereat
the soul of the Liberal grew joyous within
him, and he warmly asked the prospective
big-game destroyers what they would drink.
William McLinen, of N. S. Wales, one of
the most splendid patriots and fearless Tiger-
baiters of them all, was characteristically
strenuous in his advocacy of the drawing of
a clear-cut line betwen political vice and
virtue. In July, 1910, he said: —
I would suggest, as a beginning, that in Parlia-
ment, the term Labor should never be used for the
Socialist party ; also that in the announcement of
candidates at the forthcoming and other elections, our
leading journals should use the designation Socialist
as contradistinct from Liberal.
By such spirited pronouncements the anti-
Socialist elector was greatly heartened. He
took the people who were after his suffrages
very seriously. He believed in the menace of
the Tiger and its cubs, and told himself that,
with a bit of luck, the entire menagerie
would shortly be exterminated. And
what has his reward been? Actually he
is offered here and now the revolting soec-
tacle of his f 12-per-week wild-beast assailers
not merely sparing the Tiger, but offering it
raw meat and other sustaining food.
The rank Socialism which is bound up
with any “comprehensive scheme of national
insurance” becomes apparent when one con-
siders the experiences in this line of other
countries. Germany looks after its worker
from the first onset of sickness. By means_
of free medical attendance, sick pay, mater-
nity benefits for women, permanent invalidity
pensions, old-age, widows and orphans’ pen-
sions, sanatoria, etc., the Fatherland does
its level best to sap that independence of
spirit which anti-Socialism holds to be man’s
most priceless asset. The ailing Teuton
isn’t even worried by the reflection that he
will need to leave behind him sufficient cash
to pay for his burial expenses. A funeral
benefit system assures for him the prompt
and decent planting of his remains. In the
United Kingdom, under the Lloyd George
Act, the State helps to provide the working
classes with—
(l) Medical attendance in sickness to the end of
life.
(2) Sickness pay (from the fourth day) for 26
weeks.
(3) Maternity benefit for all insured women and
wives of insured men.
(4) Sanatorium benefit.
(5) Disablement pay until the age of 70.
(6) Old age pension at and after the age of 70.
It may be taken as read that Australia will
not lag behind either Germany or Britain in
the treatment of its invalid toilers. What-
ever scheme of invalidity insurance is adopted
here, it is certain that the employer and the
State will, between them, have to find a
goodly proportion of the money. To big
concerns like the Colonial Sugar Refining
Co. and the Melbourne Gas Co.—institutions
which have lavished all their faith, and,
unless rumor lies, a fair amount of hard cash
on Liberalism —the blow will be terrific.
And it will in no wise be lightened for them
by the consideration that it has been deliv-
ered by the party which has been posturing
for years as Socialism’s deadliest foe.
Nor do the wrongs which the genuine anti-
Socialist suffers at the hands of the bogus
anti-Socialists end here. A year or so ago
the Fisher Government’s Progressive Land
Tax was reckoned the ultimate howl in the
way of confiscation and daylight robbery.
To attempt to justify it was equivalent, in
well-to-do circles, to adulating Beelzebub
or excusing the practice of matricide. It was
Socialism, if not Anarchy. Suppose the Par-
liamentary Labor party identified a flagrant
and wicked piece of individualism, and made
its abolition one of their battle cries during
several years. Assume, for example, that
girl-sweating of a disgraceful sort was going
on —something even more disreputable than
that which prevails in the white-work busi-
ness at the present time. And assume that,
after making much political capital, and
earning thousands of votes by their alleged
furious hostility to this evil, the Laborites
suddenly abandoned the agitation, and, by
so doing, inferentially bestowed an official
benediction on the thing which they had for-
merly cursed as an intolerable wrong. What,
one wonders, would be the feelings of honest
anti-sweaters towards them! It is impos-
sible to say worse than that they would be
precisely the feelings of those unhappy
Tories who were deceived three years ago by
the glozing yarns of Liberal candidates on
the subject of their undying hostility to land
taxation, and who now find that the candi-
dates are liars and that they have no more
serious intention of wiping the Fisher Gov-
ernment’s much-cursed measure off the
Statute Book than they have of casting them-
selves from the top of the post office. It is
the same, without exception, in the case of
every other allegedly pestiferous act of “the
Socialists.” The ears of Emoire-leaguers
are still ringing, in a manner of speaking,
with the denunciations which the Cooks and
W. E. Johnsons of this afflicted ■ Common-
wealth hurled at the Australian navy pro-
posals. The allegations of these political
frauds were of no light order. They claimed
that an Australian squadron would not only
be tactically useless, but that it would (1)
fail to attract any sailors and (2) be used
ultimately to blow the British navy out of
the water." The Australian navy is here, to
all intents and purposes. More men have
offered than are required. Does Liberalism,
which used to claim that an effective local
fleet would constitute a menace to the Em-
pire’s chief line of defence, feature its aboli-
tion on the party platform? Not it! Liber-
alism is trying to forget that it ever howled
for the Dreadnought foolishness. It has
definitely adopted the “Mosquito Fleet”
ideal. In this, as in other principle-swallow-
ing feats, the Parliamentary imperialist and
anti-Socialist has been a thoroughly consist-
ent Liberal.
* * * *
Of course the Liberal politician does not
perform these unpleasant and discreditable
feats as a matter of pure enjoyment. He is
impelled thereto by the exigencies of the
party machine. To do him bare justice he
proceeds often enough to the place where he
is required to make a public meal of his own
lately-uttered opinions, with the same' com-
plete lack of enthusiasm which is noticeable
in the drunk who is having a frog’s-march
to a police-station. Withal he goes —for the
alternative consists in being ousted from the
party, and from the financial advantages
associated therewith. Parliamentary Lib-
eralism does not want to be Socialistic if it
can help it. Its trouble lies in the fact that
anti-Socialism is impracticable in a civilised
State. It is a good creed to blither about,
but an impossible one to practice. Educa-
tion and Socialism, like Gilbert’s pale young
curate and love, walk ever hand in hand.
The electors may vote for a candidate who
labels himself anti-Socialist, provided he con-
ducts himself rationally in other respects.
But if he does not help to provide them with
generous doses of Socialism, they mark their
approval of his consistency by passing him
out of public life. None know this better
than the party bosses. The aim of practi-
cally all of them is office at any price. But
office depends on outside support. The Lib-
eral leaders are not much concerned to cater
for the complete Tory. The rich they have
always had with them, and they consider
that this is a support which cannot be alien-
ated. Ruthlessly disregarding the enraged
countenance of Adipose, they accordingly
proceed to chase after the favor of the doubt-
ful quantity, i.e, the man in the bowyangs.
The Australian Industries Preservation Act,
the whole of the Commonwealth’s anti-Trust
legislation to date, Federal Old Age Pensions
and various other measures of the sort —all
of them introduced by the people who now
claim to be anti-Socialists—are manifesta-
tions of this tendency to collar votes or burst.
National Insurance is coming, the Land Tax
is to be left unassailed —if, indeed, it isn’t
made more stringent—and in a little
while we shall probably have anti-Socialists
advocating State hotels and haberdashery
shops, and threatening to nationalise the
coastal shipping trade.
A pathetic figure in this maelstrom of
false pretences, and mendacity, and sreed
and broken pledges is the forthright Tory,
who believes in his demented doctrine and
sees no reason why his representatives
shouldn’t carry it out. There is nothing of
the bogus anti-Socialist about this choleric
old stalwart. He would welcome the repeal
of every progressive Act of the last 25 years.
It is his tragedy that none of the politicians
are disposed to hold the bridge of reaction
with him. To all intents and purposes he is
unrepresented in Parliament. For all the
good the place is to him he might as well be
disfranchised. The depressing, and, at a
casual glance, anomalous result, is that the
true-blue Conservative is developing into the
nearest apnroach to an anarchist that the
more enlightened countries possess. During
the last Referendum campaign Bruce
Smith, M.P.; Lee, M.L.A., of N. S. Wales;
and others publicly mentioned revolution and
bloodshed if the proposed amendments were
carried. Bonar Law, the British Tory leader,
is voicing similar sentiments in England at
the present time in anticipation of Home
Rule becoming an accomplished fact. A
number of affluent company directors starkly
defied the law the other day in N. S. Wales,
and were fined for it. Everywhere anti-
Socialism is to be observed seething with the
spirit of rebellion. The development is de-
plorable, but inevitable. There is no place
for the genuine Tory in the world as it is
constituted to-day. He belongs, by rights,
to the Feudal period, or thereabouts. He
has no prospect of establishing the justice of
his cause by weight of logic. The dynamite
cap is about the only argument left to him,
and the hand-grenade is one of his few re-
maining hopes. When Australia reads one
morning that numbers of stout, elderly gen-
tlemen have been seen crawling furtively
about after the fashion of malefactors, with
bombs and other lethal articles in their
hands, it will realise that the worst has oc-
curred, and that anti-Socialism has at last
lost the only genuinely anti-Socialist sup-
port it ever had.
THE NAMES THAT TAKE THE PLACE
OF THOUGHT AND WORK.
It is characteristic of primitive peoples that
they lay great stress upon names and sym-
bols. It is characteristic of all peoples that
they cling to the names and symbols of
earlier days long after those names and
symbols have ceased to have either meaning
or use. Barbaric crests and emblematic
coats-of-arms are still in many lands the out-
ward tokens of a superiority of race and
caste, and a College of Heralds survives and
does large business in an age of wireless
telegraphy, aeroplanes and Beef Trusts. The
bishop still wears an apron to show the
arduous nature of his toil, wives wear the
chain-link of the old marital slavery on their
left hands, men are still called dukes when
every semblance of the leadership which
their name implies is gone, and the grip of
the right hand remains as the token of
friendship when there is nojonger any need
to hold that hand away fron| the treacherous
dagger hilt. The name will survive
all kingdoms, the name (f assar will out-
live all empires, and John Bull’s patrony-
mic will still be associated with roast beef
when he hasn’t a baked sparrow of his own
that he can eat. In the realm of politics
names and party cries continue to sway the
minds of citizens long after they have ceased
to stand for the things that once gave them
force and honor. While men fly in terror
from the name which is still outside the
bounds of respectability, they cling with
fatuous devotion to the older name which
has lost all the claim it ever possessed to be
regarded as respectable or even decent. The
mediaeval reverence for the name and title
of a king is not more remarkable than the
political prostration before the party word
which stood for something in the days of
our grandfathers, but which has now lost
all its better significance. This disease
of name-worshin is endemic in all countries,
and Australia, for all its larger opportunities
and possibilities of wider outlook, is just as
much afflicted as older lands. Here as else-
where the word retains its power when all
that gave it life and meaning for thinking
men has passed out of it, leaving it as empty
as the pub that has lost its license.
The explanation of all this is to be found
readily enough. Every word which finds its
way into a language represents the accumu-
lation and crystallisation of a vast amount
of thought. The syllables themselves may
fall together almost by chance, or be coined
by a writer in a careless moment, but the
word to have place and use must have had
the way prepared for it by a great quantity
of brain effort, often spread over years or
even centuries. Thus the term Wowser,
which has, only recently been incorporated
in the Australian tongue, represents a long
process of thought on the part of thousands
of people about certain problems of the day
and the attitude of narrow and unsympa-
thetic minds towards them. It does not
matter much who invented the word—which
was, after all, no very remarkable achieve-
ment —but the fact does matter that a long
process of reasoning had led to the demand
for a word which should express the easy
and tolerant contempt of the public for the
new bigotry in social matters which had
established its pernicious growth among us.
And all this is true also of political terms
and party names, only excepting the fact
that such terms and names are more liable
to change in meaning and force than any
others which become regularly incorporated
into language. In the first place, then,
thought, and lots of it, went to the making
of every one of these labels. They stood for dis-
tinct and often very complicated ideas which
had been slowly evolved. But having once
got these labels at some cost, the greater
number of the world’s citizens are content
to go on using them in order tp avoid
further thought and effort. It is much
easier to read the daily papers, go in the
evening to a theatre or picture-show and
talk to a friend in the tram or nub about
the Balkan war or the latest fight at the
Stadium than to do a little serious and in-
dependent thinking about the real meaning
of things which are so easily packed up in
unopened parcels and labelled with handy
names and words.
* * * *
As illustrating the force of names in poli-
tics the election for the Blayney seat
(N.S.W.) furnishes a striking object lesson.
One of the three candidates went to the poll
as a Liberal. It is a good old name sugges-
tive of big things, for there were once big
Liberals, and in some countries the name
still stands for honesty of purpose and a
battle for vast reforms. But even in the
land where the word came to birth its mean-
ing has been a very changeable one. Eng-
lish election crowds waiting for Liberal
speakers to address them on questions of the
day still sing an old election song to a sort
of hymn tune :
We’ll raise again the Liberal flag
Of Gladstone, Cobden. Bright,
And peace, retrenchment and reform
Shall be our cry to-night.
It isn’t much of a song, and it isn’t true.
The Liberalism of Gladstone wasn’t the
Liberalism of Cobden to any extent, and the
Liberalism of neither had even a decent
superficial resemblance to that of Lloyd
George. Still, the song goes with a swing
and Liberalism is a good catchword. What
more should a crowd want? But the Lib-
eralism of N. S. Wales is not even in any
sort of succession from the thing that wore
the label in the past, or in any sort of sym-
pathy with the thing which wears it even
now in England and Canada. The present
Liberals of Australia have no sort of affi-
nity to the Gladstones, Greys and King-
stons who once were proud of the title. It
is now simply a serviceable name for a party
containing the dregs and leavings of all
parties which have been united by force of
circumstances into an organisation whose
purpose is to get the loaves and fishes of
office and oppose all reform and bolster up
by every possible means the interests of the
fat man against those of the lean. Older
Liberals, if they troubled at all about the
matter, might reasonably rise from their
graves to hurl charnel clods of indignation
at the usurpers of their honored title; but
the word answers its purpose, and any num-
ber of electors at Blayney and elsewhere are
prepared to take it quite seriously.
* * * *
But the name Liberal is not singular in
that it is the only term likely to deceive
citizens at Blayney. Beeby stands as an
Independent, and the word by which he de-
scribes himself is apt to conjure up in the
public mind a vision of the strong and sturdy
democrat who, for the truth that is in him.
refuses to be bound by the conventions of
party, and, aiming at great reforms, insists
on following conviction and conscience at
all costs and in the face of all opposition.
But Beeby is not an Independent of that
sort, for at the very outset of his campaign
he made it clear that it was his intention to
form a third party. As the very terms In-
dependent and party are contradictory,
Beeby’s declaration made it plain that he
wasn’t the thing that he pretended to be,
and evers other word he has spoken has
made it equally clear that his desertion of
existing parties was due to nothing large
and free in his political outlook, but merely
to his desire to form part and parcel of
some organisation of narrower outlook and
smaller aim than the thing from which he
had retired or been driven. Still, the word
Independent has an attractive sound, and as
a vote catcher is at least as good a label as
that worn by either of his opponents. The
third candidate, who is now out of the race,
was branded Labor. Here again the term
was wholly misleading, for Johnston stood
as a supporter of the McGowen-llolman
Ministry, which, while wearing the Labor
name, has treated its party pledges as a jest,
and shown a spirit which bore no faint re-
semblance to that of the big organisation
which placed it in power. John Smith, of
Blayney, might well be pardoned if he hesi-
tated before giving his support to a Minis-
try which had placed the interests of a State
above those of Australia, and had acted in
a spirit of bitter hostility to the Common-
wealth whenever the opportunity arose.
Johnston might be the best of Australians,
but Johnston’s return could only have the
effect of strengthening the hands of
McGowen’s hopeless Ministry, which
neither represents Australian sentiment nor
supports Labor aims. Still, the name Labor
had its influence on votes, and served all the
purpose that the best label could serve even
if it were a true description of the goods
labelled.
* * * *
Blayney election is an unfortunate busi-
ness—for the electors. With an alleged Lib-
eral, Independent and Laborite to choose
from, they were still practically disfran-


chised. ihe alleged liberal was merely a
Tory of a very inferior brand ; the so-called
Independent a party man of the smallest
kind who thought he might win an advan-
tage in a re-shuffle of the political cards;
and the person selected to represent Labor
was compelled to present himself as the sup-
porter of a Government which has done
more to bring discredit on the Labor cause
than all the crooked works of the opponents
of that movement. It was a pretty choice,
and what the result of the second ballot may
be cannot matter the quiver of a lizard’s
tail to any Australian. But the political
names are good and high-sounding, and even
beautiful, and in our much prized system of
party politics, which cheats John Smith
to his own apparent satisfaction, the name
counts for a good deal more than anything
else. Possibly a time may come when John
Smith will get tired of being cheated and
insist upon his politicians having one name
with only one meaning. When the only
political party is called the Australian,
things will begin to move forward.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Dropping the Passenger.
Alfred Deakin practically severed his con-
nection with the alleged Liberal party of
Australia last week. He has resigned the
leadership of the Opposition, and after the
Referendum campaign he proposes to go
into retirement for good. It must be a
blessed relief to him, and it is certainly an
even greater one to the reactionaries into
whose midst he was driven four years ago
by one of Party Government’s queer necessi-
tous shifts. Melbourne Argus voiced the
sentiments of Toryism all over Australia
when it remarked (9/1/T3) :
The retirement which Mr. Deakin announced yes-
terday was too long delayed by a year at least. The
circumstances which impel him to relinquish his post
now were equally existent then. His leadership of the
Liberal party during the session of 1912 left much —
almost everything—to be desired.
To straight-out Conservatism Alfred
Deakin was anathema from the jump. The
ordinary man, realising this, would have re-
garded the position as hopeless and unten-
able and retired long ago. But Deakin has
the gift for which W. E. Gladstone was
famous, and of which A. J. Balfour is one
of the most distinguished living exponents,
of being able to talk himself into almost any
given point of view. Yet the Fusion com-
pact or swindle put too great a strain even
on his accommodating intellect. He was a
convinced high-tariffist. As the price of
association with ,T. Cook. Bruce Smith
and Co. he had to sit inert, and watch the
manufactories of the Commonwealth lan-
guish, and the workers of Australia go job-
less, while the sweated goods of Europe and
Asia poured through our defective tariff
fence. He knew the fence needed a general
overhaul. But by the terms of his bargain
with the importers’ representatives he was
precluded from so much as suggesting that
it required a fresh paling here and there.
The mental strain of convincing himself
that this was the better fiscal part must
have been terrific. He was an instinctive
democrat, a man with the imagination to
vision the joyless lot of the majority of the
workers even in this favored country, and
the heart to wish to better their condition.
The Trade Marks Act (1905), the Excise
Tariff Act (1906), and the Australian In-
dustries Preservation Act (1907) were all
his measures. The Trade Marks Act —a
scheme to compel the registering of a wor-
ker’s mark on all goods in order that the
consumer might know whether they had
been manufactured by unionists or non-
unionists —was denounced by his present
allies as an outrage on British freedom. Ilis
New Protection scheme and his anti-Trust
legislation were greeted with the heartiest
curses of Anti-Socialism. And. naturally so.
Anti-Socialism would have had even less
right to the name than it has, had it sat
down peacefully under these revolutionary
proposals. By the irony of Fate the sole
aim of the Fisher Government’s Referen-
dum campaign is to effectuate the very legis-
lation for which a Deakin Government was
originally responsible. There is nothing
more trying, not to say heart-breaking, for
a politician than to have to find convincing
arguments against the validating of his own
Acts. And that is the Herculean job with
which Deakin has been faced for some time
now. Of course he has been a failure at it.
For years he has been a mere passenger
aboard the “Liberal” ship. And now, de-
spite all the civil periphrases about ill-
health and the anguish of Messrs Cook and
Millen at his departure, the crew have
dropped him overboard. It has been done
gently, and a comfortable spot has been
selected. Had he waited longer it would
have been a question of. his colleagues
marooning him on a desert island.

A nation has a right to the use of all its

A Trade Which Has Become Too Safe.
Last week a Alelbourne citizen made the
ratal error of going to bed with a couple of
Hundred pounds or,so on the premises. In
due course the customary masked robbers
arrived, lhey differed from those who have
been operating with such success in Sydney
and Adelaide in little but the dreadfulness
ot their language. Amidst a flood of hair-
raising oaths one of them shot the house-
holder dead, after which they left with the
money. It is just possible that these particu-
lar criminals may be apprehended ; the lurid-
ness of their speech was so much over the
odds as to afford a useful clue to their
identity. But so many of the malefactors
are not arrested that the law-abiding section
ot the public is feeling simply rotten. Here
is a civilised country, with costly courts,
gaols, penitentiaries, lock-ups, etc., a fair-
sized police force and a big army of detec-
tives in every State, and yet, seemingly, any
crude scoundrel possessed of sufficient cash
or credit to acquire a loaded revolver and a
mask is able with comparative safety to
burst into what house he pleases and rob
and slaughter the inhabitants. When the
dazed sociologist has done wondering why
this noxious trade should have become so
popular—whether it is due to the immigra-
tion policy or something else —he might pro-
fitably consider why the practitioners are so
rarely caught. For the most part they have
been not merely daring, but heroic in 'taking
risks. In Sydney, the other day, a band of
them stole a launch, steamed to the front
door, in a manner of speaking, comman-
deered all they could lay their hands on, and
afterwards cruised comfortably away into
oblivion. Others have walked leisurely up
the garden path, adjusting their masks
doubtless as they went, barged in through
the handiest aperture, and so home again,
richer by every portable article in sight.
The police, whether in Sydney, Melbourne
or Adelaide, have visited the place where
the valuables used to be, and after assuming
a siSindalised and at the same time knowing
air, have gone away, and, in most cases,
achieved nothing. It is quite possible that
they are incompetent. The pay of the
policeman is still so small all over the Com-
monwealth that intellectuals avoid the
Force; in fact, it is not rushed by persons
of even moderate intelligence. And, com-
petent or otherwise, are there enough of
them? It was stated a month or so ago
that in one North Sydney suburb of over
10,000 inhabitants there was but a solitary
constable. Many of the Melbourne suburbs
are grievously under-Dunned, and the same
criticism applies to Adelaide. The com-
bined futility and fewness of the metropoli-
tan police throughout Australia must have
a considerable bearing on the popularity of
burglary-CMm-murder as a calling. The ob-
vious thing to do is to set this part of the
national house in order, without delay. Bet-
ter-brained p.olice and more of them will cost
the States money, but the loss of such citi-
zens as he who was slaughtered last week
costs them infinitely more.
A Word Against the Boycotting of
Patriots.
Pending the appointment of a High Court
Judge to fill the place of the late R. E.
O’Connor, Liberalism is yapping strenuously
for what it calls a “non-political lawyer.”
So far as one can gather, any old lawyer
will suffice for this signally important billet,
provided always he has had no previous
association with statecraft. The inward-
ness of this demand becomes apparent wliefi
one remembers that many of the candidates
with Parliamentary experience are Demo-
crats, while practically every prominent bar-
rister who has confined himself to the prac-
tice of his profession is a hide-bound Tory.
There is, of course, an occasional exception
to this rule, but he is almost as rare as a
vegetarian shark. The Sydney Telegraph
put the “Liberal” case as follows: —
There are reasons which render the political
judge even more conspicuously out of place in the
High Court than he would be as a member of the
State judiciary. There is no need to go to militant
politics, in or out of Parliament, for Federal judges.
Other things equal, the less prominently a man has
figured in political strife the better his qualifications
for any tribunal, etc., etc.
Now, if there is one direction in which the
United Kingdom shines more refulgently than
another, it is in the quality of its judges.
And this is how the Lord Chancellor spoke
of their appointment, according to London
Times, at the last Guildhall Banquet:—
The office of Lord Chancellor was one that was
very puzzling to foreigners. The genius of British
law was very remarkable. Its sole purpose was to
see justice done according to the standard of recti-
tude which prevailed in a country like theirs, and to
that end they insisted that their Judges should he
trained not merely as lawyers or jurists, but as public
men, knowing something of public affairs.
I declare that if I were to be tried for a high
political offence I should wish to choose for those
who judged me twelve good men and true taken from
the Opposition side of the House of Commons. /Ap-
plause and laughter,.) You cannot get that training
in public affairs in any other place more perfectly.
Thus it comes.about that the head of the law the
•Judge to whom*is assigned the duty of superintending
not only in the supreme; tribunal of this country,
but of the Empire— is expected by the public to
have come through a career as a public man which
involves his spending a large part of his time m the
House of Commons-
It is this kind of thing which makes our country
unintelligible abroad,, but which enables us to com-
pass things which are not easily compassed elsewhere
wfget a wider outlook by that kind of training and
we get advantages which cannot be got bv nations
who g separate bad judges as a profession b -V them-
selves That is why our judges have always had the
closest relation to pubi c life
England has hardly had one really great
judge who was.a non-political. And the
same remark applies to America. Had local
Liberalism’s present aspirations been nut
into force from the beginning. Australia
would have lost Griffith, its greatest judge
to date and N. S. Wales would never have
had tS benefit of Martin's services. The
demented experiment has, fortunately, never
been tried on anything resembling an ex-
tended scale, and it is to be h °P« d tbqf ]t
never will. If there is one type that for a
multitude of reasons, is foredoomed to failure
“n the High Court Bench, it is the citizen
who has never considered his country so
well worth serving as his banking account.
Australia’s Aged and Decrepit Artillery.
“Don Q.”: The Bulletin’s remarks
(26/2/T2) about the deplorable arms of
the Turk apply equally to those of the Aus-
tralian. When the war-correspondent
writes up the invasion of the North, in
19 —, this is probably how he’ll commence :
The utter defeat of the Australians was due to the
overwhelmingly superior artillery of the invaders. It
wasn’t a fight, this gallant death of an army, it
was a mere slaughter. With obsolete artillery and
indifferent rifles, fashioned after the British pattern,
the Australian simply had no chance.
Although we have a few moderately efficient
18-pounder field guns, the bulk of the can-
non is 20 years and more old. In Queens-
land, where I am just now, the defence
ironmongery consists of three batteries (in-
complete) and a few ancient Maxims. The
field guns are principally of the old 15-
pounder type, and, if I’m not greatly mis-
taken, several of them are old muzzle-
loaders, which were converted into breech-
loaders years ago. Then we have one bat-
tery of 18-pounders—fairly good weapons,
fitted with the pneumatic recoil buffers.
Yet, when this gun is compared with, say,
the modern French field gun, it is a sad and
disturbing spectacle. The extreme range of
the 14.3 pounder used by the Bulgarian is
6500 yards (considerably greater than that
of our 18-pounder), while the rapidity of
fire possible with the French gun is more
than double that of the British-backed
weapon. Under pressure, 30 to 35 rounds
per minute can be fired from the French
implement, and I’ll defy Australia’s crack ar-
tillery squad to get 15 rounds per minute
(service conditions) out of our best gun. To
watch our Defence Department procrastin-
ating, you’d thing improvement was impos-
sible. Hearken to this. Bulgaria, with a
population only about equal to Australia’s,
completed the re-arming of its artillery one
year before hostilities broke out in the
Balkans, and to-day can place in the field an
artillery establishment slightly in excess of
Great Britain's, and of much more deadly
use. For all practical purposes there are
now but two forces in modern land warfare
—infantry and artillery. All other branches
are but sundries, so to speak. One remark-
able feature about the recent Balkan battles
was the close support of infantry with ar-
tillery, mostly mountain batteries. It was
rather a shock to the old school to find field
guns practically in the firing line with the
infantry. As for the Turks, they were
knocked endways by this kind of in-fighting.
The Bulgarians got the idea from the annual
manoeuvres of the German and French
armies prior to the war. Australia should
send some of its brighter young officers to
Europe to learn the science of war. They
should be in the Balkans at the present
time, along with the military repre-
sentatives of other nations. Meanwhile,
we might indent, say. five batteries of
the best French field guns, a parcel
of Vicar-Maxim’s latest machine-guns, and
a case of the world’s best military magazine
rifles. I believe in every nation making
its own guns, but if there’s going to be
slavish copying of another fellow’s type of
gun. let it be the best type. Of course, this
would be opposed to all the traditions of the
British War Office, and would naturally lead
piffling little tin-tack merchants and calico
jimmies in Australia to lay a charge of dis-
loyalty against Defence Minister
But that’s nothing to the charge that will be
laid against Australia unless something ol
the kind is done
Australia as she Could Be.
“Telemachus” on “Australia as she is”
(B. 2/1/’l3) inspires “Aquarius”:
Australia is the most arid of the continents Yet it
contains little desert. Arid countries are always rich
countries when watered. The. fertility of ar lf p„.
is due to a quality inherent in aridity itself. Pro-
fessor Hilgard found, as the result of more tha
1000 analvses of soils, that the soils of the and Ye
(United States) contain on an average, three
times as milch potash, six times as much
magnesia and 14 times as much lime as those
of g the East. The high percentage of fertilising
elements explains the superior productiveness of
semi-arid and arid territories. Auguste de Gasparin,
in discussing the great advantages of the use of water
in a warm climate, makes the following terse state-
ment: “Two units of humidity, multiplied by two of
heat gives four ; but four of heat by tour of humidity
gives 16. Such is, in fact, the rate of progression in
which are manifested the advantages produced by the
Association of these two essentials of vegetable
Kr Two-thirds of Australia is arid. It is a land of
few rivers, though it possesses the
known underground reservoir. But the value of the
artesian and sub-artesian supplies is very fairly recog-
nised and the underground reservoirs may now, there-
fore ’be left to take care of themselves The impera-
tive question of the day is the interception and utili-
sation of the surface waters, so that not one drop of
those which fall within the Murray basin, to start
with, mav be lost in the Southern Ocean.
The question of the water supply for a continent two-
thirds arid may be best approached by discussing the
potentialities of its largest streams. They are to.be
found in that, area known as the Murray basin, an
area larger than France and Germany combined with
aAnnnUHon of no more than a few hundred thou-
sands 3 as against a combined population of over 100
millions in the European area. The interior of Aus-
tralia cannot he attached to civilisation until this
territory is made water-famine proof, and thus turned
into a base for attacking the country further north
an The° r Murray basin is equal to one-seventh of the
total area of Australia, and no large portion of it is
unsuitable for the wants of man and beast. It in-
cludes the Murray, and the Murray s tributaries and
tributaries’ tributaries. It occupies one-sixth of N. S.
Wales an area of Queensland the size of Victoria,
one-half of Victoria and the south-eastern portion of
South Aus. The development of the potentialities of
this area is a national proposition. The water supply
of Australia means national life. Its neglect means
1903, after seven years drought, Australia was a
sand-blown wilderness, with half of its live stock
starved to death and its great plains white with
bleached bones. Man’s improvidence ! Man s cruelty .
T nek the Murray from its mouth to Echuca, the
DarlTn- toVaAgett, the Murrumbidgee to Hay. Thus
the billabongs and. the lakes would become permanent
reservoirs The 70 lakes along the Darling are natural
storages Cawndilla holds, when full, 17,196 millions
of cubic feet; Lake Menindie 16,700 million cubic
feet The flow from the latter, after it has been
filled bv flood waters, maintains navigation in the
Lower Darling for many months after it has ceased
higher up. Construct a weir at Talmalino, near
Albury, to hold up 11,000 million cubic feet of
water'; a weir at Bungowannah, with a mam offtake
channel to the Goulburn River weir, and thence to
flow to Waranga basin, and onward from there to
Brim to supply water for the southern Mallee, in
Victoria; construct another offtake from Bnngow-
annah to irrigate the country between the offtake and
the Edwards River, north of Deniliquin with branch
channels to Jerilderie, Conargo, Mnlwala and Denili-
nuin and thus command two million acres of the
finest land in the world. The Burrinjuck reservoir
and the weir at Narrandera, with the main offtakes
and branches, are already well under way, and under
careful management will be an invaluable object
lesson. At the head waters of the Lachlan River
construct a weir, and another at the head waters of
the Macquarie River, near Bathurst. Go on weiring
until the work of conserving every drop of the rain-
fall is done. That is the way to reclaim one-seventh
of Australia from water famine, and there is no other
way. By that method 31 millions of acres in the
Wagga district, of reddish loam, can be irrigated,
and nearly 10 million acres in the Southern Riverina
can be turned into lucerne paddocks to provide fodder
for the silo.
When all this is done the base will be there. Then
tackle the Cooper Creek basin, and solve the mysteries
associated with the Diamantina and the Barcoo and
Lake Eyre and its once glorious gardens and forests,
where roamed the mammoth mammalia, and where
lofty eucalvpts rose through the gloom of dense under-
growth to be the dwelling-places of the Kadimakara,
who feared the terrors of the earth.
Art, Nudity and Some Notions.
The wisdom of “F. J. 8.” :
“S.T.S.”: (B. 21/1/T3) says that George Lam-
bert’s 1000 guineas, collected from a Russian prince
for “The Man with the Mask,” were earned primarily
by the audacious handling of the nudes in the pic-
ture. Doesn’t that say everything? Art is getting
feeble and groggy on its legs, because our artists do
not know how anything looks without its stays. If
an artist’s only model is his wife, so much the worse
for him ; it is like a “star” always permitting his
jealous wife (invariably a third-rate actress) to play
all the leading lady rSles. There are, of course,
exceptions. Millet’s wife must have been one such.
I like that anecdote about Madame and her artist
husband. Millet compelled his wife (he could
afford no other model) to wear the same shirt for an
uncomfortably long time —not to paint the dirt, as
his early critics would have the world believe, but
that the rough linen should simplify its folds and
take the form of the body, that he might give a
fresher and stronger accent to those qualities he so
loved, the garment becoming, as it were, a part of
the body, and expressing, as he said, even more than
the nude, the larger and simpler forms of Nature.
Millet was right, but the nude is better. Accord-
ing to Rodin’s recently-published opinions on art,
the artist cannot have enough of direct impressions
from living, nude bodies. In his studio he has
always some half-dozen or more nude models walking
about, and when he notes a pose which appeals to
the artist in him, he arrests it, and models like a
fury. In default of other, the Boxing Stadium is a
capital studio. What finer sight on earth than two
living, throbbing organisms like Midwood and Bal-
zac in strenuous muscular antagonism! I was pleased
to see Australia’s greatest artist in line studying the
nude at the Stadium the other night. Folks may look
at pictures till the cows hop out of the canvas, but
that won’t make them artistic. The art instinct is
to be got only by making art a national matter.
Pictures, statues, buildings, fountains, gardens—open,
free, everywhere. Surround the nation with works
of the best art, and they will become art critics in
time, almost, but not quite, as good as an ordinary
French chiffonier, for there is something in the
French nature which makes for art, and there is
something in the British nature which doesn’t. Per-
hans it is our pre-mid-Victorianism. Anyhow, we’ve
had it quite a while. And never do what you don’t want
to do simply because it is supposed to make for
culture. Just look at our Town Hall fashionable
symphony concerts. If Dore were in want of faces
to put into his studies of lost souls in Dante’s
“Inferno,” where could he find more useful models?
Thev are listening, say. to Bach’s Symphony in G
Maior, and thev sit like a party in a parlor, all
silent and all damned. If vou could only see their
little personalities in the nude, you would never need
to visit the Chamber of Horrors in a waxworks exhibi-
tion.
The North Melbourne gravitation goods-
yard trouble has got the Vic. railway heads
down, and at present they can scarcely speak
civilly to anyone who mentions the matter.
The bother began when the Department de-
cided to plant its sorting-yard and the neces-
sary embankment on the filled-in bed of the
Moonee Ponds Creek, which is merely allu-
vial slush, and very soft slush at that.. As
fast as clay and gravel were carted thither
and tipped on the spot where the embank-
ment was wanted, it sank into the ooze ; but
the Department thought that if it kept, on
dumping enough it must some day get a little
bit of solid ground. But it miscalculated.
There is no certainty that if it humped Kos-
ciusko down by the drayload it would get
what it wants, because as fast as the soil is
tipped out the ooze disperses and makes room
for more carted stuff to spread out. Already
the banks of the Moonee Creek have been
affected, and that occasionally turbulent riv-
ulet may at any time spread over the sur-
rounding country. Near Dynon-road an
acre of what was lately a swamp has been
raised nearly 12ft. This is all right so far.
but there is no guarantee that things will
stav as they are; the patch may rise to a
peak or it may slop all over the place, dam
back the Moonee Creek and the adjacent
Saltwater River, turn Flemington racecourse
into a lake, and send Footscray under the
bay. The Department has of late been hump-
ing mullock from the Caulfield duplication
works —which is a long way to carry dirt —
and it has several more supplies to fall back
on ; but it has already spent about £150,000
on the job, and has got so little to show for it
that it is making “other arrangements.” It is
now spending £90,000 on similar gravitation
yards further up the Northern line, at Tot-
tenham, to “relieve the pressure” on the
North Melbourne yards —when it gets them.
Tottenham is mostly blue metal, so there is
little fear of subsidence there, whatever else
may happen.

When the great trouble comes Australia
and Maoriland will have to pull together or
hang together, and the sooner they co-ordin-
ate their arrangements the better. But they
won’t do it so long as they don’t know one
another better and take a more neighborly
interest in each other’s happenings. The
default is mainly on the Australian side.
The Maorilander who. comes to the Common-
wealth for a change pr on business, kicks
like a regiment of mules at the lack of news
from God’s Own Country in the dailies.
Tom Mills —the M.L. pressman who is
president of the Australasian Country Press
Association —told the latter body the other
dav. in Sydney, that Australian country
newspaper men didn’t know “the first. thing
about news gathering and the distribution
thereof; and he heaved a verbal brick at the
metropolitan press concerning its lack of
neighborly and news interest in M.L. A.
S. Biss, a Wellington business man, is a
still later kicker. He has just got back home
from Sydney, and has unloaded his mind to
a reporter about the news the papers there
do not print. Moreover, he makes charges
which no self-respecting daily paper should
take lying down. Says lie :
I am given to understand that a good deal of news
is forwarded, but a good deal of it is never printed.
As a matter of fact, I am inclined to think that they
do not want to give Maoriland any publicity. What
is always published, however, are the reports about
there being heavy weather on the coast.
The M.L. Press Association gathers in half
a column of Australian news daily for its
papers; and there won’t be much love lost
for Australia on the part of the Maorilander
until the Commonwealth papers reciprocate.

A business man said the other day that
when his business gets the best of him
he puts on his hat and slips away to a

<he best relaxation he knows of.
Our theatres are all in the business district.

COES
A Case Where Silence isn’t Golden.
Thebe is a type of Australian who seethes
with indignation when references are made
to the menace of oversea nations —particu-
larly if the nations are yellow or gamboge.
They would have the Commonwealth regard
the foreigner as a beneficent being who
spends all his waking hours manufacturing
cheap articles for Australians to import,
with never a thought, so long as he is left
unmolested, of playing the barbarian’s sorry
rdle. The cheerless fact is, the alien, and
particularly the colored alien, is far from
being the well-disposed and harmless charac-
ter that the typical importer pictures him.
Frequently he is shifty and unscrupulous,
and his eye is directed to the main chance
with tireless assiduity. Take the follow-
ing, for examnle, from the Yorodztj, a Tokio
journal, which has lately been urging the
advisability of Japan throwing over its asso-
ciation with Great Britain, and concluding
an alliance with Germany :
We Japanese do not hesitate to declare that Japan’s
interests lie in the partition of Great Britain and
her colonies. By the establishment of an alliance
between Germany and Japan the latter may take
possession of Australia, while the former may es-
tablish a protectorate over the various countries
and islands in the South Pacific. Russia will in
future make a descent upon Persia, and it is a fore-
gone conclusion that Germany will absorb the
Netherlands whenever a unique opportunitv presents
itself. Having enemies on all sides, Great Britain,
which has no intention to fight, will thus be placed
m a helpless condition. Certain it is that in that
event the Transvaal would declare for independence,
Spain would take Gibraltar, the United States annex
Canada, while France may once again become the
protector of Egypt. If things come to such a pas*,
the two-Power standard navy of Great Britain will
naturally become a useless weapon.
The indent-agent variety of patriot would
argue that such disclosures of opinion should
be civilly disregarded by the press of Aus-
tralia. In his opinion the people ought not
to be told that there is a powerful nation,
adjacent to their homes, whereof a section—
and a fairly cultivated and extensive section
at that—is in favor of disregarding its most
sacred treaty obligations at the slightest
prospect of material gain. The Bulletin
disagrees with .the indent-agent patriot. In
its opinion there is little point in sparing the
feelings of the foreigner at the expense of
the badly-needejd enlightenment of one’s own
fellow-countryman. That way lies the very
calamity which has caused us to be the in-
adequately defended people we are. For
years the “right-thinking” classes have been
repeating blithely that Australia has nothing
to fear from the invader, since Britain’s
navy is impregnable, and since, anyhow, the
foreigner is far too kindly and conscientious
to think of doing this country any damage.
Up till quite recently scores of these people
actually argued that Australia wasn’t worth
taking, and therefore not worth defending.
That delusion has been dispelled to some ex-
tent. Our vast heritage is no longer guarded
by means of an upraised umbrella, so to
sneak, but with a modest-sized meat-chopper.
We have an army that will be formidable
when it grows up, and a navy that should be
effective when it materialises. But there
are people who don’t want the army to grow
up or the navy to materialise. Therefore we
thank the Yorodzu for putting the matter
so plainly. Nothing more should be needed
to satisfy the Commonwealth that it hasn’t
started its Defence preparations a minute
too soon, and that it will be mad if it eases
un in them for a moment, and almost equ-
ally demented if it doesn’t rather push for-
ward with its preparations to the utmost of
its financial capacity.
It is cabled that the Indian Government
has suspended opium sales owing to the
accumulation of stocks in China; also that
the Chinese Republic shows signs of want-
ihg to repudiate the “opium treaty” with
England, and that the British Minister at
Pekin has warned the person with the lately-
docked pig-tail that if he doesn’t mind what
he is about the thing will have to be reported
in London. The whole business is the dead
limit of national infamy. The Indian opium
traffic was once the monopoly of the Great
Mogul, who was just the sort of person to
run an injurious trade of the kind. Clive
won it for the East India Co. at the battle
of Plassey, and the fat co. worked it for all
it was worth. Chinese Governments, recog-
nising the drug as bad for their subjects,
from time to time prohibited its use and im-
portation, and Anglo-Indian Fat had to con-
duct a contraband trade for about half a
century. Then the Chinese authorities got
a*gry and sent a person called Lin to de-
stroy as much of the stuff as he could find.
Lin did the job with Chinese thoroughness
and made an end of about £2,000,000 worth.
Still the British ships smuggled the iniquity
in, aDd the injuries to smugglers caught red-
handed caused Britain to go to war. As
China had neglected to keep any sort of De-
fence force to uphold its objection to the
destructive evil, it had to sit down licked
and buy all the opium India liked to send
along. Naturally, also, the Chinese began
to grow the stuff themselves, and all the
efforts of Chinese statesmen to suppress the
bad habits of their countrymen were of no
use in face of the British determination to
collect the cash which the bad habits were
worth to India. This sort of thing went on
until the Americans got the Philippines and
joined with the Chow authorities to suppress
the vice. At last in 1907 Britain made an
offer to reduce the export from India by
gradual withdrawals from the market each
year until 1910, provided China didn’t raise
a supply of the stuff or get it anywhere else.
China agreed, and the market price of
opium rose so much that India was draw-
ing almost as much revenue from the re-
duced output as it had drawn in former
times from the contraband trade. Now
China begins to think that the British agree-
ment is a little too good for the foreigner—
and England once more protests. But the
twentieth century is alleged to have arrived,
and the new China knows that Britain will
not, and dare not, enforce with cannon the
demand that the Chinese people shall destroy
themselves with a noxious drug to swell the
Indian revenues.

Faust is still behaving in a thoroughly
ungentlemanly way at Sydney Theatre
Royal, and, thank Heavens, meeting with
the due punishment of eternal damnation.
Now that the clergy are being allowed into
so many of our theatres on the
object being to get from these pious dead-
heads an endorsement of the high moral
purpose of the show—the writer is
emboldened to weigh in with his little
psean of holy praise of “Faust.” After
witnessing it he resolved definitely and
finally not to sell his soul to the Devil. One
of the conditions of any bargain of the sort
is, plainly, that Beelzebub shall attend the
vendor, in the absurd garments of the nether
world, at all subsequent revels. Rather than
be humiliated by such a companion, the
writer would live and die a Wowser.

the Solomon Islander of another epoch props

Shore Bridge, and turns his eyes from his
guide book to the historic spot where the

less catch his eye:—

And then he will recall, with a warm glow
of gratitude, that all is yet well with the
Empire.
The sentiment and charm of “Milestones”
continues to fill Sydney Criterion with de-
lighted audiences. And the almost perfect
cast adds a coping-stone to the success.
