It was the expressed intention of Allen
Doone to give a fortnight to “The Parish
Priest’s” tame morality mission at Sydney
Palace Theatre, but trade has been so brisk
that the evangel has been extended at least
another week. It seems to be the nature of
Sydney, to hunt for novelty even if it lead
in the direction of the Ten Commandments.
* is * is
“The Monk and the Woman” continues to
be a Scandal at the Adelphi, Sydney, or a
Great Moral Lesson, according to the point
of view. One thing is certain —it is a great
financial success; and, in an age of moral
doubt and mental uncertainty, profits cover
a multitude of qualms.
“My Fancy” is not, as might be suspected,
the title of Taylor and Arnold’s latest rag-
time song, or Claire Romaine’s newest room-
wanner, but the nom-de-hop of the newest
Tivoli dancer; and The Bulletin offers no
opposition when the management books her
as the “greatest sand- and soft-shoe dancer
of the age.” She is billed in awe as having
appeared at a Royal Command performance
in London, and she is quite capable of giving
George Rex his money’s worth. Minnie
Kauffman, the pretty trick cyclist, and
Chinko, the youthful juggler, are putting in
a farewell season. The rest of the pro-
gramme hasn’t altered much. Next week the
Ratty Frank troupe of ground acrobats will
begin their eely business.

“The Count of Monte Christo,” a tabloid
version of Dumas’ novel, took 60 minutes
to unwind its three reels at the Crystal
Palace during its stay. It is rather lengthy,
but the film is a good one, and follows the
yarn as closely as the camera can. The hero’s
missions to Napoleon at Elba, his imprison-
ment and his escape to become owner of the
Island of Monte Cristo, his subsequent re-
turn home and the disposal, one by
one, of his haters —it is all very well worked
out. “Heredity,” another of the endless
Indian yarns, was a good second. “The Plot
That Failed,” a Labor v. Capital yarn, is
booked for the tail-end of the week.
* * x *
When the moving-picture colonel puts a
good detective yarn into the field, he can
generally be sure of victory; and West’s
knew it when they unwound “Nick Winter
and the Stolen Favorite,” at the Glaciarium
this week. Nick is another of the very
numerous made-to-order detectives who wan-
der through about 3000 feet of film, without
doing as much as O’Callaghan’s sleuths, and
then wake up during the last few flicks and
win by a neck. In this case a gang makes
use of the remarkable likeness of one of its
members to Nicholas W. to steal Whirlwind,
the favorite for the Grand Prix of 1936, or
somewhere. If the film had been 500 ft.
shorter the gang would have made a good
job of it, but the extra distance upset the
pot, and Winter, Esq., got another rise in
consequence. Next week “Mimosa Fan,” a
Jap yarn, will be flicked.
Hugh Lloyd is earning godfr money and
delighting National Amphitheatre patrons
by bounding madly upon a rope. Some day
he will inadvertently bound off the rope and
break his neck, and then those who were for-
tunate enough to be present at the tragedy
will feel that they have not only got their
money’s worth, but a bit over. The public’s
love of danger (the other fellow’s danger)
makes countless vaudevillians risk their
glittering lives. John Fuller, tenor, is still
causing the welkin to ring with his plain-
tive cries, and Zif, Lyla Thompson and
Pedrini help to strengthen a fairly powerful
bill.

At the Royal the color of Cleopatra’s hair
is still making matter for good advt. dis-

wig. The Serpent of Old Nile was remark-

changed the color and dressing of her hair
between the times when Antony tore himself
away from her coils, andi to the
palace where his pleasure lay. The question
invites Shakspearian controversy.

Melbourne Princess’s again shelters “The
P>ad Girl of the Family,” the bedroom play
whose rare moments of sanity are like the
feeble glimmer of a vesta in a twilight
world. There is little to provoke enthusiasm
in the resurrection, though Hilliard Vox's
chaste rendering of the villain’s part is a
matter for ashamed applause. As Harry
Gordon, Vox illuses the Bad Girl with the
starched effrontery of the perfect swell, and
his chase of the chick of the foolish Lord
Erskine, whom his pa holds deep in the
financial soup, is equally sinister, but well-
behaved. Lieutenant Marsh, R.N., who
loves Gladys Erskine in the blue-and-gold
upholstery of the Bulldog Navy, is our old
acquaintance Robert Inman, who, having
fitted his head with a flaming thatch, might
stand for a bit of bushfire, P. V. Scully
enacts Lord Erskine, and J. P. O’Neill, as
“Shylock” Gordon in earnest pursuit of his
pound of lamb, makes a brave attempt to
raise the ghost of the late John F. Sheridan
and put it into a pair of outrageous check
pants. Miss Elwyn Harvey is a pleasing
ingenue as Gladys Erskine, and Miss Ada
Guildford as the Worst Girl in the Collec-
tion tries hard to be that boshter superla-
tive. Miss Buckley and the gramaphonic
D’Arcy Ivelway hold the stage, and occa-
sionally the audience* as the comic relief,
Miss Buckley sporting glad rags, as usual,
and insisting that she is the entertainment.
Of the latest explorer:—
Professor Baldwin Spencer’s lecture on the North-
ern Territory might have been a mere picture show
for all that 50 per cent, of the crowded audience in
Melbourne Town Hall could hear of his utterances.
Admission was free; therefore, nobody felt justified
in exhorting the lecturer to speak up. When he was
most audible he sounded like a special pleader for the
Federal Government, whose agent he is. Everything
in the Government’s management of the Territorv is
beyond criticism, from Spencer’s point of view. Away
from the coast the climate is good; in fact, it is
delightful and invigorating for four months of the
year. Its drawbacks have been overstated. There is
no urgent necessity that the Professor can see for
the Government to lure settlers to this land of pro-
mise with greater inducements than have thus far
failed to attract them. The only question that wor-
ries Spencer, seemingly, is how the aborigines can be
fully protected and maintained in their native use-
lessness. He doesn’t even dream of a dav when the
blacks shall have duly disappeared under the in-
fluences of civilisation, and the North shall be a fully
protected portion of White Australia. Statistician
Kmbbs estimates the happy heathen population at
20,000. Spencer guesses 40,000.

“Bobby’s Father” has been keeping public
attention in custody at Spencer’s Olympia,
Melbourne. Bobby’s parent is a cracksman
of the deepest scowl; but under the influence
of Robert, who interrupts him at a mid-
night job, he jettisons his instruments of
felony and dedicates his future to pious pur-
suits. A similar tale of reformation was
related in “Tim” : but Tim becomes a ruly
member of society through sheer starvation,
inasmuch as his papa dies and he has to get
work in order to fill up the gap in his pro-
vision department. “Willie’s Ticker” was
good, “The Green-Eyed Monster” was
better, and Pathg’s Gazette showed George
V. inspecting .guards of honor, as usual.

Weather in Adelaide : Warm. Amusement
calendar: Quiet. Royal: The Anderson
Co. in Nat Gould’s sporting “Chance of a
Lifetime,” with the mare in the bedroom,
and the stolen Gold Cup which Mark Twain
used _to make fun of. Tivoli: Closed.
King’s: Crowded, with Albert and Hillier
(who are funny, but ought to cut out some
of their highly-spiced jokes), and the
Frenchman Bow, who wrestles amazingly
with himself, while his lady partner floats
round and warbles. Empire: A Cinderella
pantomime, filmed. Wondergraph: Scott’s
Antarctic pictures, very comforting on a
warm night. West’s: The Robinson Crusoe
girl and tile Waterloo picture. And the
Dandies (outdoor concert) and the Pavilion
(indoor pictures all day) have both reached
that pleasant stage of public approbation at
which crowds drop in without regard to the
programme, certain of getting something
good.
Short, but very pointed, is the inscription
which the relatives of St. Clair Stone, who
was killed at Mt. Lyell on October 21, have
placed on his tombstone at Queenstown
(Tas.) : “A human life sacrificed on the
altar of dividends.” It sounds pretty raw
when it is put that*fway ; but the statement
is quite true when applied to most fatal
mining accidents. There are very few that
are not preventable—it is simply a matter of
spending money on the prevention.
The Liberals don’t know what to do about
Deakin’s Ballarat seat in the Reps. Deakin
himself was a very doubtful winner; and
any other man the Liberals have in the bag
is an almost certain loser against the Labor
nominee, McGrath, M.L.A. Just at present
the tempter is whispering in the ear of Pea-
cock, also M.L.A. But Peacock has a fairly
safe seat in the Assembly, and some hopes
of taking Peter Mcßride’s place at the Mines
Department, when the present Minister goes
to London as Agent-General, vice Taverner ;
and the owner of the laugh isn’t enthusiastic
about the exchange. A certain seat and a
probable portfolio, even among the insects,
look much better than a pretty certain wal-
loping in an ineffectual attempt to get among
the lions.

“Mdb”; Garnet Walch began in Hobart, and
ended in Melb., • but was emphatically a Sydney
mhn. In a decade there he got about 40 of his
pieces upon the stage. This was unparalleled suc-
cess for an Australian. His best was .the extrava-
ganza of Trookulentos,” which had more vogue than
anything on the boards from an Australian pen ex-
cept “Robbery Under Arms.” Reading some of
Garnet s theatrical filigree and svllabub, I have
found the literary quality high. Taking a different
line, he equalled the almost peerless Boueicault in
good, workaday stage dialogue. Compare Walch’s
pantomimes with Hickory Wood’s! All thoroughly
well-written rhyme, quite up to such bygone glories
as the verses of H. J. Byron, the Broughs,
and Burnand. A pity Walch ever came to
Melbourne. He got right out of his swim
there, and two-thirds of his working life was
comparatively lost. Though a fish out of water as'
sec. of Melbourne Athenaeum, he nevertheless
galvanised it with a prodigious rush of about 150
new members. Then he practically had to go.
Even less of a success was the weekly Town Talk,
produced by the clever but ill-assorted trio, Walch,
Whitworth and Conway.

Oscar Asche has hones of hitting London with his
Australian production of “Antony and Cleopatra.” It
differs widely from Beerbohm Tree’s arrangement,
and will probably be preferred by Bardolaters, if
they can accept the un-Roman Oscar as a possible
Antony. Tree sought excuses for spectacle outside
the action of the play. Thus he introduced Cleopatra
in her barge—the golden-pooped barge she sat in
when she went to cultivate Antony’s acquaintance,
though their acquaintance had ripened into cuddle
long before the outset of Shakspeare’s play.
“The Chee-ild”: A parting word to “C. Ockney”
in this one-act play controversy. He now admits (B.
2/1/’ 13) that seven of the London theatres in the
fashionable quarter were, at latest, playing one-act
pieces as curtain-raisers, and four others were utilis-
ing entertainers for the same purpose. But when
“C. 0.” proceeds to say that the remaining 16
theatres give no preliminary canter of any kind, isn’t
it a little brassy in face of the fact that the great
majority of those 16 are running musical comedies,
musical plays, Shakspeare, Shaw (Little Theatre),
spectacular shows and (once more) long plays to none
of which does anyone ever expebt or get a “raiser” ?
Let me, in a last line, return to my original point.
Even “The Quaints,” “Jokers,” “Giddygoats,” etc.,
are curtain-raisers of a sort. Do we get even them,
much less the one-act play, as a sort of sherry to
the brief feed of a two-hours-long comedy?

J. C. Williamson cos a has “struck it” in London.
When she left Sydney 18 months ago she dropped

After this she assisted Bert Gilbert in a sketch

sketch, ‘The Ticket Collector,’ was off the bill on
Thursday, Friday and Saturday. However, one’s loss

and held the stage for 14 minutes, charming all
present with her personality and fine voice. The
management was so satisfied with results that it
paid the full week’s salary—in itself sufficient proof
of Miss* Ray’s success.”

“Didn’t know he had a brother,” a good many
Australians no doubt said when they read of t' e
suicide in London, at 48, of William .Tohn Jones,
a 13-years-younger brother of Henry Arthur. There
was reason for the ignorance, as W. .1. Jones had
taken the name of Sylvanus Dauncey for his work
as dramatist and touring manager. (The father of
the playwrights was Sylvanus Jones, farmer.) Wil-
liam had his thoughts turned towards drama by the
success of Henry Arthur, whose “Clerical Error”
was produced in 1870. The big hit of “The Silver
King” came in 1882. Three years later Jones minor
began, at 21, with “A Divided Duty,” otherwise
known as “A Month after Date.” “Charity’s Cloak,”
“The Reckoning,” and “Love at Home,” an adapta-
tion, were all staged in 1891. With George D.
Day he was responsible in 1898 for “Bilbery of Til-
bury” and “Campano, or The Wandering Minstrel.”
His nervous breakdown, he said himself, was oae to
the worries of London management after the com-
parative ease of the provinces.
On Boxing Night, the largest theatre in Maoriland
was opened at Wellington. It was constructed to
the order of the Fuller firm to seat 2355 people.
Primarily, the intention is to use it as a picture
theatre, but it has been fully appointed for almost
any class of show. The stage is 80ft. in width
by 40ft,. deep; there are 12 boxes (seat 72), stalls
(seat 933), dress circle (seat 350), and upper circle
(seat 1000).
“Private Boxer”: Those erratic and perverse
mummers! I noted, with pain, in a Sunday paper
recently, a par. in which the writer alluded to the
curious fact that Gilbert Parker’s dialogue in his
“Faust” was written in blank verse, but that in
the mouths of the actors it sounded like prose.
What is the use of being a poet-playwright these
times? I once knew a playwright (he wrote panto-
mimes) who had the opposite experience. To ex-
plain my meaning I have asked his permission to
reproduce a nonsense-rhyme he perpetrated on the
subject:—
A clever young author (so I’m
Informed) wrote a Grand Pantomime;
His “book” was in prose,
But the pros, (goodness knows
How they did it) all spoke it in rhyme.
“C.0.M.” : Now that the Sydney picture show com-
bine is practically complete, and the firms which
made capital out of their independence have been
brought into line, the suburban shows are likely to
be squeezed out in large numbers, and the suburban
crowds forced to come into town for their jigger-
graph entertainment. But will the crowds come in ?
They have gone to the local shows because the cheap
amusement was at their door. Were these shows made
impossible along their present lines, through the
charge for the use of films being put up enormously,
cheap vaudeville performances would probably replace
them, to the great loss of the combine. Pictures in
and of themselves are not a tremendously strong at-
traction. The public mostly finds in watching them
a cheap and pleasant mode of killing time. Nobody
is going to pay theatre prices for the best of them,
and nobody is going to make long and frequent jour-
neys from the suburbs to see them. Moreover, when
the Combine is well on its feet, the Referendum pro-
posals will be carried, and then everybody connected
with the proposed monopoly will have to pay a fine,
and the organisation will be smashed up and the
names of a iot of proprietors of jiggergraphs will be
Mud. And to be called Mud in such a connection
may not be at all a good advt.
Barton’s Circus, now touring Maoriland, has got
the steel-nerved man, although he is not advertised
as such. The other day the show was aboard ship,
when a leopard got loose, and during the subsequent
proceedings the man (a performer, appropriately
named Tackles) put in a piece of unrehearsed work
that would have made a star-animal turn look insig-
nificant by comparison. The leopard started the en-
tertainment by killing two baboons, and while he
was being driven off he got clear on the deck with
a long neck-chain dangling. Tackles grabbed the
chain, and, throwing his arms round the beast, em-,
braced him like a long-lost lover, yelling meanwhile
for help. The leopard cursed, spat, and tore with
his plaws at his assailant’s throat and body, but the,
man hugged close, while half a dozen perspiring i
circus hands bashed the great cat into insensibility •
with tent pegs and other emergency weapons. Theni
he was hurled into his cage, and the man with the
iron nerves retired to pull himself together with i
sticking plaster.
“The Oldest”: Apropos the Holloways (B. 9/1/T3).
This writer knew Papa Deorwyn, grandfather of
Beatrice Holloway, when under another name he was
doing well in commercial life in the ’so’s and ’6o’s
in Melbourne. It was boom-time in “Penny Read-
ings” when the mother of Beatrice made her debut, 1
a mere child, as a reciter. The “readings” were in
the Fitzroy Court House. She was “Little Alice”
then. Papa used to recite long pieces. “The May
Queen,” “Horatius,” and such-like. Papa was open-
handed, and trouble came in his business ; so he faced
the footlights again—-he had been an actor in early
life. Mrs. Deorwyn, a fine woman, died only a
couple of years ago, aged 84 or thereabout.
“The Melbourne and Country Directory,”
published by Sands and McDougall, continues
to grow ; it has put on 78 pages since last
year. There are now more than 1000 pages
in the alphabetical section alone, and rural
industries (apart from “country trades”)
run into 175 pages. As a source of informa-
tion the red-covered product of Sands and
Mac is invaluable.
The reverence for the naval Johnnie which
is supposed to fill the mercantile marine
received a shock at the disclosures in the
matter of H.M.S. Torch. This vessel, which
left Australia for England during last year,
was in such an unseaworthy condition that,
had an ordinary shellback taken it out as
a merchant ship, he would have had a lively
chance of standing a charge in a criminal
court, according to the provisions of the
Merchant Shipping Act. Admiralty investi-
gations showed that the Torch had not
undergone a proper survey of its hull-
plating for seven years before leaving on the
12,000 miles voyage. A steam lighter bound
from Garden Island to Pyrmont would get
better attention than that. The British
Parliament has been told that the naval
dockyard officials in Australia have incurred
the haughty displeasure of the Lords of the
Admiralty, and, considering the. casual risk
of the lives of the ship’s crew, that seems
the least that could be expected.
PLAYING HELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yes, Rrown was very gav
That ni.ir'.-o when we met;
His wife had gone away
A month or two to stay.
“I'll shake things up, you bet!”
He giggled. “Eh? Well, well!
Ihey’re all right, married joys,
But still—‘Boys will be boys.’
I reckon I’ll play hell!”
I met Brown yesterday ;
That sober, married hack
Was looking far from gay;
At home he could not stay.
“I wish my wife were back,”
He grumbled. Then he fell
To cursing loneliness.
I pitied his distress;
I said, “Why not play hell?”
Brown looked me in the eye—•
His glance was serpent-cold—
A sidelong look and sly;
He yearned to tell a lie—
A lie immense and bold.
I saw his bosom swell;
I guessed the ghastly truth ;
He’d lost the art of youth —-
The art of “playing hell!”
N.s.w.

The Bulletin takes back all it ever said
about the Melbourne police. They have
just arrested a deserter from the Challenger.
Knowing that the police were looking for
him, he kindly joined the force to give
them a hand in the search. That was only
two years ago, but already they have recog-
ised him. They wouldn’t have done it, cer-
tainly, if he hadn’t had the bad luck to
meet with an accident which sent him into
the police hospital.
FROM THE CATTY POINT OF VIEW.
BETTY : “ They say the mother is as young
looking as the daughter."
LETTY : “You mean the daughter is as old
looking as the mother.”
No previous Wages Board determination
in Victoria —not even the first, tentative
ones —caused so much frothing as that
which has just given the clerks a lift. The
best of the big commercial houses made a
few adjustments, or raised a couple of
juniors to be seniors (one junior to three
seniors is the allowance), and that was all
they needed ; but the “hard” business firms
and the struggling, scrambling possessors of
a brass plate and a brassier cheek put in
applications for exemptions by the score.
The worst feature was that many clerks
themselves asked for permits to accept less
than the minimum of 48s. per week, on the
ground that they weren’t worth it, and
would get the sack if they had to be paid
the minimum. But the Department sat
tight, and waited for the threatened volcano.
And, as it expected, the volcano didn’t
arrive. The dismissals were comparatively
few; and as there is no scarcity of work in
the Cabbage Garden- —and vastly better-paid
toil than clerking at Is. an hour —those who
did get notice to quit will probably reap the
most advantage from the award. The
Bulletin just at the moment remembers a
leading Australian merchant of the younger
school, who, after spending 12 years in an
insurance office in Adelaide, was crowded
out by an amalgamation. He was then get-
ting 355. a week, and didn’t honestly believe
he was worth more. But when he was
thrown on his own resources, he found that
he wasn’t a poor little mouse or caterpillar ;
and he has established thriving “houses” in
two States since then.
Mr. O. W. Rosenliain, of Melbourne, has
published in pamphlet form the paper he read
to the Ornithological Society’s Congress in
Launceston (Tas.) two months ago. It is
a plea for the preservation of the birds that
are slaughtered to make women’s hats
fashionable, or poisoned by orchardists and
farmers under the delusion that they are
pests. The pamphlet deals with bird life' in
many lands, and states that everywhere it is
being foolishly destroyed. Lord Kitchener
reported that the Egyptian cotton crop in
1911 was partially eaten by insects whose
natural enemies, the birds, had been wan-
tonly killed. . He was then considering the
question of the absolute protection of feath-
ered creatures. In America the inroads of
insects due to the diminished supply of birds
are said to be costing the country stacks of
dollars. And Australia, which was never
overstocked with insect-eaters, is going the
way of the older countries. Mr. Rosenhain,
who has received assurances of sympathy
from Lady Denman and the Fuller lady,
quotes Longfellow at the end of his paper:—
You call them thieves and pillagers, but know
They are the winged wardens of your farms
Who from the cornfield drive the insidious foe
And from your harvests keep a hundred harms.
If Longfellow had strolled around the Block
any day last winter he might have dedicated
a special verse to Woman. Something in
this way:—
The blood of lovely birds is on your head.
The feathers of mute murdered songsters deck
Your monstrous hats, whilst skins of creatures dead
Tell tales of guilty deeds about your neck.
“Montere”: The Bible in Schools agita-
tion is going strongly once more in Maori-
land. It is a sort of epidemic,
liable to break out at any time,
but, from past experience, not par-
ticularly dangerous to the Educa-
tion system. The teachers, who
see the matter from the practical
point of view, have all the time
been opposed to it. Were the
teaching of the Bible part of the
regular school work, it would im-
pose upon the school staffs work
for which they have neither taste
nor time. In country districts
where there is but one teacher in
the local school, and the local com-
mittee has a say in the selection
of the man or woman to be en-
trusted with the job, something in
the nature of conscience tests
would be absolutely certain to creep
in. A Wowser district would in-
sist on having a Wowser, and an
R.C. district an R.C. teacher, and
all other qualifications would be
lost sight of in the great question
as to how the new master or mis-
tress would handle the Bible
lesson. Naturally, teachers object
to the creation of such a system of
selection. On the other hand,
there is certainly a lot to be said
for the reading of the Bibid as
literature, but it is not exactly the
kind of literature best suited for
primary schools. And, anyway, if
you consider the matter on that
ground alone, the Koran or the
Mahaparinibbana Suttanta has
just about as good a claim to be studied.
Still, the matter need not cause anyone to
lose sleep. As on previous occasions the
Bible in Schools party will become a house
divided against _ itself the moment it begins
to define the thing it wants.
Recently a lot of hot air was wasted in
Melbourne and other places about the great
value of the “cat” as a deterrent to crime;
and to prove its effectiveness Alan Moore,
the burglar who shot at Geoffrey Syme, was
ordered 15 lashes. That was on December
15. Now, three weeks after, when that ap-
plication of the “cat” ought to have been
putting in its best deterrent work, Mel-
bourne wakes up to find that another armed
Sikes has actually murdered a householder.
Something seems to have gone wrong once
more with the beautiful Madden-blessed
theory.
MIXED CLOUD BATHING.
The local council objects—as usual.
An irresistibly beautiful grocer lately took
off his apron and joined in the Melbourne
Age correspondence re the wearing of' wed-
ding rings by men. The grocer (a passing
reference to a pound of butter betrayed him)
told how he used to be troubled by the flir-
tatious advances of adoring females who,
regardless or unaware of the fact that he
was a married man, would linger at his
counter and sigh inquiries about sugar and
honey. To save himself, he started to grow
a moustache and look like a married grocer.
(The married grocer, seemingly, always re-
tires from the world behind a barrier of
whiskers.) The fascinating experimentalist
had a quiet period while the new decoration
was in the smudgy stage; but when it shot
out, and bloomed, and was a joy to witness,
another swarm of females pursued him and
fought for his smile. They were the older
fashioned girls who adored whiskers. Then,
a,fter a night of pedestrianism with the new
baby, he came to work one morning with one
of its safety pins sticking in the lapel of
his coat. In a day or so, no lovesick woman
was buying the usual pound of butter in
three lots in order that she might gaze into
his eyes a little longer. The moral of it all
appears to be that, though loveliness is a ter-
rible trial to a happily married grocer, he
can, thank Heaven, still find a perfect haven
of refuge behind the infant’s safety pin.
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
An old Sydney flour mill, long a land mark on the
Parramatta-road, at its. junction with the Liverpool-
road, has just been demolished. In years gone by it
was known as Boylson’s, and a busy place it was.
The inn alongside was built in the first ’3o’s
by David Ramsay, with Simeon Lord behind him,
fdr John' Ireland, who called it Speed the
Plough. Ireland, whose descendants* still keep a pub
on Liverpool-road, had an interest in a line of coaches
which ran from the Talbot Inn, Upper George: street,
to Parramatta and Liverpool. Ramsay, who was a
doctor and merchant and son-in-law of Simeon Lord,
had 480 acres there, a dowry given with Miss Lord
when she became Mrs. Ramsay. In late years the
name of the inn was simply The Plough. Close by
were two large estates —Ashfield Park,, owned 100
years ago by Robert Campbell (of The Wharf), and
afterwards by James Underwood ; the other owned by
Augustus Alt, who got it as a grant for services ren-
dered as Surveyor-General. Alt came with Governor
Phillip, and occupies a very much neglected grave in
St. John’s cemetery, Parramatta. The travellers to
Parramatta in the old days must have been thirsty
souls. Close to Ashfield Park was the Ship Inn, or
Half-way House, kept by Henry Austin ; half a mile
further on was The Fox and the Grapes ; and a mile
and a half still further on was The Coach and Horses.
There were two others before you reached what is
now Granville —one called the Cottage in the Grove,
kept by the father of the late John Lackey, M.L.C.
At the .old racecourse alongside, John, who became
President of the Leg. Council, imbibed all his sport-
ing proclivities.
A good many severe things have been said
about the motor hog and his habits; but
there is something to be said for the oily
animal. He has his trials, and one of the
worst of them is the woman who steps into
a river of traffic and starts to paddle across
without troubling to look to right or left.
Frequently her hat is so constructed that
she is unable to look anywhere but straight
ahead. In any case, she only starts to get
out of the way when a pulled-up dray-horse
snorts over her shoulder, or a tram laden
with yells and waving umbrellas stops with
a grinding of brakes within a foot of the
place where her blouse is buttoned. Then
she squeals indignantly, and hobbles out of
the way. Usually she is saved without any
damage being done; but it sometimes hap-
pens that a traffic tangle works up at the
critical moment, and something has to be
bumped. Then the unluckiest. driver has to
select the cheapest- thing to collide with.
Last week, in Melbourne, a blankly indiffer-
ent lady, who scorned to look where she or
anyone else was going, got a motorist so
boxed that he had to take his choice between
herself, a van and a tram. He picked the
tram, and when things had stopped smash-
ing, his car looked as if it had jumped at
the Great Pyramid. The obstructor of
traffic looked curiously at the ruin she had
wrought, and went on to hold up the wheeled
procession elsewhere. If some genius would
write a cheap handbook on “How to Cross
the Road,” a lot of motorists would buy it —-
for presentation purposes.
THE WIDOWS MIGHT.
SHE: “I suppose you and your brothers were
sorry to hear of your uncle's death?"
HE: “Yes, very, very sorry."
SHE : “What did he leave?"
HE : “A confounded widow we knew nothing
about."
THE MECHANICAL MILLENNIUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tt is undeniable that in the near future the motor will
be a great asset in the promotion of national temper-
ance. — Argus (3/1/T3).
There’s a sober time approaching when we’ll sign the
pledge no more,
And the lust for lush will perish as the mileage-
figures soar ;
When the wind that fans our whiskers on the dusty
downward grade
Will wake a thirst for nothing more than lukewarm
lemonade.
There’s a beerless age ahead of us, a dry time draw-
ing near,
When each man has his motor and no money left for
beer!
Would we help the common herd in simple virtue to
advance?
Would we elevate the masses, we who toil for tem-
perance ?
Would we push our beerless barrow yet a great way
further on
Till we’ve broken up the brewers and the last, lone
pub is gone ?
Let us buy Plain Bill a motor-car, to take his missus
out—
When he’s finished buying petrol he will have no
cash to shout.
VICTORIA.

One of the duties of the matron on the
immigrant ship which brings out the “trained
domestics” from the factories of Manchester
And Nottingham is to see that the tender
female cargo is safe under hatches at a cer-
tain hour. On one ship the matron struck
trouble, and finally one night found this
touching little thing (by Mrs. Hemans or
somebody) pasted up in the women’s quar-
ters of the floating doss-house: —
The curfew rings for Girls Only
The Matron feels sad and lonely.
Where are niv girls,
Mv precious pearls?
In the glory hole, dancing Salome!
The verse was dreadful, but tbe truth was
probably more so.
Of course, there is no truth in the old
wheeze that there is one law for the rich and
another for the poor ; but it would take more
than a handful of Sydney magistrates to dis-
prove the rumor. Two cases last week: —
(1) The Representative of the Hungry Classes: A
man charged at the Central Police Court with having
obtained a meal at the A.B.C. Caffi on the previous
Saturday? and endeavored to leave the premises with-
out paving of it, was sentenced to 14 days’ hard
labor.— (D.T., 8/1/T3.)
(2) And the Representative of the Proprietary
Classes: Fred Hunter pleaded guilty at the Redfern
Court, and was fined £. 2 or one month, for unlaw-
fully assaulting William Somner, a man in his em-
ploy, whom he hit in the eye and also kicked in the
ribs.—(D.T., 9/l/’13.)

The Christians are still loving one another
furiously in the Castlemaine (Vic.) district,
where the local preachers went on strike 12
months ago. One of many denouements
was reached a week or so back. The head
serang or president of “Conference” ruled
that the trustees of the church where the
row was could legally attend the “Quarterly
meeting.” But the local parson, having his
own views, insisted upon the three trustees
quitting. They wouldn’t quit, so the police
were called in to assist in their exit from
“the Lord’s house” ! There is no record of
what the angels thought. They, unfortu-
nately, are off the reporters’ beat.

“Persina” saves her country:—
That something unusual was stirring in our Gipps
land townThip was evident. Even a stranger must
have noticed it: There were secret whisperings and
mysterious conferences. First of all it. looked as
though the whole town was changing its addres .
Every person was struggling with «e article of
furniture. But it wasn’t that—it was merely that the
Women’s League had issued invitations to an At
Home at the local hall, “to meet Mr. Irvine M.H.R.,
and Miss Cameron, organising secretary Of course
I went The stage had been decorated with pot ,
nlants and kerosene cases, only a convenient table-
cloth partly hid the cases. Seated on these were the
committee. A farmer, who had been a waiter, and,
therefore knew all about etiquette, had crowded him-
self into his old dress-suit and ushered in the guests.
Several mistook him for Irvine. Almost the first to
arrive was our local meaty butcher. Mr. Andrew
McPherson ” called the usher in a loud voice. What
Hie hell do you want?” said Andy, coloring to some
extent. You could have heard a bomb burst. Andy
noticed the ladies seated on the stage in evening
dress. The sight was too much for him. He turned
tail and fled. Among the pretty-early arrivals was
a voung woman who said she thought it was going o
be a dance and proffered a coin to the usher, who
attempted to refuse her admission. However, she was
soon past him, and took her unannounced. Mrs.
pvan was the next, bonnet and all. She had her in
vitation card in her stocking, but produced it easily.
“Mrs Pumpkin,” roared the usher presently. Pre-
Hnt sir ’’ said Mrs. Pumpkin, and pushed on towards
the stage The train came and went. No appearance
of Mr Irvine or Miss Cameron. The strains of an
accordeon were heard outside the hall. Let s have
a darnce,” said the young woman who had proffered
Hie coin. She was the only person not disappointed,
for a “darnce” it was.
TERRIBLE PAIN ON PALM OF HAND.
“One night I struck a match and the whole box
went off in my hand, burning me across the palm,
says Mr. J. It. Worseley, Margate (Tas.). The pain
was awful. I went to the store and looked round for
something to relieve me. I saw Chamberlain s Pain
Balm, and noticed the word “burns on the abe .
I rubbed it on my hand, with the result that the
pain quickly disappeared and I was able to go to
sleep In the morning I found the skin whole, and
the burn quickly healed without even leaving a
scar.”*

This isn’t the first growl of its kind :
It is to be hoped that the Commonwealth Bank will
improve on some of the methods of the States’ Sav-
ings Banks. The counters of the latter are too
crowded, the clerks too few, and the manner of their
address too loud and familiar. Mrs. Mary Jones,
after elbowing to the counter, hands over her book
and a couple of pounds. The book is thrown from
one clerk to another, and when initials are com-
pleted, arrives back. Mrs. Jones has not changed her
place, but the clerk, casting his eyes over the crowd.
calls with the lungs ot
a police court crier,
“Mary Jones, Mary
Jones.” He cries with
the same lungs when
he has located her:
“How much?” Then
the book is delivered.
When ’Mis. Hannah
Smith wishes to draw
out £ 5 and hands over
her book with the duly
signed order, the book,
after going the rounds,
is returned with the
five sovereigns or paper
money on the open up-
turned face of the
book, accompanied be-
fore delivery with the
cry, “Hannah Smith,
Hannah Smith, how
much ?” Hannah says
how much, though
everybody at the coun-
ter sees how much, and
she retires to stow it
away in some part of
her clothing. What
fine opportunities this
method affords to
thieves and spielers!
Altogether, the system
is bad. Denison Miller
is exhorted to think
out a better one.
“Melb,” discursively :
The Methodies have a move-on law, and, except
under most exceptional circumstances, a man can t
hang on to one pulpit more than five years. Mostly
he is passed along at the end of three. Yet Pastor
Edgar, the State Cabinet Minister’s brother, has just
been expatiating on his 29 years at Wesley Church in
Lonsdale-street. In 1857 the Wesleyans had a church
at the corner of Collins and Queen streets. I*V e y
sold to the Bank of Australasia for £40,000. The
denomination, demoralised by cash, thereupon built
this awful Wesley Church. The famous Jonah
organ was transferred into it from Collins-street.
This machine, already old, and said to have been
formerly in a Dublin music-hall, was bought for an
R.C. church at Rio. But the ship couldn t get into
Rio on account of bad weather, so the organ had to
come on to Melbourne. Again, however, there was
trouble, the vessel being wrecked at the Heads. As
a spec, the Wesleyans bought the organ for £5OO.
Maybe because of the windy Jonah, the church was a
dismal failure for 33 years. Then Edgar started as
a successful imitator of Taylor, of Sydney, who, by
the way, also gave the tip to Hugh Price Hughes, m
London. Edgar’s chief standby has been the “Pleasant
Sunday Afternoon.” Consul-General Hwang was one
of the' speakers last year, and made us think when he
estimated that when Australia has only 10 millions,
China, Japan, India, Malaya, Java and the Archi-
pelago will have a thousand millions.
The story of the
latest Melbourne burg-
lary, with murder at-
tached, should be an
incentive to women to
always remember
their natural means
of protection when
danger threatens. Two
burglars, armed with
revolvers, invaded the
bedroom of Arthur
Trotter and his wife
in the dead of night,
and demanded deliv-
ery of the money
which that commer-
cial traveller and col-
lector had secreted
under the bedding.
Whilst Trotter par-
leyed with the rob-
bers his wife lay ter-
rified into silence.
Perceiving that the
man who had him cov-
ered with a gun was
likely to fire, she im-
plored her husband to
give up the money.
Trotter pluc k i 1 y
sprang out of bed to
grapple with the rob-
ber, and received a
bullet in the head,
whereupon his mur-
derer’s mate took the
money-bag from the
place where Trotter
had been lying. Then
the wife shrieked, and
the robbers fled. The
piercing scream is
Nature’s ’ gift to
woman. Unhappily,
she is disposed to use
it at the wrong time,
not having been
taught to think of it
as a' firearm —a thing
to be turned on im-
mediately at the man who would terrify her
into silence. It may be arguecl that the
man, in this case, was prepared to put a
bullet into Mrs. Trotter if she shrieked;
but the chances are that he wouldn’t have
done anything of the sort.^
The Adelaide Register has been trying
to revive the “viaduct” scare, so familiar
when the Adelaide-to-Melbourne railway was
being made. About seven miles from the
city, just as the line is getting into the hills,
it crosses a narrow gully, on a steel struc-
ture which from a little distance looks spi-
dery, and, having no parapet, gives an odd
sensation to anyone looking out and down
while crossing. Engineers all swore that
the thing was as safe as any other bit of the
line, but for years most people felt relieved
each time on getting safely across. The
present alarm, which professes to come from
“an official of the department,” raises two
ERMYNTRUDE.
points—(l) The little bridges are 25 years
old, and iron corrodes; (2) they are pecu-
liarly liable to be struck by lightning. But
the Railway Department itself has replied
coldly that original care and present in-
spection make the viaduct as safe now as
ever, and that damage by lightning is so
outside railway experience as to be not worth
bothering about.
THE MINGLERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What our reporter calls “the unconventional ming-
l'ne of the sexes" is much more unwholesome than
■an be any piles of dirty jam and fish tins.
—Melbourne Age on Seaside Camps.
A sight that gives me much distress
Is George without his trousers,
Garbed, scantily, in bathing dress
Proscribed by saintly Wowsers,
And Gerty, gay and forward flirt,
Without the regulation skirt.
Though ’tis a fearsome sight, I ween,
When jam tins strew the shingle,
It is a far more shocking scene
When Bert and Benjy mingle
With Maude and Winnie in the wave;
It hurts to see them so behave.
The .melancholy ilead marine
Sown thick along the beaches,
The can that £eld the late sardine,
Or potted prawn, or peaches,
Are things of innocence beside
Gay Tom and Topsy in the tide.
I hold by stern morality,
Despite the worldlings’ scoffing,
And though it pains my soul to see
A fish tin in the offing,
’Tis naught beside the things I feel
Whene’er I hear Belinda squeal.
Indeed, this tin that held sardine
My sad soul sorely vexes.
The fish it harbored might have been
Unwed, and mixed in sexes!
Good brothers, can you wonder, then,
That seaside damsels mix with men?
A pile of picnic scraps, ’tis true,
Can raise a mild commotion.
But what of John and Jane and Sue
Mixed in a single ocean ?
A sight that stabs me to the heart
Is Billo smoodging with his tart.
But hhrk, my brothers, yester eve
I hdd a wondrous vision.
The sun was just about to leave,
With his well-known precision,
When I espied upon the sand
A tin with a familiar brand.
And, as I gazed, mj r limbs grew limp
And giddiness came o’er me ;
For front it stepped a flsh-like imp
That smirked and bowed before me!
His puckered features seemed to be
Awry with spite and devilry.
“Young man,” he said, “You’re wasting time.
Why do you sit there mooning?
So brave a youth, just in his prime,
Should find more joy in spooning.
For see! the ocean hath its pearls.
Go forth and mingle with the girls!”
And from the tins that lay about
Upon the silver shingle
I heard a wee shrill chorus shout.
“Young man, go forth and mingle!”
And then I knew each empty tin
Concealed its special imp within.
I know my eye grew wide and bright.
Despite a life ascetic,
And from the narrow path of right
I felt a tug magnetic,
That sought to draw me o’er the sand
Out to the siren-haunted strand.
I felt the red blood course anew,
I felt my pulses tingle ;
And still the tiny chorus grew:
“Young man, go forth and mingle! ....
Then, from an old, bashed can I saw
A lordly lobster wave a claw.
“Good fellow, have a care!” he said,
“Stray not from pathways upper!
I am the ghost of one long dead,
Slain for a sinful supper.
But once good works were done by me
Amongst the sinners of the sea.
“In life I roamed the vasty deep
Engaged upon a mission,
Which was my fellow-fish to keep
From swimming to perdition.
Now I am dead” (his voice grew thin),
“Alas! they mingle in the tin!
“Beware the blood that bounds and leaps!
Your sinful feelings throttle.
Beware the imp that leers and peeps
From out each tin and bottle!
A submarine Chapzander speaks.
Beware when gay Belinda squeaks !
Lo, as he spoke my blood grew chill,
The spell no longer bound me,
The impish chorus now was still
And silence reigned around me.
The ghostly lobster disappeared ;
Mv heart of base desire was cleared.
But, like a man inspired, I saw
His cause for intervening.
His sad, sweet face, his waving claw
To me were full of meaning.
Indeed, a sainted fish was he,
A very Wowser of the Sea.
You smile, good friend ? But ah, be sure
’Tis not a theme for scoffing;
For well, too well, I know the lure
Of fish tins in the offing.
A devil lurks inside each tin
To tempt unwary souls to sin.
And, to this day, I feel a thrill
Mid tins upon the shingle;
I seem to hear that chorus shrill:
“Young man, go forth and mingle!”
And yet, ’tis naught to what I feel
Whene’er I hear Belinda squeal.

THE RIVALS.
PETER (at the gate): " Jeminy, but won't this
make some of our people jealous!"
“Kus” : Couldn’t get any sleep for the
mosquitoes for a couple of nights, and then remem-
bered the old bush dodge of hanging a piece of raw
pluck over the bunk, with a small tin of kerosene
suspended about 6in. below the meat. Have ever
since been able to sleep outdoors without a net.
The mosks. make for the meat; then the glare which
kerosene gives off, even on a dark night, attracts
them, and they commit wholesale hari-kari. In the
morning the tin is thick with immolated mosks.,
self-sacrificed on the altar of reeking pluck.
Cbloe, concerning a marine blossom: —
rr * le masquerading crab mentioned by “Reka” (B.
/12/ 12) is not altogether peculiar to Fiji. Many
thousands of his first cousins, with similar habits,
frequent the tropic beaches and tidal estuaries of
North Australia. The estuaries of the De Grey, Ord,
and Fitzroy Rivers swarm at certain seasons of the
year with a small crab, not larger, when full grown,
than a florin, and of a brilliant scarlet color. From
v a . d i st^ nce the river-banks have the appearance of
being covered with some bright bloom, but closer
inspection shows myriads of crabs on the march,
sometimes in single file, sometimes in other forma-
tions, but all moving in perfect order. There are
commandants, and staff officers, who might have
studied the necessary “swank” of their profession
under Sergeant Ryrie or Corporal Onslow. They have
outposts and scouting Darties, and their hearing is
so acute that a light step on the beach or a pebble
dropped away in mid-stream is sufficient to alarm
the crabs along half a mile of march, whereupon
they disappear instantly by sinking through the
sort mud. Along the North Queensland beaches are
others of about the same size, and just as militant
looking, but of a bright, electric-blue color.
* * * *
“Machete,” concerning a higli-flyer:—
“V.J.D.’s” sketch, “Told at Vila” (B. 21/11/’l2),
introduces at least one picturesque identity of the
Papua of years ago, in the person of “Thorsen,” or
Sorenson, as he was known then. Christy
had been, as “V.J.D.” states, a member of the Viking
crew which sailed its craft across the Atlantic to
the Chicago Exhibition. But that was not a
thousand-year-old craft. It was a modern replica of
the Norseman’s old sea-dragon. Christy, when I
knew him, was varl or admiral, or whatever the
ancient Northern pirates called it, .of a yawl that was
well known in Sydney when it carried the late Jack
Want and his pals on fishing (and other) expedi-
tions. A couple of Maorilanders, dazzled with Louis
Becke legends, bought the craft all standing in Syd-
ney, and sailed it to Papua, where the luxurious
fittings roused the scorn of the hardened traders and
the delighted wonder of the giant Papuan cockroaches
which went aboard at Samarai. When the Maori-
landers realised sadly what trading in Papua really
meant, they sold out gladly. The purchaser stripped
the vessel, and the crevices of the cushioned divan
yielded many curios, such as cigar-ends, corks, hair-
pins, gloves, a garter or two, and so on. Under whose
regime these accumulated it is impossible to say.
Subsequently, the craft fell into the possession of
the improvident and likable Christy, and his flier
and his fiddle had equal place in his affections.
Nothing delighted him more than a race, and villages
with copra to sell had to wait until his yawl had it
out with some lugger or big, mat-sailed East Cape
canoe, the Guenevere being the only craft in the
Papua of that day able to hold its own with the
native fliers. For these and other reasons, Christy
did not prosper, though his mana was great up and
down the reef. As for the old Guenevere, she gradu-
ally went the way of most fast females, and last 1
saw of her she smelt horribly of bSche-de-mer.
“Te Babarantiko,” as a versatile under-
taker :—■
Following up the subject of the disposal of
corpses by colored peoples, I have seen in cholera
epidemics the peaceful defunct hurrying seaward
on the mighty ebb of the Hooghly. I have helped
to persuade persistent dead to relax the clasp-knife
position taken over our chain cable in the Irrawaddy,
and I could tell you things about the
Meinam, too, and a raft of teakwood logs.
Also, there was the ancient submarine
burial place of Natata islet, in Makin
lagoon, seen by but few, if any other,
white men’s eyes. It was an irregular
circular wall constructed of coral »ock
some 10ft. or 12ft. below lowest ebb.
In the centre of the enclosure was a black
chasm, about 6ft. across, in the reef, the
chasm in its turn being surrounded by a
stone wall. The natives described the
hole as bottomless. In times gone by the
dead, wrapped in special mats, were sunk
in the chasm, and eventually eaten by
crabs and fishes. At a later period the
same people adopted slow roasting as the
best means of disnosing of the cadaver.
Shallow trenches filled to the brim with
glowing husk and t’uri wood surrounded
the bier on which lay the corpse. The
women, dishevelled, ash- and dirt-be-
daubed, squatted around the trenches,
giving vent to hair-raising laments, and
grabbing up and scattering glowing em-
bers in the air, to fall on and scar their
nude bodies. Nowadays those people
bury their dead a la white men, in a
rough wooden coffin (if the local trader
can be beguiled into giving the boards
on credit), or wrapped in mats. Owing
to the water being reached at a very shal-
low depth all. through the atolls, usually
only a dozen inches of sand covers the
departed. There are generally native wells
near by. The handiest disposal I have
seen, and came near sampling myself,
was at the vellow fever hospital (Hos-
pital del Isolamento) in Santos, Brazil,
where a large pit, partly filled with
quicklime, received and neatly got rid of
the very frequent corpses handed in every
day when Yellow Jack was busv.
“Flinderino”: Hoes anybody know the
weight of the largest clam-shell gathered on the
Australian shore? Whilst on the Barrier Reef some
time ago a blackfellow offered me for a shilling the
biggest one I’ve ever seen. The bivalve, which was
embedded in the coral, must have weighed close on
half a ton. The nigger claimed ownership, as he saw
it first. I declined the offer, as I hadn’t a steam
winch on me at the time. Director Le Souef, of the
Melbourne Zoo, had a pair of shells which weighed
4cwt. Mariner Sykes, an ancient Queensland pilot,
found a brace on the Endeavor Reef, which he esti-
mated would weigh about 7cwt. with the fish inside.
This giant was afterwards collected by some bSche-
de-mer fishermen, and eventually found its way to
Sydney, and was placed outside the doors of the
Museum.

BILL WAYBACK (after studying the bill of fare with interest): "Ere, bring me all wol's
on 'ere, an a piece o' bread."
“Yarrum” : For its inches, there is not a
gamer bird living than the willy wagtail. Once, at
the Patterson River (N.S.W.), I came upon a pretty
little nest with two young Willies in it. The birds
were almost full-fledged and could fly a very little.
It was evident, from feathers and hair lying around,
that they had both had their first flutter, accom-
panied by the parents. After a little while, one of
the youngsters, reaching for a peep at me, fell out
of the nest, and fluttered feebly out over the widish
river. It dropped into the water, out near the
middle, and would have been drowned in a short
time. I had my collar and tie unshipped preparing
to swim out; but just here the old birds came to the
rescue. Kicking up an awful bobbery, they flew out,
settled down in the wet, one on each side of young
Bill, and towed and pushed him ashore. Then they
preened and dried the little one’s plumage, and
helped it up to the nest. There was great rejoicing
in the camp, in which the other little William joined.
The feat struck me as all the more remarkable, be-
cause the willy wagtail, although fond of a dip
(which it mostly has from a bending branch, bob-
bing in and out of the water), never by any chance
flops bodily into the moisture.

“Old Fritz”: “Ginger” has seen his first
blackfellow, found him circumcised, and remarks:
“This is the only part of Australia where the ancient
rite is practised.” “Ginger” has been prospecting on
a very old field, for not only circumcision, but in-
cision (Sturt’s “terrible rite”) is a custom of all the
Australian tribes that have not fallen wholly under
the influences of the white man’s “civilisation.”
“Silas” : Hawkins was an assisted immi-
grant with a patch of land at Ibbooby (Vic.). When
he had been on the place a month, he was still sit-
ting on a stump, looking fixedly at the holding,
trying to make up his mind where to start first.
Cardon, the rabbit inspector, found him surveying
the land, and gave him a friendly hint about doing
his share towards knocking out Brer Bunny. Carden
scattered a glance over the same locality a month
later, and found evidences that the plague was mak-
ing marked progress. Hawkins was still sitting on
a stump, thinking that perhaps it would be best to
start clearing in the nor’-east corner. The inspector
reproached him: “You know, you’ll have to do
something to clear out these confounded rabbits,”
he said. “All the people around here are complain-
ing, and your block seems a regular breeding place.
Get to ’em with something, right away.” Hawkins
was hurt. “Get to ’em!” he whined. “Why, blime,
ain’t I bin sprinklin’ insecterbane all the mornin’ ?”
“Hisiu”: Papua contains 7,765,342 spi-
ders. Of all shapes and sizes, their variegated
pigments would rival the show on Flemington lawn
on Cup Day. Some make their homes in the scrub
wood, others in the thatch of native houses, others
in the banks of the creeks. The
home of one variety is made of
clay, something like the trap-
door. spider’s of N.S.W. and
Queensland. But the residences of
the Papuan cousins are without
doors. The bite of a spider which
makes its home in the palm
thatching of huts is greatly feared
by natives, and is often fatal.
This spider is quite a little fellow,
with black legs and a scarlet body.
Another variety, to be met with
in the mountains, resembles a
land crab in size. Its color is
black, and its legs and body are
clothed with fur. In the Mekeo
district (inland from Yule Island)
a large spider weaves a very
strong web, with which the
natives make fishing nets. This is
done by tying together the ends
of a forked branch of a tree, so as
to form a frame similar in shape
to a tennis racquet, though larger. The web is then
wound around the frame, and Brother Brown gets
busy—or as busy as he ever likes to get.
“Peva” : The Papuan doesn’t want much,
and the cocoanut tree provides most of it. The milk
gives him a cool and refreshing drink. The nut is
food. It contains a fair percentage of oil, which he
uses as a medicine. When mixed with ochre and
other pigments it furnishes him with paint for his
personal adornment, and for decorating his shields,
spears, canoes, gods, etc. He extracts the oil in
a simple way. The nut is scraped, and the scrapings
are wrapped in a piece of fibrous bark (taken from
that part of the cocoanut tree where the branches
shoot from the stem) and squeezed. With the
shell of the nut he makes cooking pots, spoons, etc.
The rind he uses for fuel. And last, but not by any
means the least important, in a country where the
rainfall is very heavy, the branches provide him with
a first-class thatching for his house.
PURE ENGLISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the closing of some of the higher schools for
the Christmas vacations, a plea was made for the
use of pure English. —News item.
Myself, I’ve often thought of this ;
I have, in fact, been very sad
To hear young Bill or little Cis
Use English which their poor old dad
Can only class as hoodlum cheek—
It knocks me bandy, so to speak.
I’ve known the wretched kids come home,
And jaw about their pals at school—
How one was ratty in the dome,
Or dillv, or a balmy fool;
And, if I chide, they set up lip—
It sometimes gives a man the pip.
I used to go and watch their play,
Forgetting thus my unpaid bills ;
But never since that dreadful day
They said, “Oh, nit; here comes his Jills—
Let’s do a duck across the park,
And blow him out—the bald old nark!”
These are the sort of gags that hurt,
So, on the fly, I caught young Bill,
And said a piece. “It is a cert.,”
I chinned to him, “you’ll get your fill
Of leather dope, my wayward son—
Why, blime, boy, you take the bun.
“What sort of lurk is this you’re at?
Where do you learn this slang-stunt coarse?
I’ll whack your spine-bone through your hat
If you don’t cut it out, old horse.
Leave such to pugs and vulgar coves—
Pure English, lad, your birth behoves.
“When I was young, in College years,
My old man lobbed round every day,
And pitched a tale into my ears,
Much as I’m pitching you to-day.
He’d say, ‘Bog into Shakspeare, Syd ;
He has the English style, no kid.’
“But, nowadays, old Shak. seems down
And out, as far as I can pick,
And purity of speech done brown—
No wonder teaching-blokes get sick.
You’ll find you’re up against it, Bill,
If you don’t chuck this stable frill.
“It is so low to use such chat—
Think how it jars your ma and me.
This is the last, yes, strike me fat,
The last time I will stand it—-see?
Henceforth, if slang you don’t eschew,
I’ll dust the liver out of you!”
N.S.W.

“Leichhardt” : This is in answer to “Mimo-
sa” (B. 5/12/T2). A carpenter I know set out to
ride from Eddington to Fort Constantine, 80 miles.
Being a poor bushman, he missed his road, and con-
tinued his journey till he sighted Cloncurry (Q.).
He had then done 76 miles, but turned back till he
hit the turn-off to “the Fort,” where he arrived in
the twilight, having covered 114 miles. He was back
at Eddington next day long before sundown, making
194 miles in two days. Same individual rode regu-
larly every Saturday afternoon for weeks a distance
of 48 miles, sometimes lengthening the trip six miles
by humanely taking his horse round to an off-side
pub for a long beer. On Christmas Eve, four years
ago, I myself did 82 miles on a 14-year-old mare,
followed by a dog the whole distance. This isn’t
much of a record for the horse, but, as the temuera-
ture around Cloncurrv romps up about 110° in
December, it was a fair-up for the dog.
CHRISTMAS AT THE MULGA CAME
“K. O. Gill”; If Australia was suddenly
confronted with the full extent of the damage and
suffering to animals caused by the barb-wire habit
it would be considerably astonished. Moving round
the North Coast (N.S.W.) recently, I found the same
old traces of the curse that I have become accus-
tomed to. There is hardly a horse that has not one
or more scars caused by the pronged wire, and, asking
the old question, I learned that numbers of valuable
animals have been so badly injured as to neces-
sitate destruction. If a barb-wire fence was of
any more use than four plain wires strained tight, I
might begin to understand its popularity, but it
isn’t. Also, it is not so cheap—a piece of news
which will probably make some fool people brush up
their thinking apparatus.
"Pick”: When the wind-endurance trial
is being run, Tilba Tilba, at the foot of Mount
Dromedary (N.S.W.), will be somewhere about.
Ancient Binghi evidently thought so too, for Tilba
means windy, and the double name indicates that
the tribe’s head meteorologist considered the region
extra special in the way of howling gales. White
interlopers afterwards upheld the idea by attaching
the names “Hurricane Hill,” “Whistling Point,” and
“Stand-agin-it” to parts of the landscape. It is a
region of almost perpetual blow, wherein the only
thing the wind has never been known to shift is the
cow-cocky’s conscience.

“Snakespear” : A few years ago it was the
almost universal belief that the snake population of
Papua was very small. This, like many other be-
liefs about Papua, has since been exploded. The
Possession’s snake families are, in fact, fairly
numerous, and their re-
semblance to well-known
Australian crawlers has
earned for them such
names as the black,
brown, green, diamond,
ring and carpet. Some
are very venomous, not-
ably the brown snake,
which is met with fre-
quently in the coastal dis-
tricts. The death adder,
too, is pretty common in
the hill country. I have
killed a few. I know of
two instances where dogs
bitten by them succumbed
within 30 minutes. The
largest snake is the New
Guinea python. The
markings on its skin are
somewhat similar to those
of the Australian carpet
snake, but the skin itself
is much darker. It grows
to an enormous length.
Some time ago I saw the
dried skin of one which
was killed on one of the Northern goldfields. It
measured over 30ft. in length. I know of another
instance where one was killed and found to measure
over 20ft.
“J.G.” : Re “A.Y.A.’s” par (B. 5/12/T2).
Blacks are not immune from bee-stings, and, more-
over, they don’t compare with white men when it
comes to robbing a nest. Have seen white men in
N.S.W. and Queensland go out, and do a successful bit
of burglary with nothing to help them but a smok-
ing stick; whereas I remember once, when four abos.
on the Barambah settlement laid siege to a hive,
it took them an hour, after falling the tree,
before they were game to get to close quarters, and
they didn’t do it then until they had burnt about an
acre of herbage in order to cover their advance. Binghi
has no heart to endure bee or any other stings, and it
is only dire necessity which makes him, at any
time, crack Mr. Busy’s crib.
“Mimosa”: He was out on the station for
experience, and he wasn’t imported goods either.
We were on the road with sheep. The fence on one
side of the “break” for a couple of panels was not
too safe for the first night’s camp; so “Sydney” was
sent ahead and told to “brush up” the wire at the
back of the canvas, and have it finished slick, as the
sheep would be there in an hour. When we arrived
Sydney was still hard at work “brushing up” the
fence. But not with timber and small boughs. He
had a tin of polish he had ridden to the store for.
Says he to the boss: “She does not shine too good,
but that’s the rust. Next time she’ll look better.”
“Tiaki”: The younger Maori is beginning
to recognise that some of his race’s ancient cere-
monies, songs, etc., will go the way of the moa if
the remnants of the old generation are not made to
disgorge their store of knowledge. So a council has
been sitting to collect the lore. Great secrecy is
observed, and none but those Who receive permission
may view the records or be present when the evidence
is being taken. Some influential Henares are going
to build a large carved council-house at Carterton, in
the Wairarapa district, where the good work is to be
continued in the near future. One subject which
occupied the council’s attention was the ahikomau.
This was Henare’s ancient method of preserving fire.
First, a suitable bank was selected, a deep trench was
dug in it with the lower end down hill, and a log of
poporokaiwhiria (Iledycaria dentata) was laid in the
opening with its lower end resting on two boulders.
A fire was then lit round it, and, when well alight,
was buried in with earth. The old hands claim that
fire thus lit would be a glowing coal 10 and even 12
years after it was started. One or two of the com-
mittee started out to try the trick and prove the
old chaps to be tarradiddlers. After four months they
returned, and, on unearthing the log, found it to be
a glowing mass, just as the patriarchs had testified.
» * * *
“McNorth” : The Papuan uses his ears as
a parcels express. The lobe is pierced when its
owner is very young, and the opening is gradually
distended until in many cases the ear flaps oh the
shoulder. In the loop thus formed he hangs the
family cash-box and quantities of his household
goods, so that when he goes to the garden to see if
his wife is doing her best, there is not much left for
the servant girl to get away with. Apropos ears, it
has been said that the' Papuan has a good one for
music as well as for furniture removing; but this is
doubtful. Some years ago six members of an itin-
erant Salv’army troupe visited Samarai. They were
possessed of the usual cornet, trombone, drum, etc.
Before commencing the performance the “captain”
presented to the wide-eyed niggers a number of coins,
beads, etc., with the understanding that the presents
were to be handed up to the performer they reckoned
played the best music. The show started. The cor-
net blew his hardest, the trombone man nearly burst
a lung, while the drummer worked himself into a
frenzy. When peace once more reigned the Papuans
were told to give their presents to the best musician.
Like one man the crowd rushed forward and heaped
their gifts at the feet of the rouseabout who banged
the big drum.
* * * *
“Te Babarantiko” : In his book, “Papua,”
Administrator Murray, talking of the small regard
of the nigger for human life, tells how a Papuan
on trial for murder explained that he killed a man
because “he talked too much”; another declared he
had killed a man because he was “no good.” I can
bear witness to like happenings. On the evening of
May 5, 1911, a Gilbert Islander quietly got out of
his bunk in one of the labor barracks on Ocean
Island (each barrack holds about 100 men), went
over to another bunk occupied by one of the com-
pany’s native police, struck a match to make sure
of his man, drew a sheath knife and slit open the
said man’s stomach. He then coolly returned to his
own bunk. “He talked too much,” said the nigger
in his defence. “His chatter kept me awake every
evening.” I know of another case where a native
on Maiana Atoll killed a woman and a girl for no
other reason than that, in his opinion, they were
“no good, and not fit to live.”
“Marloo”: A Northern River sapling,
which has long been the pride of Nimbin, 20 miles
north-west of Lismore (N.S.W.), was felled the
other day. It was a pine, just over 7ft. in diameter
at the butt, and contained 13,661 ft. of timber.

“Te Babarantiko”: Practically all the
languages spoken by the brown people of the wide
Pacific, from Maoriland in the south to Hawaii, and
west as far as Java and the Malay Peninsula, have
the same inter-relation that most European languages
have. It is a far cry (some 2100 miles) from Maori-
land to the Line Islands, yet the Maori word for dog,
te kuri, is the Gilbert te kiri. The Gilbert Islanders
also, by the way, call a dog te kamea, but that is
perhaps modern, and a corruption of “Come here!”
Also they call the animal te kaukau, which seems to
have been inspired by the dog’s bark. (Primitive people
usually name things animate by the noise they make.)
There is no end to words available for comparison,
but I will only say (as these words have so often
been used in Maoriland pars.), that mate has exactly
the same meaning in the Gilberts as in M.L., and
that the Maori waliine and the Gilbertese te aine
(woman) is practically the same word when spoken.
It is a still farther cry from the Line Islands to the
middle and west of Malaysia, yet I have found similar
words in both localities absolutely identical in mean-
ing, though the languages thereabouts would be unin-
telligible to the people of the Central and Southern
Pacific. Samples: Malay, mata (eye); Gilbert
Islands, mata. Malay, ikan (fish) ; Gilbert Islands,
ika. Malay, rumah (house) ; Gilbert Islands, uma;
Javanese, umah. More particularly are the words for
numbers closely related throughout Oceania and
Malaysia. Islands several thousand miles apart have
many of the numbers, especially (and curiously) 2,
3,4, 6,6, 7 and 8 almost exactly, and in many cases
exactly, alike.
“Gouger” : One of the many peculiar cus-
toms of the Pascoe River (N.Q.) natives is that of
never speaking by word of mouth to a man to whom
they are related by marriage. I have often told one
of them to go and spear some fish, but he, for some
reason or other, not wanting to go, has passed on
the request by finger-talk to another native. The
fish duly arrived. But, if there are no marriage ties
about, the natives do the necessary yabber-yabber by
tongue and lips. These blacks live very well. The
bush is full of wild fruits, game of every sort, and
enormous yams. A favorite dish is the carpet
snake, which is very plentiful in most of the scrubs.
I have often shot a healthy-looking snake and
watched the boys seize it, and bite its stomach open
with their teeth, to satisfy themselves that the' rep-
tile was fat. That ascertained, a hole is scooped in
the sand, a good fire lighted in it, and a number of
flat stones heated. On one layer of these the snake
is coiled, then another layer of hot stones is spread
on top of him, the whole being covered completely
over with sand, and a fire lighted on top. It is a
beautifully-cooked snake that finally emerges from
the oven, skin, intestines, all intact. When served
up with yams, and especially if some wild plums are
handy, Menzies or the Australia couldn’t entice me
away.

“F.T.S.”: Do magpies breed every year?
Though protected fowl, and, as far as I know, not
preyed upon by anything, they do not seem to in-
crease very greatly. One old cock bird has for the
past two years put up at our homestead, but he has
never introduced a lady bird, and the population
question doesn’t worry him. Apparently the only
thing he takes an interest in is the counter lunch.
ABDOOLA’S FLIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Abdoola left his ship because ’tis plain as plain can
be
The brown man who is desert-bred shall never love
the sea.
He’d rather face the sand and sun, the hot sirocco's
breath,
Than live the life which is to him the life of living
death.
Abdoola left his ship because upon the shrieking
seas
No lean, grey camel ever yet went down upon its
knees;
Because athwart the salty rim no dust-cloud signi-
fied
The sprawling teams that ever on the Great Lone
Pathways hied.
The Captain eyed the Customs men with white, un-
spoken fear ;
For when a brown man flees his ship that flight
shall cost him dear.
He played the hunter for a week —a grim, resultless
game ;
For Allah sees that all his sons shall look and
speak the same.
Abdoola steers the hunchies now, and no one seems
to know
That he is other than the man who steered them
long ago.
He wears his crimson turban and his wide, white
pantaloons,
And smokes the pipe that Abdul smoked ’neath
countless midnight moons.
For caravans are sprawling things that do not
understand ;
The footprints that are made to-day to-morrow fills
with sand ;
The desert stars are sentinels who see but never
tell;
And Allah’s sons were ever taught to keep a secret
well.
The Captain paid the Treasury one hundred sea-
stained pounds;
The Customs men, keen-sensed and grim, still go
their restless rounds ;
The Border Guards report “No trace”—but are they
much to blame?
For Allah’s sons are as the sands, and, like the
sands, the same!

PERSONAL
ITEMS
W. S. Viner, who easily defeated Dr.
Lancaster for the chess championship of
Australia, is a farmer who works home with
father on land near Bellingen (N.S.W.).
Like J. L. Jacobsen, the ex-champion best
known to the last generation, Viner is a
comfortable, nerveless person with plenty
of fat on his bones. His probable challenger,
A. E. N. Wallace, a city man and a mathe-
matician, isn’t of that type.
J. W. SUTHERLAND ,
General Manager of the
Horseshoe (Kalgoorlie, W.A.).
Statistician Knibbs is still picking the
fruits of the last census. On April 3, 1911,
the Commonwealth owned three husbands
and 18 wives who had married at 14, eight
and 92 at 15, and 25 and 343 at 16. Of
the various ladies, five of the 18 had babies
within the customary period, 25 of the 92,
and 149 of the 343. At the other end of the
table there were three wives over 100, and
four between 95 and 100. Also, there were
110* husbands and 49 wives alive with
families of 21 and upwards. Apparently,
judging by these last figures, the rearing of
a sizeable family is less wearing on the part-
ner who fills the pot than on the one who
cooks it.

will be added to the retired list in England.
He only _ awaits his successor’s nomination
to hand in his gaiters.
Iluka, on the Clarence River (N.S.W.),
has put up something like a record. Until
the other day, when the license was trans-
ferred, the name of John Rush (brother of
Mick, the sculler of olden days) had been
over the door of the local pub for 39 years.
“A. 8.” : The party most surprised by the
sudden announcement that Deakin was
going to retire from politics was Deakin him-
self. His decision, he thought, was a secret
known only in the bosom of his famliy. This
sort of thing comes from living in a wire-
less age.
Old digger William Mitchell, another
Eureka Stockader, has just died at Long-
reach (Q.), aged' 75. We begin to under-
stand what a task the military really had to
face in attacking the determined million or
so of armed diggers who sought death rather
than yield principle.
Henry Howell, for 38 years connected with
the Howard Smith Co., during which time he
rose from wharfinger to director, died on Jan-
uary 8. He had retired from active service in
July last, and, at the end of his six months’
leave, the firm’s staff gave him a send-off last
Wednesday. The reporters were collecting
names for the usual valedictory par. when
news was received that the guest, on leaving
the gathering, had practically dropped dead,
the result of excitement working on a weak
heart.
Walter Murdoch, the man who missed the
professorship of English at the Melbourne
University because he was an Australian,
goes to a similar job at the new Perth Uni,
His going discloses the fact that, of late, he
has been helping to pour out giggle-stuff for
“The Passing Show,” a dreadfully-humorous
feature of Saturday’s Argus. A man is
known by the company he keeps, and it is
painful to discover that the apparently-
respectable Murdoch has been keeping com-
pany with a jiggety muse of low character
and vulgar manners. His new £BOO a year
should be sufficient to reform him.
Another old Sydney building with ancient
memories hanging to it has just been pulled
down. It was in Pitt-street, one door from
King-street. In old days it was a hive of
lawyers. Sam Terry built it. The corner,
now a pub, was then a Bible depQt. Thur-
low, M.L.C.. Mayor of Sydney, had his office
there; so, of course, did his partner, Stephen
Campbell Brown, also a prominent poli-
tician. Next door, Thomas Fisher had his
boot shop—the same humble tradesman who
provided Sydney University with its magni-
ficent Fisher Library. In a room attached
to his shop, George Robert Nichols kept his
law books; and there it was that James
Martin, the greatest Judge N. S. Wales has
ever had, “read” his law.
The late J. Quayle Wood, who died at
Milson’s Point, Sydney, last week, aged 63,
was born at Bumbaldry station, between
Cowra and Grenfell (N.S.W.U—a property
belonging to William Redfern Watt, who re-
presented Carcoar in the Leg. Assembly as
far back as 1859-62. Mr. Watt was a
nephew of Dr. Redfern, who came to Botany
Bay for his small share in the Mutiny at the
Nore in 1797. Mr. J. Butler Wood, still liv-
ing at 93, left Bumbaldry and settled at
Brundah Creek, nearer Grenfell, where he
and his sons went in for sheep farming. In
1881 the father retired, and two of the sons,
J.Q. and Philip, took the property over,
developed it, and then started to grow wheat
on shares. After G. H. Greene, they were
the first to do it in a big way. The property
was a while ago sold to a syndicate for sub-
division purposes.
“The Wild Colonial Pressman” :
With a fairly wide experience I reckon Alfred
Deakin one of the most difficult men to report
in Australasia. It is not so much his speed (although
he could pile it up when he liked) as his meander-
ings in parentheses and his devotion to unusual
words. Very often he was disappointed in reports
of his speeches, and was inclined to think impatiently
of shorthanders whose worst fault lay in following
him too closely in his picturesque wingings in the
heights of oratory. If Deakin was confronted with
some notes, portions of which have never been trans-
cribed, he dbe a surprised politician. The most ex-
citing moments in my experience of Deakin were at
the close of a big speech in the Sydney Centenary
Hall. The press gang had the cable conveying the
news that it had been decided to send America’s
White Fleet to Australia, and fired it up to him. He
was surprised, but he surprised the packed audience
more when he let himself go. He swept them off
their balance with an explosion of metaphorical fire-
works. At the press tables we got only flashes of
it amidst the cheers and yells and general hallelujah.
It was a great performance, and I lost my hat and
my last tram.
The strenuous fight of the Freetrade
papers to put Cook into Deakin’s place as
leader of the crumbling Fusion recalls Joe’s
start in politics. Joe was a check-weigher
or something of the sort for the Lithgow
coal-miners when the first Labor party
arrived in the Ma State. The local P.L.L.
was mostly addicted to chapel-going prac-
tices, and the local Methody parson was
chosen as the Labor candidate. But he fell
sick, and Joe, as his most fluent “local
preacher,” naturally took the stipendiary
preacher’s electioneering pulpit. That first
lot of Laborites were mostly men from the
country, who felt a bit awkward in their new
surroundings (there weren’t any barristers
in the party in those days) ; so Joe, having
won the Lithgow or Hartley seat, and hav-
ing rather more education than the rest,
and being an experienced local preacher, was
given the job of leader.
Alexander Gray, who passed out on Janu-
ary 3, after 60 years’ work in Geelong, was
one of the men who made that much-libelled
place something of a manufacturing centre.
Full of Scotch grit, he established flour and
woollen mills (the latter as far back as
1869), fellmongeries and tanneries, besides
taking a hand in a ropeworks. One way
and another he amassed considerable dirt
and other wealth, but distributed a lot of
it in the best manner—without advertise-
ment.
