“Billy B.”: Deakin suffers greatly from
lack of digestive powers. That’s his trouble
just as it was ex-Governor-General Hope-
toun’s. This is how he put it to John See
once : “You’d think I was a very fortunate
man. I've got money, estates, position, chil-
dren of my own —everything in that respect
has been good to me.” “Yes, my lord,” says
the attentive See. “But,” dammit,” cries
Hopetoun, snapping down the blind as it
were, “I’ve got no stomach.” At least, he
didn’t exactly say “stomach.”
“Old John” Robertson, one of
the two most picturesque figures
in the politics of N. S. Wales, had
plenty of chances to make monej
while he was in office; but he
scorned every suggestion of the
sort. Out of one proposed deal
alone he could have made £20,000
or £30,000 for himself; but he
sprang like a wolf upon a man
who hinted that such a thing might
be done, and gave the public the
benefit of the opportunity. Natur-
ally he died dog-poor, and left his
family in rather a bad way. One
of the daughters married, and
struck the awful misfortune of
having five children born deaf and
dumb. They have been able to do
a little for themselves, but not
much, and their mother has had to
always help them along; but now
the old lady is 70, and the struggle
has become well nigh insupport-
able. A fund has therefore been
started by people who want to do
something for the afflicted grand-
children of an unselfish man who
did much for N. S. Wales; and
contributions can be sent to T.
H. Nesbitt, the Towm Clerk of Sydney, at
the Town Hall; he is one of the joint sec-
retaries.
There are some strenuous old journalists
in Maoriland, and T. W. Leys, editor of the
Auckland Star (daily), is notable. The
other day he celebrated his 50th year in
journalism. Born in England 62 years ago,
he was taken to M.L. by his parents in boy-
hood and almost immediately began to learn
the mechanical side of a newspaper on the
old Southern Cross. After three years, he
found his way into the writing dept, and
gradually worked his way to the sub-editor’s
chair. He relinquished it to do similar duty
on the Star when that paner was founded,
and in 1876 became its editor. He has been
at the same job ever since, and shows no sign
of loosening up a very sturdy grip
of things. Part of his grip is on the
proprietorship, and that helps to find some of
the wealth he occasionally distributes when
a good cause needs oiling. When Ballance
was Premier, Leys was offered a seat in the
Legislative Council, but, though he is a con-
firmed politician, he prefers to make hi*
moves out of sight, and declined the offer.
RICHARD
HAMILTON ,

There is likely to be a “temperance” rally
in Victoria, particularly in the R.C. Diocese
of Melbourne, when the new Coadjutor-
Archbishop arrives from Maynooth in a
month or so. Already there is a pretty
strong no-drink party among the clergy of
the Archdiocese, and Dr. Mannix, as a
pledged water-man, will naturally put him-
self at the head of it.
A VISION OF STRENGTH AND BEAUTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“How did Mr. McGowen look in his Court dress?”
“Just splendid. He’s so big and strong. 1 wish
he would wear it oftener. Lord Chelmsford thinks
he looks so well in it.”
■ —Sunday paper interview with Mrs. McGowen.
Yes, Life is a drab proceeding
Through a vale that is rough and dim ;
Some color and joy it’s needing;
We could do with a sight of Jim.
We are hungering—yea, we’re thirsting
To set eyes on his gold-laced coat,
And his silken stockings bursting
With the calves that are now remote.
Most men in tubular trousers
Like elephant’s legs go past;
Adonis, with lean-shanked Wowsers,
Is mislaid in the pageant vast;
But bravely in satin breeches
Lo 1 a Vision of Strength goes by,
With faith in the straining stitches
And pride in his conquering eye.
Packed muscles like mountain boulders
’Neath the beautiful broadcloth slip,
When he swings his mighty shoulders
And the tempered blade at his hip.
Oh, nothing can stop or stay him —•
There is nothing better on view^
Till Cohen crawls up to slay him
With his elegant I O U!
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Commonwealth Bank took off its coat
on Monday morning and started the savings-
bank business throughout the Commonwealth. The
ordinary banking business will follow next Monday.
There will be 1891 savings-bank agencies, nearly all
of them at post offices ; but for the present the Com-
monwealth Bank will have to depend on private
banks to do the other part of its business outside the
capitals. The “Wales” will be the general agent; in
Queensland the Q.N. will have the collecting agency,
in S. Aus. the Bank of Adelaide, in W.A. the Western
Australian Bank, and in Tasmania the local Com-
mercial. The Savings Bank is offering 3 p.c. up to
£3OO. For fixed deposits the ordinary branch offers
2 p.c. for six months, 3 p.c. for 12 months, and Si
p.c. for two years. It belies a rumor and makes no
offer for current account money; but it makes this
concession—it charges nothing for keeping a current
account unless the account falls below £5O. If that
limit is passed it at once charges the usual ss. In
the official circular nothing is said about the charge
for accounts that are not in credit. An account
£5OOO in debit might be —and in ordinary circum-
stances would be —vastly more profitable than one
which hovered between £5O and £lOO in credit;
vet, apparently, a charge of ss. is to be made on all
debit accounts. No doubt the difficulty would be to
discriminate between the bad debit accounts and the
good ones. Ordinary banking business of every sort
is to be undertaken—including, of course, advances
on all the usual banking securities.
Tasmania is the onlv State which has accepted the
Commonwealth’s offer for the taking-over of the ex-
isting savings banks. The scheme accepted by Tas-
mania (and still open to the other States) is some-
thing like this. The Commonwealth Bank will take
over all assets and all liabilities. The assets consist
mostly of the State’s 1 O U’s. The States may get
those I O U’s back on handing over their equivalent
in new debentures, secured as usual on the general
assets of the States. The Commonwealth offers to
accept 3J p.c. debentures at 95 in place of I O U’s
carrying 3| p.c. and 4 p.c. debentures at par for
those carrying more than 34 p.c. ; debentures to run
from 20 to 40 years. A 1 p.c. sinking fund is to be
also provided, to be used in reduction of the deben-
tures issued. That money is, however, to be lent to
the States at current rates if it is not required for
the repayment of depositors. This is carrying things
to an extreme in the struggle to propitiate the can-
tankerous States. It is obvious that it isn’t going to
be a real sinking fund at all. The thing works out
this way. The State has borrowed monev from the
people through the State Savings Bank. " The Com-
monwealth undertakes to pay back the people. As
security, the State which has borrowed the money
gives the Commonwealth certain debentures. The
S back 1 p.a., which the Common-
wealth may use to pay the people whose money it is.
But if the people are not waiting on the doorstep for
the money, the States take the money again. The
people may come in in a flock in a few months, and
the Commonwealth must pay them on the nail ; but
no matter how much it has to pay it can’t call on
the States for any money till the next 1 p.c. is due.
If they weren’t quite mad, and if there was no other
lion on the track, the States would accept that offer
But their trouble is not about the monev they have
borrowed, but about the monev they hope to borrow
If the Commonwealth controls the Savings Banksj
their dread is that they won’t be able to dip into the
till, collar the people’s savings, and put them into
the good old “permanent and reproductive works” to
quite the same extent as they have been doing lately.

Though still the Benjamin among Australian banks,
this little one is growing. For a good many years
after it moved down from North Queensland, it got
along with a paid capital of £lOO,OOO. That wasn’t
very much ; but, then, 15 years ago its deposits were
only £182,800. To-day they amount to £906,214,
and bills, etc., bring the total liabilities up to
£984,825. In view of the steady yet highly satis-
factory growth of the business, it was decided last
year to get in another £62,500 of capital The
bank was then in 50,000 shares of £7, paid to
£2. A call of £1 ss. per share was made, payable
by instalments. Though at the end of the half-year
only £31,250 had fallen due, £51,437 had been
paid—a pretty good indication of the shareholders’
confidence in their bank. This increase of capital
explains the jump in the dividend figures for the six
months. Some of the new capital could hardly have
been put to profitable use straight away; hence,
naturally, the profit, though easily a record, does
not look so well when calculated on canital and
reserves combined. This shows what these funds
have earned of late: —-
June, 1908 £6 0 10 p.c. Dec., 1910 £8 0 2 p.c.
Dec., 1908 612 1 „ June, 1911 717 9,,
June, 1909 7 3 7 „ Dec., 1911 817 9 ,
Dec., 1909 712 5 „ June, 1912 916 7 „
June, 1910 712 5 „ Dec., 1912 811 2 „
In the circumstances £8 11s. 2d. is remarkably
good, because the capital is taken as at the end of
the half-year, whereas an unknown quantity of the
new capital was paid in late in the term, and none
of it was available right through the term. Things
were bad with the North Queensland once; but the
country recovered quickly, dividends were kept very
moderate, and a lot of hard writing-down was done.
It is probable that, having got into the writing-down
habit, the management is still dropping a bit into
the hidden stocking whenever an unexpected stroke
of good fortune comes along. Judged by the interest
return, there can’t be anything wrong with the assets
nowadays.
The next table shows how things have moved in
12 months and 10 years:—
Liabilities.
1902. 1911. 1912.
Deposits .. .. £261,541 £912,967 £906,214
Bills, etc 56,694 51,564 78,610
Assets.
Liquid assets .. 108,559 396,285 423,972
Advances . . .. 305,913 634,349 692,596
Premises, etc. .. 20,258 60,451 53,500
D e p°sits have shrunk a trifle —money withdrawn
probably on account of the new capital, in which
case the bank’s resources are none the less. Anyway,
trom shareholders and the public it had £1,064,530
months ago, and now it has £1,146,261. The
public s money in the business is now equal to about
£o 10s. for every £1 of the shareholders’ visible
funds; and that is well within the safety limit. The
cash and liquid assets represent 43 p.c. of the lia-
bilities, and that is a good way inside the safety
line, too. It means that the bank could, even on its
present resources, take a bit more advance business
if the right securities were offering. Like most other
banks it has no doubt been going very slow in the
sugar districts, where it was born, the sugar in-
dustry has been, and ought to be, highly profitable;
but when it comes to the future —the thing which
the bank has to consider when it is a matter of
helping a farmer or would-be farmer with some
cash—the uncertainty of the last two or three vears
has been paralysing. Banks, like most other busi-
ness concerns, don’t care much what conditions are
imposed on them, or on the industries on which they
depend, so long as they know' what those condi-
tions are going to be, and can figure accordingly
for a few years ahead. When they can’t figure ahead,
it means that they either turn the business down or
charge a stiff price to cover unknown risks. This
is what has been happening in connection with the
sugar industry; and it is particularly rough on the
little man, the struggler, the starter, who has per-
force to lean up against a financial prop or keep out
of the business altogether.

That gives some idea of how Australia and Maoriland
can pull themselves together. There were few cos.
that got into worse trouble in the crash that followed
the borrow-and-boom bubble of the ’Bo’s and early
’9o’s. So much of the often-doubtful blessing of
foreign capital was poured into the country that
values of squattages and farms —squattages especially
■ were inflated ridiculously by men who could borrow
too much and too easily. Then the owner of the
foreign money got uneasy and wanted it back; and
drought and rabbits came; and prices of wool, etc.,
fell; and on the overstocked country (overstocked in
the struggle to pay interest on inflated values) the
losses were terrible. Of this co.’s capital £1,042,142,
or about four-fifths, had to be written off as lost;
even the debenture-holders were called on to forego
£1,448,325.
In the natural order of things the debenture-holders
would simply have taken possession. But probably
they were not organised; anyway, they concluded
that the men who had led them into the soup were
the best qualified to lead them out again. So the
shareholders, after writing off the beforementioned
£1,042,142 were permitted to retain a paid interest
amounting to £135,000 or thereabouts in the con-
cern. But they were tied up in various ways, and
the payment of divs. was next-door to impossible.
When, therefore, it began to look as though very
decent profits might be made presently, a compromise
was sanctioned. Part of the debenture debt was
represented by a third mortgage, and the poor
beggars who held that stock had a ghastly time,
as the top lines of the table show. It was therefore
agreed between all parties in 1903 that, if conditions
were made such that the shareholders could get divi-
dends, the third debenture-holders might take as much
in excess of their then maximum of 44 p.c. as the
shareholders got in excess of 5 p.c. That is how it
comes about that in the last few years the third
debentures have had 7 p.c., while the shareholders
have had 74 p.c.
Notwithstanding these just-mentioned payments,
the profits and recoveries have been so great, while the
country has been going to the dogs on account of
Labor rule, that in the past three years £265,012 has
been added to reserves. In such circumstances there
was naturally a call for a rearrangement of interests.
And on the part of the shareholders the call was all
the shriekier because, while their shares were only
10s. paid, there Was a liability of £8 10s. on them.
The concern was obviously lop-sided or top-heavy—
£l3s,349 of capital owned by the shareholders who,
within certain limits, bossed the show;
£3,296,337 owned by debenture-holders who
played second (or third or fourth) fiddle. Hence
during the past few months a scheme of reconstruc-
tion has been incubating, and the last mail or two
brought word that it had got the sanction of the
Court. The details are not clear in every respect,
but this is the gist of it:—•
(1) First debentures ( £2,350,000) and second de-
bentures ( £540,764) ; total £2,890,764 carrying
4 p.c.—These are to be turned into £1,000,000 of
first and £1,000,000 of second mortgage debentures.
The balance ( £890,764) is to be paid off out of
share capital.
(2) Third debentures ( £405,573). —These are
worth 7 p.c. p.a. at present, as mentioned above. In
place of debentures pref. shares are to be issued,
£175 worth for every £lOO of present debentures.
This will mean in all £709,752.
(3) Shares. —The share capital is £135,349. Of
the shares 208,942 are £9, paid to 10s.; 60,000
are 10s. fully paid. The £9 shares will be reduced
to £5 10s., and the unpaid £5 will be called up.
This will bring in over £1,000,000. Part of that
money will be used to pay off £890,764 of the first
debentures. But, apparently, some of the ordinarv
share capital is to be turned into pref. capital. Any-
way, as explained to the Court, the pref. capital and
the ordinary capital are to be £1,000,000 each. The
old and th° new thus work out as follows:

There is a discrepancy of £110,792 there, £724 of
which is represented by capital paid in advance of
calls. Perhaps the full amount of the new pref. shares
won’t be issued. Also, the interest payable on the pref.
shares isn’t anywhere mentioned in the reports
available. Presumably the premium payable on the
third debentures (75 p.c.) comes out of the re-
serves. Part of the assets of the co. consists of all
the shares in the N.Z. Land Association. This
concern is to be transferred bodily to the recon-
structed N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., and
the Association’s nice little reserves will of course
swell the parent co.’s.
Getting back to the past year’s work and figures,
this is how the latter compare with those of a
couple of previous years:—

Properties and stock have thus been reduced to a
comparative trifle. Certainly that is good; but it
means that the big profits made during the past
few years out of the sale of written-down properties
can’t be repeated. The money realised, as well as
the accumulated profits, have gone into advances;
and there is still a lot of work to be done in that
way—the work of financing rural industries.

Thus for the past half-year £11,250 is paid away
in divs., £10,560 is put by, and £1267 is written
off against depreciation of plant. Nothing has lately been
written off freehold premises; the explanation being,
no doubt, that the unearned increment of the land
has more than counterbalanced the depreciation of
the buildings. Even so, it is doubtful whether some-
thing should not be written off. Buildings are worn
out in the process of earning a co.’s profits, and
leave to be replaced; and when a co. sets about
the replacement it finds that for all practical pur-
poses the increase in the value of the land has
made no difference —so long, that is, as it uses that
land for the purposes only of its business. In other
words, it can’t pay the building contractor with the
increment unless it sells part or all of the land.
However, the Herald Co. could make a very liberal
allowance for depreciation of premises and still
show astonishingly good results. When it took
over the two papers it paid no less than £198,000
for the goodwill. Thus, of the £250,000 of capital,
only £52,000.was represented by anything visible to
any eye except that of faith. During the past
eight years £87,185 of the intangible asset has
been replaced by very tangible ones. The change
in the position in the past six years is perhaps shown
clearer still by this comparison:—

Thus in six years the co., besides paying its divi-
dend, has put £28,133 into good city freehold, and
has laid by £41,784 in hard cash or gilt-edged
securities—mostly hard cash, on fixed deposit. It
has still some distance to travel before it can show
20s. per £1 share in hard assets; but the share-
holders are probably not anticipating liquidation
and a division of assets just yet.

This society has just had its 50th birthday, and,
talking over its history, it remarks: “Up to
the present not a bad debt has been in-
curred.” That is a remarkable record, and probably
accounts for the directors’ apparent contempt for
such a thing as reserves. Nevertheless, the manage-
ment might well remember something about a well
and an old familiar pitcher that was broken at last.
No building society ever ought to have a bad debt,
of course; but things don’t always happen as they
ought to. The salvation of the society hitherto has
been that there has never—in modern times, any-
way—been a bad, sudden slump in Perth. Yet in
the early days of the society it was once so very sick
that the Government was implored to take the busi-
ness over and run it in conjunction with the Savings
Bank; and it was only because the Government
wouldn’t or couldn’t “arrange the requisite loan to
enable the society to be dissolved” that the doors
were kept open. The table that comes next shows-
the amount at present outstanding and a few other
things:—
1900. 1905. 1910. 1911. 1912.
Share capi-
tal . . £18,737 £30,921 £34,483 £34,597 £39,102
Fixed de-
posits, &c. 11,442 8,061 9,336 3,664 7,578
Advances 30,315 39,932 40,910 36.598 47,262
Other assets 980 1,439 5,299 3,873 1,860
The advances made during the past year have thus
been unusually large. The cash actuallv paid out to
borrowers has been £24,306, all or nearly all, no
doubt, on new securities. If anything serious hap-
pened to Perth, and there was a bad drop in values,
the society would thus be in great danger of having
a lot of securities thrown on its hands; and, since
its reserves are so preposterously small, the capital
would be in danger.
While there has been no bad accident, the share-
holders have been doing extraordinarily well. Four
shillings a week paid for eight years ( £B3 Bs.) buys a
£lOO share through the accumulation of the money
at. 5 p.c. The bonus has been additional ; during the
past year the shares which have matured have drawn
£l4O 16s. 9d. This shows the record for a few
vears past:—

It illustrates the amazing power of compound inter-
est ; for neither interest nor bonus is payable till
the maturity of the share. Still, even compound
interest couldn’t do it unless the investment in Perth
mortgages brought in a very fine return. As things
settle down in the West the tendency of that return
will, however, probably be downward.

Kodak (Australasia), Ltd., formerly
Australian Kodak, Ltd., which began as from June,
1908, with a small deficit representing preliminary
expenses:—

This co. was formed to take over the photo-supply
business run by Baker and Rouse, and Baker and
Rouse Prop., Ltd., with an interest in the Eastman
Kodak Co., U.S.A. It was a good, clean flotation, and
the accounts have throughout retained their good,
clean appearance. Thus the first line on the assets side
of the balance-sheet runs this way: “Goodwill, trade
marks, trade names and processes, Nil. (No value
has been placed on these or anything other than
tangible assets.)” Moreover, the reserves—that is to
say., accumulated profits—apparently include nothing
in the shape of a depreciation allowance for actual
wear and tear, that allowance having throughout been
written right off. Even the buildings are written
down, though most of them are new: 2 p.c. of their
cost has been struck off during the year. This indi-
cates very prudent accountancy, and suggests that the
reserves are very real. Probably the co. has in tan-
gible assets a good deal more than £39,781 in ex-
cess of its capital. It used to have nearly £28,000
in Eastman Kodak shares, and still has £15,038 (at
cost). Numerous sales of the stock have been made,
and in 1910 and 1911 “profit on sale of investments”
—evidently this Eastman investment —amounted to
£7402. The £15,038, therefore, probably means a
present value of another £lO,OOO. Certainly mer-
chandise stocks represent £90,203 of the total
£228,649 of assets, and it is impossible to check
that big item ; but if you can judge what you can’t
see by what you can see, things are all right. The
next biggest item in the accounts is freehold pro-
perty, £59,902, on which there is a plaster of
£10,000; the latter and current trade debts
( £21,367) represent all the liabilities. The ac-
counts altogether leave a very nleasant taste in the
mouth.
The Murrumbidgee Co-operative Mill-
ing Co. (Wagga, N.S.W.) :
Written
Nov., Profits. Dividends. Off. Reserves.
1909 .. - £5,058
1910 .. £1,890 5 p.e.= £l5OO 5,448
1911 .. 7,145 6 „,, 1800 £lOOO 9,793
1912 . . 10,293 9 ~ „ 2700 17,386
In 1909, the co.’s 21st year, a dividend of 7-i p.c.
was paid, along with a bonus of 12s. 6d. per £1
share. At the same time a fresh issue of shares was
made, absorbing the 12s. 6d. In other words, the
capital was increased from £18,598 to £30,000 by
the cheerful process of capitalising reserves. Ap-
parently there had been previous transfers, as the
capital to start with was under £lO,OOO. The past
year’s profit, calculated on even the £30,000 basis,
gives a very gaudy . return. But it is doubtful
whether anything like it can be made this year. The
co., like most in the wheat-handling and milling
business, is a speculator. It bought grain heavilv,
and the directors had to find just on £60,000 to
finance things. The market rose, and the co. un-
loaded at a big profit. As at November 30 its stocks
were low—just as well, for the market has been
easier since. If the management believes that there
is an improvement ahead, it has no doubt been buy-
ing heavily again. And that’s where the gamble
comes in ; for no man can say definitely what the
market is going to be. It looks as though there
should be a recovery after the first rush is over,
speaking generally. Thousands of farmers must sell
as soon as they have harvested in December and
January; thousands more prefer to sell rather than
go to the expense of storing. In January, 1912, mill-
ing wheat averaged 3s. 74d. per bushel in Sydney,
and crept up to 4s. 6jd. in October, 1912. Yet it
by no means always works out like that. In Decem-
ber, 1909, the average price in Sydney was 4s. Id.,
and in January, 1910, 4s. 3d. By June it was down
to 3s. 4d. Again in December, 1910, the price was
3s. Bd. ; by March, 1911, it was down to 3s. 3d.
These figures suggest how uncertain the trade is. If
the country miller is buying for a rise, he is all right,
if the rise happens; but if he holds off, expecting a
fall, he is handicapped, inasmuch as he may presently
be unable to get his requirements locally ; and that
means extra freight. However, he seems to mostly
do very well indeed —largely because he isn’t under
any illusions as to the nature of the business, and
doesn’t burst out into 50 p.c. dividends when he has
a fat year. He capitalises his reserves certainly;
but that doesn’t mean paying out money—at any
rate, not in a lump. In this case, notwithstanding
the capitalised reserves, there is a feeling that the
co. is over-trading, and the directors are considering
how they should set about increasing their capital
from £30,000 to £60,000. They may take the easy
course of capitalising more reserves; but it is to be
hoped they won’t do much in that way. A co. in this
business ought to have a strong contingency fund.
On past records shareholders can get a very good re-
turn on any moderate amount of capital they may
put into the business, and the contingency fund,
while earning money just as much as if it were capi-
tal, will protect the principal.

Last year Australia made a good show
as a producer of copper, though far too much of it
was sent away in the unrefined state, to provide work
for the foreigner. Mount Morgan was the big gun,
with 8818 tons refined at Kembla. Great Cobar sent
away 6654 tons of blister, more heavily charged with
silver and gold than in any previous year. Mount
Elliott did a brilliant run, producing nearly as much
jhe red metal as Great Cobar. To their disgrace
both of these concerns shipped their metal unrefined.
" allaroo and Moonta emphasise the improved mar-
kets by paying increased dividends. This co. is well
up to 6000 tons for the year. Mount Lvell, some-
what out of form, produced 3124 tons of fine copner
in the half-year to September 30, and looks like
giving the Great Cobar a close run for second place
when it gets into full work again.

Water and Sewerage Board, are now removing all
machinery to Melbourne works to manufacture similar
pipes for the new O’Shannessy water scheme for Mel-
bourne water supply, the cost of which will be over
£lOO,OOO. The work is to be completed within 12
months.*
Messrs. Babcock & Wilcox, Ltd., have received
the order for the supply of all the steam generating
plant and accessories for the electrification of the
Victorian Government Railways. The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.*

for an issue of £150,000 worth of 6 p.c. debentures;
this was in August, just after the accounts had
closed. At July 31 the co. owed £77,829. No
Profit and Loss account is published ; but manufac-
turing account shows a debit of £28,288, although
only half the administration expenses have been
charged to it. The outlay was £61,343; sales and
stocks represented £33,055, leaving a loss of £28,288.
“Stocks of oils, etc.,” are, by the way, taken in
“at estimated cost,” which is a poor way of doing
it. In a statement of assets and liabilities, share-
holders don’t want to know what the asset cost; they
want to know what it is worth. The Murrurundi de-
posits are not very large, but they are rich, and there
is no reason at all why, well worked, they should not
pay handsomely. It remains to be seen whether they
will be worked well. Some of the estimates of
revenue put forward hitherto have certainly been
ridiculous.
* * * *
Broken Hill exported minerals valued on
the spot at £4,186,200 last year, which is £1,086,091
more than the record for 1911. Dividends totalled
£1,529,417. The big figures are due to the boom in
metals; the increase in tonnage is comparatively
slight. Last year 747,077 tons of all classes of ore
and concentrates were railed away, an increase of
only 10,967 tons. During the 12 months 279,915
tons of lead concentrates, valued at £2,769,170, were
railed, an increase of 2952 tons ip weight and
£787,731 in value. Zinc concentrates weighed
433,054 tons, value £1,320,666, an increase of
11,023 tons and £273,646. It is significant that
there should have been such a small increase in ton-
nage, in spite of the vastly-increased expenditure
and the boom in metals. Assuming that the
279,915 tons of lead concentrates returned an average
of 60 units of lead, the Barrier produced 167,949
tons of that metal last year, irrespective of the few
thousands of tons to be recovered from zinc concen-
trates.
Bellinger, general manager of Great
Cobar, left Australia last week in pursuit of a holi-
day from the weary struggle to get and keep an
adequate staff of good miners, and also with an out-
nut that refuses to travel in the right direction.
Meanwhile Danvers Power, from Sydney, is in charge.
Last year was responsible for only' three decent
monthly outputs, and immediate prospects are not
encouraging, as the current quarter will be cut into
by holidays. It is reported that the Minerals Separa-
tion plant, on its trial run with the Chesney tailings,
proved an unqualified success, and that some wonder-
ful figures have been sent on to the directors in Lon-
don. Engineer Herzig, examining on behalf of
European interests, was favorably impressed with
Cobar affairs, and rumor has it that a deal is now
being made with the 15,000 reserve shares. Capital
at date of last report was £925,000 and debentures
£724,000 —an awesome array. By the way, the
time is over-ripe for something to be heard about the
accounts for the year ended last June. The current
year is more than half gone.
Cadia (N.S.W.), after a lot of spasmodic
but short-lived efforts, is now seriously on the trail
with a blast furnace that has supplemented the old
and wasteful small reverbs. As a result of a fair
amount of development work, there are respectable
reserves to keep the furnace going with 800 tons of
ore per week. The ore is not abundantly rich, is low
in sulphur and horribly high in alumina, with ten-
dencies towards producing pig-iron instead of copner.
The mining is a battle against weight of overburden,
as the deposit has only a small dip. Above it there
is iron ore, plentiful and of good grade, but this may
not be touched as filling. Into the bargain there is
the magnificent Canoblas Hill rising from the mine,
on the wav to Orange. Because of its iron deposits
Cadia made claim to a railway, but the claim has
not yet been sympathetically received.
* * “ * *
Occidental, outside Cobar, is having
trouble which, unlike the plant, is not of its own
making. The mine is closed down for want of rain.
An issue of more capital was recently made, funds
being needed to re-organise the newly-erected treat-
ment plant. Not that this has so soon got obsolete ;
but it never had a chance of doing good work with
its disappointing string of variegated units. The
mine appears to be all right, but better management
is needed. Dividends have sadly wilted since metal-
lurgist Chappie departed. An offer of eight to nine
shillings a share for the mine is said to have been re-
fused by the company. It was understood that it
came from a source in some way connected with the
late metallurgist.

Gw alia Consolidated (W.A.) passed its
accounts and agreed to a reconstruction scheme at a
meeting held in London on December 4. 8., M. and
Lo. out of the management.
For the year ended July last Kalgurli’s
report shows that the average return was 40s. 7d. per
ton. Since then it has been 395. The amount distri-
buted m dividends during the year exceeded the work-
mg profit by £32. As in the case of most of the
other honestly-worked mines at Kalgoorlie, costs
show a tendency to grow, the result of dearer water
and fuel and greater depth.
About a year ago North Kalgurli (W.A.)
down its manager’s suggestion to spend
£2OOO on deep development, and let tributors have
a go instead. The latter struck a few rich dabs ; but
as the average was not good enough, work ceased.
.Now the manager again urges the co. to do a bit on
its own account ; but with erratic shoots and uncer-
tain values staring it in the face, the Board is hesi-
tating.
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) claims to
have put years on to its life by finding two new
lodes, and a local paper declares that the mine is
looking better than it did two years ago. Something
is also said about a probable jump of a shilling in
the price of shares—which gives an impression that
the information is loaded. At present the co. is
treating the second-largest monthly tonnage on the
field, but aggregate profits are not likely to be suffi-
cient to allow much of a cut among shareholders.
Carr-Boyd has induced some Melbourne
people to find money to probe the Cosmo' Newbery
leases (W.A.). A Smythesdale (Vic.) expert is
reported to have visited the outlandish region, and
got encouraging results from samples. Though with-
out much hope, The Bulletin wishes old “Pot-
jostler” luck.
* * * *
Burbanks Main Lode (W.A.) met share-
holders in a cheery frame of mind on December 5,
when a number of “encouraging features” were re-
ferred to. Amongst these were increased ore re-
serves, new ore discovery at the No. 5 level and the
improved appearance of the main shoot at depth. A
gentle hint was, however, given shareholders that
they must not expect the increased returns of the
past few months to continue, as the output had only
been run up to pay for new machinery out of current
revenue. On the whole the mine is looking well, and
a prosperous career seems assured for some time to
come.
* * * *
Where there’s life there’s houe. In the
last 15 years considerably more than half a million
sterling has been extracted from quartz in Menzies’
Consolidated ; but owing to receipts being monoed
up in working expenses, pickings, etc., no money has
been available for dividends. The past year has,
however, witnessed an improvement in values at depth,
and at the No. 14 level the main shoot' has been
proved for a length of nearly 500 ft., and a width of
4ft. averages lOldwt. The management thinks
prospects are brighter than for years past, and there
is still a chance of shareholders getting something.
Having issued the whole of its capital, the
Board of Whim Well Copper Co. (W.A.) is thinking
out a new scheme. It reckons there is every justifica-
tion for making an increase, as “even then the pro-
perty will represent considerably more than the issued
capital.” The Bulletin suspected that something
like this would happen, and said so at the time the
co. was paying away in dividends money that it
should have held on to for equipment and develop-
ment.
The taking over by a London co. of Mount
Oxide is practically completed, the co.’s financial
man, Earle Hermann, having signed up with Edmund
Davis, of the very robust Consolidated Goldfields
group. The new co. will have a capital of
£475,000, of which the present co. receives
£200,000 in shares, and working capital of
£150,000 is provided. The Queensland Govern-
ment is ready to permit the co. to link itself up
with the railroad to Cuthbert. the co. building the
line and the Government providing the material and
giving very long credit at 5 p.c., while retaining
the right to resume the line at 5 p.c. ov.er construc-
tion cost. Latest developments at the mine are
good: values and widths increase as the level pro-
gresses north towards the iron mountain. Appar-
ently some poorer things are to burden Oxide’s
richness, as there is already talk of increasing the
capital sufficiently to take in the Cuthbert group
and the Dobbin, Crusader and other mines held bv
the very lazy Queensland Copper Freeholds Ltd. The
Dobbin, at any rate, has a shoot of ore so rich
that the Government should break the Copper Free-
holds Co.’s loafing habit.
North Australian Exploration Co.’s Mount
Lindsay mine is to be floated into a 150.000 share
co of 10s. each; 30,000 are in reserve. North Aus.
Exploration Co. gets 60,000 paid up, and provides
underwriting out of that; 60,000 are offered at
2s. Mount Lindsay is 35 miles from Cloncurry, and
the authorised Macgregor railway will bring it,
within five miles of the line. The mine has shipped
a lot of ore of consistent grade—between 20 and
25 p.c.—and J. H. Fawcett reports it a good thing.
* * * *
At Great Fitzroy (O.) expenditure and
revenue continue to run nearly a dead heat. Revenue
for December was £12,662 ; expenditure, £11.968.
Copper was taken at £BO 4s. 7d. per ton. The re-
turn was 132.76 tons copper, 5430 z. gold and 16090 z.
silver. Costs work out at practically £9O per ton
of copper. Concentration and high costs seem to be
inseparable. But with low-grade stuff what else can
be done? Even Mount Morgan is now talking about
concentration for some of its ore. For the month
5?11 tons were milled at Fitzroy for 979 tons of
concentrates, assaying 11.61 p.c. # copoer and B,7dwt.
e-old The mill recovered 2.1 units of copner per ton
of ore treated ; and’ some of the metal is probable
lost in the subsequent urocesses. With metal at £BO,
2 p.c. copper is worth 325. If the costs swal-
low 32s and a trifle over, as they do. the profit must
come from the gold ; and the separation of gold and
copper costs more than a bit.

* * * *
After providing for the December divi-
dend, St. Mungo, Cloncurrv, had a credit of £l7OO.
During the half-year, to November 30, the mine pro-
duced 761 tons, bulking 23 p.c. copper. Sales realised
£4006, and the ore at the mine and on the road to
market is valued at £3068 ; total, £7074. Expen-
diture at the mine took £2920, so the gross profit
was £4154—£5 9s. per ton of ore and over £23 per
ton of copper. Reserves are very limited, approxi-
mately 900 tons. The shaft is 150 ft. deep, and the
bottom is in hornblende schist, with intrusions of
granite. Further prospecting will be done by winz-
ing below No. 3 level, and the chance of picking up
another deposit of chalcopyrite appears fairly good,
There is £BOO on fixed deposit—not over-much for
a co. in 40,000 shares and a pretty heavy expenditure
staring it in the face.
Mills' United, Charters Towers, has failed
to respond to a very expensive course of development
in the deeps ; but the hope of finding something isn’t
dead, particularly since the Brilliant Deeps struck a
pay shoot at 2600 ft. The total return last year was
£57,752, from 20,341 tons. Shareholders got a six-
penny dividend ( £7500). New Brilliant Freehold
won £52,571 last year from 18,640 tons. It paid
£26,250, or 3s. 6d. per share, which was the largest
distribution on the field. East Mexican, once such a
favorite, crushed 4822 tons for £12,701, but was
unable to divide anything. It shelled out £33,962
m 1911. Marshall’s Queen crushed 5332 tons for
£14,346. Its latest return, 308 tons for an average
of £5 12s. 7d., is more encouraging. Next door the
New Queen Central reports a crushing of 173 tons for
an average of £l2 6s. 3d.
The New Year brings as much cold com-
fort to Walhalla (Vic.) as the Peace Congress in Lon-
don to the dreadful Turk. The Long Tunnel Board, hav-
mg given six months to the search underground, hasn’t
hit up anything worth while. So the word passes
round—“ Resources exhausted. What is to be done
next?” The directors plainly don’t like their job.
Being men with easy arm chairs and seats on the
Boards of well-protected, well-fed cos., they aren’t
the gritty stuff to stir the Walhalla viking out of
his coma. So they recommend that the co. be
wound up. This is banking the fires with a ven-
geance. To justify themselves they point out that
at 3375 ft. the reef is narrow and disturbed and the
average value is low. Nothing, to their mind, will
serve but to put up the shutters and sell off. If
Walhalla agrees to this the biggest gold producer of
Victoria goes into the lethal chamber with hardly a
mourner. To date 756,169 tons from the Long
Tunnel have given 813,0500 z. gold, and paid
£1,283,400 in divs. The old hands who believed
in Cohen’s Reef and in the Long Tunnel are of the
past; there is no one of the Pearson breed to ring in
supporters. What the Board might have done was
to have given the Hartrick policv a trial. Obviously
the deep ground isn’t going well; but why not test
some of the shallower region to the south? The
Bulletin believes that Klug, of 8., M. and Co., had
an idea that formations there ought to be tested;
but as some folk didn’t like Hartrick, the thing was
never done. And, with the cash all gone, the easy
opportunity has passed.
* * * *
New Chum Goldfields, Bendigo, recently
got on to a patch of plum pudding gold, and some
mining sneak passed the word round that a Christmas
dish was in stock. However, the manager, though
as rotund as Falstaff, got in promptly, to the
merriment of the Beehive, which well knows how
hard it is to catch that particular weasel asleep.
New Chum Goldfields —rich patch notwithstanding—
doesn’t make headway. The biggest work is at about
1100 ft., where the saddle shows 34ft. wide. Here
vields have worked out at about 3dwt. per ton. So
well have costs been watched that this return has
more than covered expenses. But shareholders in
these davs of high living want something more than
that. Their chance may come from nearer the sur-
face. But as Specimen Hill is draped in desolation
just now, it would take a mountain to move out-
siders to enthuse over prospects, or to bid up for
shares on mere talk.
New Langi Logan, Ararat, looks like hav-
ing a run, despite its 100,000 shares. If so, then
North Langi, Souths and Centrals may liven up after
six months of dreadful stagnation. New Langi has
the continuation of the Cathcart ground, as well as
two miles on the big Langi gutter. Mining to-day is
confined to the latter, but every foot of good wash
the Central Cathcart opens south-east adds to the
possibilities of the New Langi claim, lying, as it does,
next door. While Central is thus giving the office
for the Cathcart lead, New Langi itself is brushing
ahead with its level under the Langi lead Here
bore after bore has given such results as to establish
—unless bores lie, as they so often have done that
the wash in the gutter ought to pan out tip-top. It
is however, hard to forget the terrible experience ot
the Charlotte Plains, under a man as cautious as
Capron ; it frightens one off trying to spot deep-lead
winners on bore results. As at the Cathcart Central,
the wash must be entered before one can tell for cer-
tain This stage ought not to be distant at the
Langi, for the water there has been far below
croakers’ anticipations. Success at the New Langi
betokens the second advent of deep-lead mining in
Victoria; failure means its practical extinction.
Adelaide woke up suddenly out of its New
Year holiday sleep, switched on the light, locked the
door and telephoned for the police, because of a scare
head in the local Advertiser, stating that the
Standard Oil Co. of America had bought 50 square
miles of Kangaroo Island, and proposed to spend
several million dollars in putting down 200 bores in
search of petroleum. The Attorney-General was lured
out of bed in order to tell a reporter that the Govern-
ment would not let South Aus. get into the grip of
the Oil Trust. Then it was found that the author
of the scare head had mistaken the Standard Oil Co.
of Australia, a Melbourne concern, for Rockefeller s
octopus corporation. As a fact, the Melbourne co. is
getting the money and plant from London to make
an heroic effort to bore down 2500 ft., where it
hopes to meet with uncounted barrels of oil.
There is a lot of hard mining work done
in Tasmania without much fuss. Minerals valued at
about £1,500,000, were produced m 1912. Cutting
weights, values were as follows: Tin, £582,879
blister copper, £432,273; silver-lead, £322,103,
gold, £148,275; coal. £24,736; osmindium. £0355 ;
and wolfram, £3OOO. Considering how little the
Government does to help the mining industry, this is
a remarkable record. And by help is not meant
monetary subsidy, but track-cutting, road-makmg,
bridge-building, geological surveying, plan-drawing
and diamond-drilling, all proper Governmental activi-
ties, which are altogether too much neglected in
every one of the States. Only about one-fifth of
Victoria has been surveyed geologically, yet that
State owes its prosperity and population to the
mining industry. #
Statist Knibbs has compiled a set of tables
showing that the expectation of life for the Aus-
tralian baby is longer than that of any other newly-
born in the world. Thanks to Pure fiood Acts, purer
milk better sanitation and the effect of more educa-
tion all round, the Australian kiddy has a much
better chance than his co-eval anywhere else. rhe
critical age is from birth to 12 months, and infant
mortalitv during that period is diminishing all over
\nstralia. In Victoria it has dropned from'lls
deaths per 1000 children to 75 per 1000 within a
decade. The figures show that the expectation of life
at age 0 is improving all the time. Here it is (in
years) :
1881-1890 .. Males, 41.20; Females, 50.84
1891-1900 . . Males, 51.08 ; Females, 54.76
1901-1910 . . Males, 55.20 ; Females, 58.84
The next-best country to be born in is Sweden, where
the exepectation figures for 1901-1910 were —Males,
54.55 years; females, 57.00 years. Germany and
Austria are unfortunate countries for the infant to
see first, as their rates are the lowest in Europe ; in
Germany, males 40.56, females 43.97 ; in Austria,
males 37.77, females 39.87.
* * * *
E.W.R.: It looks as though about 10 p.c.
above par might be a fair thing; but there are lots
of possibilities to pull that figure up or down. For
instance, the business might be highly speculative,
and be the sort of thing that would blow clean out
with the first puff of dull times.
A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, January 13, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
Easter falls so early this year that the
race-week balls will be pushed into the
warm end of summer. Swarms of musicians,
and m * nor > are already scraping the
hddle and tearing up and down the piano-
keys in anticipation of hurriedly bursting
into concert-bud about the second week in
March. Ladies who live for their clothes,
however, are in the worst plight. They
must choose between the Cup finery, which
was heavily rained on in November last, and
a swelter in fur and velvet. Probably they
will swelter. A new summer outfit for fes-
tivities in the sulky warmth of March is
only for the purse of Mrs. Moneybags : the
section of Society which looks twice at an
eighteen-penny cab-fare will have to follow
its thrifty habit of producing its new winter
garments for the Easter carnival of Hoofs
and Horns.
From January to the middle of March is
a period of migration for a considerable
percentage of this city’s population. They
make swallow-like darts to other States and
countries, varied by short-distance dives into
the local surf Manly and Cronulla are in
the damp-towel era, and the bare brown leg
° f fry is a thing of joy on every
surf-beaten beach. Grace Palotta is said
to have been sighted on the near side of a
Manly breaker the other morning. But the
man who thinks he saw her won’t swear
that it wasn’t a mermaid wearing her head
in a fascinating surf cap.
Launceston is said to be ready to greet
the Denmans with bunting and acclama-
tions as they pass through ; and “board and
residence is likely to put on airs and ex-
tras The threatened rush of Society trip-

The opening day of Manly’s surf carnival
consisted chiefly of crowd. Thousands of
citizens craned their necks over the shoul-
ders of thousands of other citizens, and
made heroic efforts to see a few fancy cos-
tumes burst into a breaker, or a small boat
wallow in the trough of a long green sea.
±his was the ocean-beach end of the carni-
val on Saturday, as the crowd, gazing
through the elbows of the rest of the crowd,
saw it. W hat the smaller section who
knew enough to struggle into the right
places gazed at seems to have been quite
different.
Having had most of the breath knocked
out of his body in his fight to cross a dozen
yards of overcrowded Sydney wharf to the
Manly steamer, the visitor at length reached
the village, and bought a programme of
events, it cost one penny, and appeared
to. be worth at least eighteenpence. It con-
tained nearly as much information as an
encyclopedia, with a long list of events. The
visitor squeezed through the crowd on the
Corso, hurried breathless to a sandheap by
. S6 L a ’ aud sat down to study his pen’orth
of information. Then he found that its
commercial value was, so far as he was
concerned, about two-thirds of a farthing It
never mentioned where each thrill was' to
be found. So the stranger let himself loose
in the crowd, and tried to find out things
tor himself. In the intervals he worried, or
was worried by, several hundred persons
who were also on the trail of events that
were happening somewhere. It all seems so
Sl e m ivr e en we read about it over the rim
ot Monday morning’s teacup.
The blue face of Sydney Harbor was lit-
tered with all sorts of small craft on Satur-
day, when Hordern’s 90-h.p. Kangaroo was
snoring and boiling along victoriously at 5(1
miles an hour. While the first heat was
being run, a motor-launch fringed with
white-clad spectators came slowly along
towards I arsley Bay. The man at the
wheel may have been able to keep a bright
lookout; but the crowd on deck seemed from
the shore to be too thick for such formali-
ties. And then, of course, there was the
scooting hydroplane to watch. Suddenly
there was a sharp report, as if the motor-
boat engine had blown out, and two heads
were bobbing in the water. But there hadn’t
been any explosion; the preoccupied launch
had merely run down a little sailing boat
and shivered its timbers. Luckily nobody
was drowned. But if it had been another
bind and lumbering launch! Yachtsmen
allege. that some of the launches pay no
attention to the rule of the deep, wet road.
t is going to mean a long bill some sunny
afternoon.
Vice-Admiral Tate, who has recently re-
lred from the control of the Portsmouth
docks, passed through this town on his way
to Maoriland. He means to sample both
islands in hurried snacks of lakes, hot and
cold, and geysers on and off-strike. He has
a son on the much-discussed H.M.S. Torch
but fails to rise to any leading questions the
curious may throw out re that ancient craft.

When last heard of, Mr. and Mrs. James
Edmond were seeing Manila. There is a
good deal of scenery in the Philippines, pun *
tuated with cock-fights on Sunday. Con-
cerning the latter. Mr. Edmond writes
In Manila the bullfight has been suppressed, except
on ver}' special occasions; whether it is suppressed
on the special occasions also, it is hard to find out.
But the cockfight continues every Sunday. On the
Day of Rest almost every native seems to have a
rooster under one arm. I saw one event last Sunday
at Bos Banos, and backed the right fowl. Total
plunder, one peso (about 25.). The bird that ran
second threw in the towel inside three minutes. Much
poultry is eaten here on Mondays, while the defeated
warriors are still fresh. The bolted rooster never
lives to fight another day. He is reared from early
egghood with battle in view. He goes into the ring
tor the first and last time. He hands in his resigna-
tion, perhaps in a minute, and then his career is
over. In Manila there are some veteran birds with
a record of many victories, but they are few and
tough.
This place is noted for its fine roads (about the
city, anyhow), the brightest green scenery imagin-
able, swift motor-cars, old cathedrals and shilling
drinks, the cigar, at 2 5 d., can also be commended.

A hen the citizen sits down in his anger
and his haste, and writes to The Bulletin
demanding an instant apology and £126,543
damages for daring to say that he was a cruel
thief, when, as a matter of fact, all he did
was to water the milk on philanthropic prin-
ciples, the said milk being far too strong in
its raw state, the belligerent has, for 25
years past, been getting a reply referring him
to Laurence and Laurence. Or, possibly, it
wasn’t Laurence and Laurence 25 years ago.
ihe “and Laurence” is an addition of more
modern days; it was tacked on when the
youngster who had served as a model for the
Little Boy from Manly grew up and learned
how to charge six-and-eightpence. The
struggle to stave off the infuriated citizen
who, wants the £126,543 mentioned above
hasn’t been so fatal as might have been ex-
pected for it was a very robust gladiator
who said “Thank you” the other afternoon,
when the L. and L. office staff of to-day, and
some of the office staffs of other days, trooped
in and handed over an album of portraits.
This was the office’s way of saying “Many
happy returns” of “the senior’s” birthday in
the Law. He had just had his fiftieth.
Mr. Sinclair, the Dunedin man who took
baronet Ward’s seat on the Imperial Trade
Commission when that versatile gentleman
hopped off it, goes on to M.L. ahead of his
colleagues. He passed this way, after re-
turning from England, and will wait in M.L.
for his mates. After prying the depths and
shallows of the Dominion’s commerce, the
Commission will plumb the trade secrets of
Australia. S’Africa is next on the list I
fancy.
Mr. G. D. Doran, manager of the West-
ralian crew who compete in the inter-State
sailing race in these waters on January 18
arrived a few days ago. The W.A. boats are
Hie Eurus and Westana, both 18-footers.
The prize money is about £75 all told, and
the right to hold the Mark Foy Cup for 12
months. On Mr. Doran’s native Swan most
of the boat races are won with the aid of the
Fremantle doctor. This is a light S.W.
breeze which occasionally exists only in the
imagination of devout Perthonians. It’s sup-
posed to fan their heated brows regularly
every afternoon; but sometimes it fails to
keep its appointment from Sunday to Fri-
day, though manager Doran says it is de-
pendable on Saturdays.

The Zoo lady elephant, generally known
as Jessie, though absent-minded friends
sometimes call her Alice for short, was very
fidgety the other week. The authorities
discovered that she had some reason for the
kink in her temper as she had a whitlow on
hrenri° f he u- toes - So they put a large, fat
bread poultice on the part. In the morn-
ing they found that the lady had eaten the
whitlow’ Th WaS StUl ann °yed about her
whitlow. They put on another poultice, and
built it on the part with a bulwark of tim-
ber. Then they left the lady to her beautv
sleep. Next morning the outworks were
matchwood, and Jessie had again devoured
the poultice. The foot was worse.
One of Australia’s greatest surgeons was
called in to see what he could do with a
lady who ate her home-made remedies. He
lanced the festering pimple, not without
some trepidation, elephants being out of his
line. The huge beast bore the pain without
any sign of wrath, and merely shuddered
with the anguish, like a human Stoic. Next
day, being interested in his new patient, the
doctor entered her boudoir, keeping carefully
out of the way of the hearty kick he ex-
pected to get if Jessie could put her hoof
on him. To his amazement, she turned
round, apparently recognised the only per-
son who had given her relief, and held up
her sore foot for his inspection. The sur-
geon tells this story.
When the last mail left Paris, the colossal
Clara Butt had been singing with the Lam-
oureux orchestra. Later, the Butt organ
flung its profound contralto notes across
the Salle Gaveau, where it was much appre-
ciated. Paris hadn’t heard the songs of
Clara and the musical Kennerley Rumford
for three years or thereabouts, and it seems
to have taken the wanderers to its heart, or
its_ best cafe, or something of the kind. Gil
Bias lashed itself into a foam of flattery
over the Butt voice; so its size and depth
must be unimpaired by the years that the
locust hath eaten since we heard it in Syd-
ney.
A line from Paris tells me that pianist
Florence Menkmeyer gave a successful ex-
hibition on December 5 in the Salle Gaveau.
This good-looking concert hall was filled to
its uttermost keyhole, and great was the
applause thereof. The Menkmeyer pro-
gramme included a snack or two of her own
compositions, with a solid helping of Ruben-
stein, Moskowski and Chopin.
Another blow ! This time it comes from
one Theodore Flateau, author of a novel
branded “The Soul of the Dancer”:—
The Manora’s passengers were of the usual types
(of travellers in Egypt) the thin, crudely hearty
and heartily crude Australians, with their vivacious
and terrifyingly sophisticated women folk, and, as a
salt to the leaven, a sprinkling of the really nice
people.
Tone is what we lack, my dear. There is
nothing for it but to import a few more
Governors as patterns—say, one to every
20,000 of us.
The Germans had a day out at Clifton
Gardens on Saturday. It was the 20th
national handclap over the confederation of
the German States. Consul-General Killiani
made a speech which the State Frights
might clasp to their inner consciousness
with good results. Mr. Killiani is a man of
wide experience, of learned mien, and not
given, as a rule, to throwing away his
words. In the social round, he looks a re-
mark at least twice all over before he
launches it on a frivolous sea of frothy small
talk. So the lady voter who feels herself
torn both ways, in the matter of the Refer-
endum may, with all the more confidence,
pin his hints re the strength of unity on to
her hair-net or some other handy portion of
her attire.
Ancient members of the pioneer Cox
family are fast disappearing. Mrs. Francis
Cox, the last to depart, was a daughter of
Captain Bayliss, of the 17th Regiment; she
marned Francis, eldest son of Henry Cox,
of Mulgoa, in 1857. She was 84 at the time
ot her death, and had never travelled in
a train. The one-horse gig of olden times
was good enough for her, she told a vounger
generation. The old lady’s home was at
Mmah, near Mudgee.

3 palpitated at the click of high-heeled

office table is covered with a blizzard of
papers, and no powder-puff or hairpins have
risen to the surface.

Twelve Months in

on a “Triumph.”
Riding for twelve months in the “back blocks” is enough to test the
stamina of any Motor-Cycle, and that the “Triumph” came through
such an experience and is practically as good as ever speaks highly
for its reliability.
Mr. A. McArtney is the owner of this machine, and, writing from
47 Morrisset Street, Bathurst, he says:—“l rode my ‘Triumph’ all
through the ‘back blocks’ this year, and never had one bit of trouble
with it, and I gave it some rough work on rough roads.”
The “Triumph” is the greatest satisfier you can get for a mount.

1,000,000 COPIES SOLD OF THE POPULAR
“BOOKSTALL” SERIES of AUSTRALIAN NOVELS
Written, Illustrated and Printed in Australia.
Price One Shilling Each ; Post Free 1/1.
“ BINDAWALLA.”
By THOS. E. SPENCER
(Author of “ How McDougall Topped the Score,” “ Mrs. McSweeney,” “ Spring Cleaning,” etc.).
With a Frontispiece by Percy Spence.
Crown Bvo. 296 pages. PRICE. 1/-; POST FREE 1/1.
The genial author of “McDougall” and “Mrs. McSweeney” gave it as his opinion that
“Bindawalla” was the best book he ever wrote. He nursed it for years, polishing here, altering
there, until he was quite satisfied that, as a story of bush life, both from the humorous and
the dramatic side, it was good and faithful work.
Those who have become acquainted with Mr. Spencer’s other books should certainly read
his last; they will be amply repaid.
This is the largest book every issued by the Bookstall Co. —a compliment to the author
and a decided advantage to the reader.
“Chandler of Corralinga.”
By R. J. CASSIDY. Fully Illustrated.
PRICE 1/-; POST FREE 1/1.
Chandler was what is generally known as a ne’er-do-well, a bad lot, who was mixed up
in every crooked happening around Corralinga. But he had his good points also, and meeting
the squatter’s daughter casually one day, he fell in love with her, and determined to turn over
a new leaf and prove himself a man.
In his hard try he has to fight both physically and morally in order to establish his right
to woo his lady love, and the story of his battles is quite fascinating. The ringing of the
shearing shed is a bit of realistic writing that would be difficult to beat, and the fight with
the city-bred banker has few equals.
“BASE BRANDS.”
By H. M. SOMER. With Eight Illustrations by Lionel Lindsay.
PRICE 1/-; POST FREE 1/1.
Mr. Somer is the popular secretary of the Royal Agricultural Society of N.S.W., and a man
who is recognised as an authority on the horse and the bush, a combination in which most
Australians take a keen and abiding interest.
In “Base Brands” he has produced a book of uncommon merit, full of the dashing adven-
tures that accompanied sheep stealing and cattle duffing in the early days, yet not wanting
in the threads of romance that are always interwoven with the episodes of human life. His
portrayal of Captain Rundall, the daring hero with the dual personality, is an admirable piece
of work that will appeal strongly to lovers of bush romance.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 13, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Federal Government House turned its
close trimmed lawns into a picnic ground for
the Post Office folk on Saturday. A free
show of some sort buzzed at every corner,
and a band blared and thumped energetically
for the entertainment of the invaders. A
merry-go-round revolved tirelessly, an “<>ceun
wave” afforded diabolical pleasure for those
who love the heaving deep, and daylight fire-
works soared aloft and burst in "the upper
air, releasing strange, silken shapes that sank
softly back to earth and were scrambled for
by the youngsters. Young Judith and
lhomas Denman, shepherded by two nurses,
got a large share of the frayed scarlet silk
and dressed thmselves like Red Indians,
much to the envy of other gaping chil-
dren. Two very hot clowns, with melted
paint streaking off their faces, tumbled on
a platform and played wfith an overdressed
monkey. The big House happened to be
open for inspection by the Belgian naval
cadets; but when the perspiring 2000 showed
a disposition to tramp through the sacred
halls, alarmed flunkeys began hurriedly to
close the doors. Two long, flag-decked mar-
quees, with buffets manned by girls, con-
tained food and drink. The crowd haunted
these _ shelters until the ceremonious tea-
drinking by the house party allowed them
to make their onslaught. The Denman lady,
very droopy and dainty in filmy white, took
some Ministerial guests into a corded-off cor-
ner. The G.G., looking cool and comfortable
in grey, had his watch chain arranged in a
cross on his chest. The idea is sure to
become fashionable amongst the postmen this
year.
Commander Corneille, of the Belgian cadet
ship, wore his red moustache in two exquisite
curls. Valcke, acting-consul for the Flat
Country, and Vanderkelen, a Belgian of
great social tonnage here, helped at the spe-
cial spread. Chamberlain Xevill won many
small hearts with his smile, and worked
really hard at lifting little people on
to the merry-go-round. Prime Minister
Fisher came in a bashed grey hat and
travelling rig, and brought the family
in a car. Mrs. Fisher was away
packing for Sydney, and sober Andrew
hurried off to join her aboard the express.
Mrs. Thomas looked cool in a linen dress
and lavender veil. Defence Minister Pearce
and his wife showed off their considerable
family, one of whom is a small, white-
haired boy named Lloyd George. Mrs.
Hughes floated round in a white lace dress
covered with a cool cinnamon brown film.
The Barttelot lady was a picture in soft
purple from hat to shoe tip. Her military
husband walked in full regimentals. The
Belgian cadets, after being inspected, scat-
tered to chat with friends and girl chums.
At teatime the picnickers went and helped
themselves, and the abashed sun went
down on a couple of thousand hot and happy
people feeding “in no uncertain manner,” as
the daily paper would say.
The Denman pair, with the family in
leash, left last Monday for a loaf in an
unnamed fishing village in the Tasmanian
speck. The exact locality is a State
secret. Later on, the cadets will be put
through their facings in Launceston, and
then the pair will go south to Hobart to
see the Cup run, and attend a few functions.
Chamberlain Nevill is to meet them there
and take his trick at the social wheel. Miss
Verney and the family will be collected on
the way back. The vacation from vice-regal
pick-and-shovelling is to last three weeks.
Gaud Mayor Hennessy and his wife had the
Town Hall carefully decorated for the recep-
tion of the Science Congress people. The
Mayoress, who supported some long pink
feathers and a few nice clothes, had her
frock-coated lord on one hand and Professor
David on the other. Several men unburd-
ened their souls in praise of the gathering,
and everybody listened politely. The Ant-
arctic Prof, said some wise things very
quietly indeed; but he happened to finish a
comparatively unimportant remark with the
rolling phrase “on the illimitable ocean of
the Unknown.” The crowd evidently felt
that it was the oratorical cue for a round
of applause, and they woke up with a crash
of hands and feet that halted and surprised
the speaker. He blinked at them in humor-
ous amazement, then caught up the thread
of his discourse and snipped it off with a
few keen observations. A heavily impressive
organ-recital followed the speeches, tea being
carefully locked up till the thunderous music
reached a tumultuous end. The Hennessys
have thusfar made a feature of the organ
recital at their functions; but on each occa-
sion, some tactful coaxing and shoo-ing has
had to be used in order to herd the frivollers
within earshot, tea and a string band being
much more to their light taste. An organ is
much too churchlike, my dear, and, besides,
it is so horribly loud one can hardly hear
oneself speak. This time the official plans
were.laid with great cunning. The reception
was in the organ hall right next to the tow-
ering thing of pipes and keys. No civilised
person could rush upstairs to tea before the
hostess, and so the thunder had to be en-
dured. Right through the programme, the
Mayoress s pink feathers gleamed like a
fixed oriflamme; but the guests mostly
sighed like the winds in the trees, and
rustled and yawned behind their gloves. I
suppose it must be annoying to have a
>ea f idle organ just accumulating dust; but
the Hennessys are a trifle too enthusiastic.
Ihe only consolation Society has is that it
ft one of the kind you turn with a handle.
And, of course, there is no monkey with it.
The absence of the monkey is a great relief.
Lovie Mueller, the Bendigonian songstress,
is back from Italy with her sculptor hus-
band, Signor Ettore Cadorin. He is a pro-
fessor at the Academy of Fine Arts in
Venice, and has brought out with him some
beautiful bronzes and marbles. Travellers
speak glowingly of examples of Cadorin’s
work seen in Italian cities. About one thing
there is a special lot of raving—a Venetian
monument to some baroness.
The Belgian Consul Huylebroeck, who has
been m Melbourne for years, and was much
m endence in connection with the entertain-
ment of the Belgian naval cadets, is about
to shift to a Government appointment in
Holland.
Alderman Jack Marks has taken out his
ticket via America, and is off early in
I ebruary. His portmanteau bears many
scars, and he easily holds the travel record
amongst city aldermen. Uncle Jack, a bach-
will be chaperoned on his pilgrimage
by Laura Levey, a niece still in her earlv
springtime.
Ex-Police Commissioner Chomley’s widow
and her friend, Miss Baird, leave at the end
ot the month for Monte Carlo, where they
will most probably have a little flutter at
the tables with an “infallible” system which
has been presented to them free of cost for
trial. Neither of the voyagers will be par-
ticularly downhearted if they lose; but they
expect to get some amusement out of their
attack on “the bank.”
People here feel very sorry for Wheatley,

at Greenlawn, the Fairbairn country cot

Sir Alexander Napier, the tired little
baronet who was here for a couple of years
as A.D.C. to the Dudleys, is engaged. He is
to marry Joan, only daughter of the late
Edward Ashurst Morris. The deceased par-
ent was rich—disgustingly so, in fact. Sir
Alexander has no ancestral or other man-
sion in Scotland, where he comes from, but
the projected alliance should put that little
anomaly right. He has left the Grenadier
Guards. By the way, Captain Gilliat, an-
other of the Dudley A.D.C.’s, has also aban-
doned his regiment, the Rifle Brigade.

Mrs. Rumor says an approaching divorce
will show the unwisdom of a husband im-
muring his wife in the country ere he goes
off travelling. In this case, the wife, finding
the country dull, developed a slight but per-
sistent malady which demaned constant
attention by a susceptible medico in the
nearest township. That’s where the storv
starts.

Mr. Merz, the electrical expert who came
out from London to modernise our suburban
railways, has gone to Westralia on the
same sort of business, ere returning to his
native hemisphere. Reginald Wilson, an-
other ornament of the same firm, who abided
with his wife and daughter at Mandeville
Hall, Toorak, has already departed. The
Wilson lady, who was a vehement golfer and
an enthusiast upon countries and climates
other than Australia, seemed to rejoice in
packing her trunks. Miss Wilson, who is
still in the kingdom of flapperdom, was an
expert on the ice, and carried some stunning
carnival trophies away with her.
Theatrical and other circles have been
excited about the sudden departure of one of
ladies of the Asche Co. It is said that she
was expected at an “Antony and Cleopatra”
rehearsal when she "was already on her way
to London. So the astonishing story goes.
And it is going pretty strong.

bi ought Mrs. Powell, the Bananaland daugh-

vision in one corner of the motor. The
Ba-rttelot lady was in black. Chamberlain
Nevill rode out and watched the plav criti-
cally. It was such a busy social gathering
and tea was so eagerly looked for, that the
women could scarcely spare a glance for the
galloping, thwacking contest itself. The
usual superheated remarks of the players
were carefully muffled when the ball was
being fought for on the social side of the
arena; but when it was hit elsewhere, the
wrath was wafted down unashamed to the
spectators.
The G.-G.’s enthusiasm for the ancient
game has had an exhilarating effect on the
local horse trade. Australian breeders had
got into the habit of sending all the polo
ponies to India; but one man recently con-
fided that the handy little animals now
brought a better price locally, and that India
had to sit up and bargain vigorously.
Ivy Schilling was the guest of honor at
an A.D.C.’s party at Government House last
week. Her injured limb—one of those with
which she has made her name—is better
again. She is doing two dances a night as
in the brave days of yore, and generally the
sun is smiling on her classic brow.
Prahran has been giving farewell parties
at top speed to Donald Mackinnon, the
debonair, grey-haired politician, and his big,
important wife. The pair are off to scatter
some of their money among foreigners.
Mrs. Harper is busy arranging an en-
tertainment for the poor souls who are hav-
ing a good, summery time at Macedon. That
handsome youth, Peter Schuler, will put his
fate to the touch with a dramatic trifle from
his own fountain pen. The production in
MS. form has been purred over and pawed
over by the Very Best. It will be produced
on the 25th, and if smoke rolls down on
Melbourne we will know that it has set the
tindery bush on fire.
The trousseau chiffons of Mrs. Walker,
only daughter of the Brighton Ramsays’
have been laid aside. She is in mourning for
a much-loved little brother, who succumbed
to the after-effects of an attack of measles.
It was owing to the boy’s illness that the
Walkers postponed their contemplated honey-
moon trip round the world.
Captain Oswald Watt (you will remem-
ber he married pretty Muriel Williams) is
at Fremantle, or thereabouts, on his return
to these shores. He is the only Australian
of wealth, and one of the few T Australians of
any description whatever, who was suffici-
ently daring or mad (read it which you
please) to earn an aviator’s certificate*from
the Royal Aero Club. He is attached to
the Commonwealth forces, and reported on
the flying-ground site at Duntroon (N.S.W.)
when he was here last year.
Little Mollie Wilson, daughter of Norman
of the sporting Wilson tribe, and sometime
a dainty dancer in the J. C. Williamson
ranks, is now living happily in Holland with
a Dutch husband whom she met in London,
while she was studying the British style of
dancing at the Empire. Another Victorian
girl who is wedded to a Dutchman is Maud
Black, a Gippsland heiress who fluttered in
Melbourne under the chaperonage of the
Best matrons. A third Hollander by mar-
riage is (or was) Miss Julie Clark*, niece
and adopted daughter of the late squatter
Simson and his wife. For a couple of Mel-
bourne seasons Miss Julie reigned as the
smartest of the smart. After a scamper
round India, Paris and London, the little
Victorian heiress lost her heart to a Dutch
widower. The pair now lead the simple life
in Holland. In that land of windmills and
exaggerated trousers, simplicity is still a
fact and not merely a fad. Thus the dearest
wish of the bachelor Cantain Goedhuis. of the
Dutch boats that trade between Australian
ports and Java, is to presently quit the sea
and “grow the what you call flowering cab-
bage” in dear, dumpy Holland. And he
ought to have plenty of time to grow “the
flowering cabbage,” for the skippers of the
East Indies service retire at 45.
