POLITICAL
POINTS
Australia’s Postmaster-General, C. E.
Frazer, who is at present on a visit to
Maoriland, has there broken the not too
pleasant news that Sydney’s
Labor daily is not expected
to be on the streets until
some little time after the
Federal elections in May.
LOOKING AHEAD.
GRANNY : “ Playin' with that big, fierce dog
agin, are you?"
THE BOY : “ He’s not a big, fierce dog. He's
only a pup .”
GRANNY: “ But 'e'll grow into a big, fierce dog
some day, an then he’ll bite somebody, an'
they'll die."
Deakin’s retirement from the leadership
of the Fusion party on the ground of ill-
health recalls that he sustained one previous
retirement for the same reason. That time
he had coalesced with the Tories of the
borrow and boom period. Coalitions seem
very trying to even a man as affable as
Alfred.
The Fat party is barracking solidly for
the State savings banks as against those
owned and run by the Commonwealth. The
anti-Sosh party, as usual, doesn’t know that
the thing is loaded. The one reason why
the States want to hang on to the Savings
Bank is that they may have control of the
funds in order to use them. So the party
which says Socialism is a tiger is barrack-
ing for a system whose object is to provide
sustaining food for the ravening animal.
During the Sugar Trust case in Maori-
land, Chief Justice Stout took a dive into
history, and brought up something of in-
terest about the crimes of “forestalling,”
“engrossing” and “regrating.” This was
the old law-book definition :
Forestaller is he that buyeth corne, eattell or other
merchandise, whatsoever is saleable, by the way as it
eometh to markets, faires, or other like places to be
sold, to the intent that he may sell the same againe,
at a more high and deer price, in prejudice and hurt
of the Commonwealth and people.
Pirate Enterprise is always blaming the
atrocious Labor party for "interfering” with
ancient laws. As a matter of fact, it is
Pirate Enterprise that has done most of the
“interfering” with ancient laws: the Labor
party is doing little except to root up a few
of the weeds that bowelless commercialism
has planted within the last three-quarters of
a century.
There was a terrible shindy recently
when the Sydney Water and Sewerage
Board wept on the public’s shoulder and
wailed that it had no money to carry on
its works because the shockingly wasteful
Labor Government had starved it. Henley,
M.L.A., who is a member of the Board and
a maddened Liberal politician, shivered the
atmosphere with his protest; another mem-
ber was so overcome that he described the
Government as inhuman or thereabouts;
and the Tory press shrugged itself and asked
what else was to be expected from Labor.
The Minister at this stage arose in the
middle distance and read a few figures as
follows: —
Voted to the
Year. Board by Amount.
1910 .. Liberal Government ..£440,000
1911 . . Labor Government . . 484,000
1912 .. Labor Government .. 585,000
Since then there has been a comparative
calm.
“Singemoine” holds an autopsy :—-
Alfred Deakin, clear-sighted and honest, has his
prototype in England in A. J. Balfour, the philo-
sophic doubter whom the Tories deposed the other
day. The wholly satisfactory party leader is one who
is either so elastic in conscience that he can swallow
anything, regardless of his own views, or so tractable
as to be able to absorb the whole party programme at
a gulp, and believe the sentiments are his own. Joseph
Carruthers was of the former class. All political
manoeuvres came alike to him, provided they seemed
profitable. To-day a flamboyant King’s man, to-
morrow he might head an agitation to evict the
Governor-General. Now a Socialist, now an anti-
Socialist, he was one thing always, and that was
thorough. Honest doubt he refused to know the
moment the party whip cracked. G. H. Reid was
much the same —prepared to take up anything and
drop anything as the exigencies of the situation de-
manded, and remain jolly and fat through it all.
Andrew Fisher is a good example of the other sort of
leader—the one who is not bowed down by an over-
analytical intellect. The party shibboleths are good
enough for him. He wouldn’t criticise one of them
for worlds, and he couldn’t if he would. Deakin, as
leader of a high Protectionist party, with democratic
leanings, was in his element. He could allow him-
self all the introspection he liked, and never
bump against a scruple. But in alliance with tariff-
sinkers of the old blue-mouldy, Tory class, he has
been about as comfortable as a sufferer from chronic
rheumatism. A more cynical and less honest man
would have stood it. Deakin couldn’t. And the ver-
dict of most of those who attend the inquest will be
that his retirement was a happy release for all con-
cerned.
The South Aus. Government is once more
talking about its unfinished Parliament
House. Away in the days before Federation
it started to erect a palace, but the cost was
too horrible, and the scheme left off half-
way. Then Federation came,
and all State Parliaments
dwindled in importance; also
S.A. very sensibly reduced the
number of members in both its
Houses, and the betting as to
the palace being finished was
about 100 to three against.
Meanwhile the finest site in
Adelaide is occupied by a lawn,
and kindly creepers have modi-
fied the hideousness of the blank
walls on the unfinished side.
There is room there for a splen-
did building of any sort, but to
squander it on a Parliament
whose Houses are quite corn-
fortably provided for already would be sheer
State-assertion of the wasteful kind.
Maoriland will soon be entitled to wave
some sort of proud tail feathers over its
citizen army. On March 31 last, 61,797
territorials and senior cadets were regis-
tered, and there had only been 362 refusals
to take the oath and 1161 absences from
drill. It doesn’t look as if the weird com-
bination of Peace Society and Red Tie was
cutting much ice in the Shaky Islands.
THE UNANSWERED CALL.
In the meantime there is quite a little flutter of
recrudescence in our own Freetrade camp. The
disease, which in Australia was virtually stamped out
before the Fusion, has reappeared in blotches and
threatening sores. — AGE.
AUSTRALIA (after the 10,005th call): “Fancy
being threatened with this form of measles
at my age, and not a soul in the shop
willing to doctor me!”
The annual report of the Vic. Government
Statist on (among others) the precious “in-
dependent” labor unions promoted by Packer
and the Abgus makes interesting reading.
The Agricultural Implement and Machinery
Independent Union had 611 memDers who
paid £24 14s. in entrance fees ; other contri-
butions from members for the year amounted
to £lB4 12s. But donations and subscrip-
tions from outside totalled £461 ss. 9d. So
for every £1 paid by members to their union,
kind and quite disinterested outsiders sub-
scribed about £2 ss. The Melbourne Tram-
way and Omnibus Company’s Employees’
Union had a membership of 100; but 22
quitted during the year, leaving only 78 on
the roll. Entrance fees totalled a whole £5,
and contributions from members amounted
to £35 55., donations and subscriptions
from non-members totalled £215 4s. 7d. So
for every £1 paid into this union by its mem-
bers, kind friends came along and threw in
£6. A lot of people were very indignant
when Justice Higgins said that this brand
of union was fostered by and run in the in-
terest of the employers. Yet, if they weren’t
employers, who could these kind friends have
been ?

COUNTRY.
Illustration for up-to-date encyclopedia of
political knowledge.
Apparently a little kite-flying was done in
Joe Cook’s interests just before Deakin’s re-
signation was announced. “It is considered.”
said various papers, “that Mr. Cook has a
good chance of selection as a member of the
Inter-State Commission” —the Commission
which is to be the eyes and ears of the Com-
monwealth as regards its trade and infant
industries. Essentially a Protectionist com-
mission ; and Joe Cook, Freetrader, a mem-
ber of it! If that joke were played, this
paper would expect a codlin moth to be
appointed an inspector of orchards when the
next vacancy occurred.
The Commonwealth Government has given
S. Aus. a year’s notice of its intention to
cancel the lease of the railway from Port
Augusta to Oodnadatta. the first section of
the Transcontinental. Since June 30, 1911.
the State has retained the railway at a rent
of “one peppercorn,” paying the expenses and
taking the revenue—which, by the way,
doesn’t anything like cover the outgoings.
When the South Aus. Government agreed to
hand over the line, it was, of course, an
understood thing that it would be carried
northward, thus opening up South Aus. land
before entering the Territory; and though
the present Commonwealth Government has
announced that it favors the straight line,
the Peake Ministry is suggesting that some
guarantees should be given before the
Augusta-Oodnadatta section is taken out of
the State’s hands.
Professor Baldwin Spencer, of Melbourne
I niversity, has come down from the North-
ern Territory to say :
I spent the whole of the year in the Territory. It
has a perfectly healthy climate. I did a considerable
amount of hard physical work and was never in better
health.
As usual, the papers which wanted black
and tan labor, and were quite sure the
white man couldn’t live and work in the
North, have got into the habit of printing
these things without the faintest blush. In
fact, within three years they will swear thev
have been saying the things all along
Inspired yarns still keep appear-
ing in the Sydney press to the ef-
fect that the late residence of the
Governor-General is to be the home
of N.S.W.’s State Governors in
future. It will be bad business
for the State Governors if the idea
is carried into effect. The plain
fact is, the class of State Governor
who is now appointed to an Aus-
tralian job lacks the independent
income requisite to “keep up” such
a vast pile. The Carringtons and
Hampdens of other days were rich
men ; the Rawsons of modern times
are not. They have to live within
their official screws; and a Gov-
ernor could no more run this place
on £5OOO per annum than a bank
clerk could support a yacht and a
motor-car on his earnings.
In the early days of the Mater-
nity bonus one of the Liberal sheets
remarked loftily that it was an in-
sult to offer any lady £5 for becom-
ing a mother. Up to date, no less
than 19,481 claims for insults have
been made, and only 100 applicants
have been treated with true Lib-
eral respect —that is to say, their
applications have been courteously
rejected. It is sometimes rather
terrible to be treated with respect.
The Tory person is just now yelling in
heart-broken tones for more immigration
with a view to finding soldiers to defend the
country. It is strange how the Tory can
interest himself even in such a matter as
Defence when it suits his book. Only a few
months ago the whole idea of defending
Australia was to his mind utterly absurd ;
now he talks as if nothing else really mat-
tered. The immigration problem is one of
comparatively easy solution. Australia can
take countless thousands of white men when
it makes room for them by ordering the
Tory person to get up and stand on one
side" At present the position is that the
Tory, having engaged for his special use 10
out "of 12 carriages of the national train, is
roaring to the throng on the platform that
there is any amount of room in the two
packed carriages. It is not a very convinc-
ing roar.
“F.H.”: The recall of unsatisfactory Par-
liamentary representatives, following the
American procedure, is advocated by
“8.N.F.” (B. 2/l/’l3). I am with
“8.N.F.” But it would lead to some hot
canvassing in certain parts of Australia. It
is not difficult to picture the feverish activity
with which, say, the Women’s National
League would get to work if a Labor maa
managed to jostle into the Reps, for Koir
yong f Vic.). He would not have to do much
nor say much to have the pack after him.
Electors’ petitions are, I believe, the first
stage in the recall biz. Judging from the
success of the W.N.L., etc., in obtaining
postal votes in the Kooyong electorate —a
record for Australia at the last Federal ex-
citement —the petitions would be garnered
in short order. It’s a democratic idea, no
doubt; but, then, according to Toryism, so
was the postal vote, deceased.

The second ballot makes us acquainted
with strange bedfellows. The Labor man
being out of it, the official Labor paper urges
its readers to vote for Withington at
Blayney rather than Beeby— to vote
for the Liberal in the name of Labor
solidarity. .It may be good party tac-
tics ; in which case so much the worse for
the party tactics that tell you to vote for a
man who will yield you two things out of
the 12 you want, rather than the man who
will yield you 11.
Before the Science Congress in Melbourne,
B. E. MacDonald said he knew personallv
one family which had left Australia because
some of its members had been compelled to
drill with the rough State school boys ; and
on account of this huge misfortune to the
Commonwealth, he advocated a scheme of
purchasing exemption from service. If there
were a branch of learning to be described as
Fatprotectionology or something of the kind,
MacDonald might shine as its professor; but,
on the bare face of things, the man who
advocates the recognition of a social ine-
quality based upon cash can have no decent
claim to a scientific outlook on social pro-
lems As for the family who left Australia
well, just as there are weeds that a garden
would be better without, so there are families
that merely take up the room that better
people might occupy. Whenever we are told
of somebody being driven out of Australia
are supposed to be shocked, and generallv
are. It is time we got out of the habit.
A FACULTY OF FLOGGING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The manner in which he swung the cat excited the
admiration of even the blase prison officials. His
efficiency he attributes to the application of brains to
his work. While it has been customary among pre-
vious unlettered flagellators to rely' on main strength
and stupidity, “The Native Cat” applies modern
scientific thought to his methods, with the result, he
claims, that far more pain can be inflicted and far
more bitter cries extracted from the subject.
—Newspaper report of the recent Melbourne floggings.
Yes, science is the thing to-day’ in school and mart
and field ;
Our trading trebles at its touch, our lands increase
their yield;
The wheels of industry roar on before its giant force;
It is the very pulse of life, its being and its source.
In olden days, left far behind, the man of strength
was king;
And he whose fist was like a hoof was first in every-
thing.
The self-made teacher of the tribe (though slow of
wit was he),
’Twas sad to note how dull his points through sheer
stupidity.
Quite aimlessly he’d brain a wight with one impatient
blow.
He could not understand at all the' surer way, but
slow,
To instil education and enforce the tribal rules,
Was scientific method for the poor benighted fools.
A clod who had his eyes put out with white-hot
copper wires,
Or limbs torn out of shape by racks, or half cooked
in the fires,
Would learn at once the truth of all the morals he
was taught.
His doubts, of course, would disappear, his instincts
go for naught.
’Tis by the science of the beast and not the strength
of him
That those great lessons of the Christ can reach the
faint soul; dim,
Unconscious almost, it is born. And thus to-day we
know
The surer way to drive them home compared with
long ago.
Yet, still we’re far from up-to-date, and lag behind
our needs
To swell the moral harvest and reduce the mental
weeds.
The time is ripe for Science to compel the blind to
see—-
We need a Chair of Flogging at the University!
Some skilled “professor” doing time at Pentridge
might be got;
Some full-fledged felon hid away for blood spilt
steaming hot;
A modern Prophet of the Cat with knotted, bulging
hands,
And claw-like fingers deft to catch and spread the
clotted strands.
All undergraduates would reach his lecture-room at
last,
When all the other sciences and cultures had been
past.
Equipped with everything but one grand scientific
plan,
They learn to flog “the double cross” to prove their
case to man.
x.s.w.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Motor-Boat Speed Championship
contest on Port Jackson, on Saturday, was not a
thrilling business. To begin with, although it was
an Australasian championship, N.S. W. was the only
State represented, and not over-represented at that.
All sorts of wild stories were told of “dark horses’’
and new boats that would do wonders ; but when the
boats faced the starter there were only a familiar
four —Hordern’s Kangaroo, Relph’s Meteor, Monck’s
Rainbow and Taylor and Harvey’s XQQ-Me. The
Rainbow was the boat which made a sen-
sational dive to the bottom of the harbor
a week or so ago. The fact that it appeared
and ran suggests that the owner and repairer are
men who can get a hustle on. Crowds had congre-
gated along the harbor foreshores, wherever the boats
could be seen on the run between Rose Bay and
Manly. Weather conditions were absolutely perfect.
The first start was a failure, but the second was ex-
cellent. Kangaroo, Meteor and Rainbow were engaged.
They held one another beautifully over the first mile.
Then Relph had to retire. Something went wrong
with the cylinders. Rainbow was behind Kangaroo,
and hanging on well; but suddenly it was made plain
that Kangaroo had not been let go. Like a bullet
from a rifle it went away from Rainbow, with a
triumphant roar like a battery gf Vickers-Maxims.
The other responded gamely, but had no hope. Kan-
garoo cut out the trip in 38min. 35sec., equal to
29.8 miles per hour. Rainbow crawled home in
46min. 37sec., equal to 24.6 miles per hour—a very
moderate speed for a championship aspirant. It was
found there was no boat to make a second heat with
XQQ-Me, so the second heat became the final, and
Kangaroo, Rainbow and the boat with the trick mon-
niker lined up together. Rainbow did a little better,
but XQQ-Me could make only 22.6 miles an hour.
Kangaroo cut out at the rate of 29.15 miles an hour,
and Rainbow went up to 26.12. Rainbow will evi-
dently improve with running. Anyhow, it did splen-
didly for a boat which four days before was at the
bottom of the harbor.
Running speed boats is a ticklish busi-
ness. Take Relph’s case. The Meteor has twice served
him a scurvy trick. Last season when the finishing
touches were being put on, it threw its owners
overboard, and Mr. Relph just managed to kick the
clutch out as he shot. The engine nearly raced itself
to pieces before he could get on board again. This
time the brute came to the starting line and actually
got going; then the cylinder head cracked across.
Yet there are people who refuse to believe that boats
and engines have any individuality. James and
Moseley have another Stubborn craft of the same
temper. It is a hydroplane, Sydney built, engine
and all. Just before leaving Lavender Bay for the
club house at Rose Bay the brute broke a coupling,
and made it plain it was not raging that afternoon.
Engines, like horses, have to be broken in. The
Rainbow’s sister-boat, Peerless, also failed to put in
an appearance, for reasons the writer wots not of.
Most probably it was just sheer engine-cussedness.
Rainbow itself had evidently been affected by the
stay under water, as it failed to make the 35 miles
an hour it was alleged to be doing when it took that
sensational plunge. Kangaroo ran like the perfect,
well-trained craft it is. At one part of the race this
boat soared up to 35 miles an hour, and was ap-
parently in no way distressed. It is doubtful if Syd-
ney has seen the splendid craft really trying very
hard. About 35 miles an hour is the top speed it
has shown on Sydney Harbor, but under another
name, at Monaco, it put up over 40 without being in
any way forced, just before being acquired by voung
Anthony Hordern. There is little doubt that' it is
capable of something like 42 when pressed, so the
man who proposes to lower the Kangaroo’s tail has
to build on a 45 miles-an-hour limit —which is tall
going.
* * * *
Altogether, Sydney Harbor was a great
sight on Saturday afternoon, being almost floored
over with craft of one sort and another, from the
tiny gliding elcoplane Rainbow to the towering
Rawhiti, sweeping along like some great white-
winged bird of the sea. The Prince Alfred Yacht Club
was running a double-barrelled ocean race. The A
Division (Dreadnoughts of 10 metres and over) was
made up of Culwulla 111., Rawhiti and the ancient
flier Thelma; the B section (under 10 metres) in-
cluded Yeulba, Rawhiti 11., Petrel, Aoma an,d
Ranee. The little Magic was absent. It was a
perfect day for spectators, the wind being light and
the water smooth. The. club officials were on the
steamer Emu, and gave the passengers a royal time.
AVhat wind there was came from the north-east,
and the whole squadron hoisted topsails for the start,
and made a brave display. Of the A division, Cul-
wulla was easily the most sprightly, and began to
draw away from Thelma. Rawhiti did not get going
till five minutes after these two. and then got close
under the lee of Bradley’s Head. Culwulla led the
way to the Heads, and then came to a sudden stop
through the carrying away of the topsail. This had
to be lowered and reset; and while it was being
done, the others came dipping gracefully along.
Once outside, the usual manoeuvring took place, and
out of the juggling Rawhiti came first on a dying
breeze. Thelma, unable to rake up enough breath
to keep properly going, retired, and left Culwulla
111. and Rawhiti to fight it out. Rawhiti rounded
the ocean mark 2min. 2sec. ahead of Culwulla. Then
sky and sea were blotted out under the mighty
spread of canvas, every puff being necessary. In
the halcyon calm under North Head, on the home
run, Rawhiti could just move ; Culwulla came creep-
ing up, and the margin of two minutes odd was
reduced to a few lengths at Bradley’s Head. After
rounding that point, a luffing match took place for
what wind there was, and Culwulla got it. Seeing
the case was hopeless, Rawhiti took in the extras,
and Culwulla drifted home with lmin. 50see. to
spare.
There was more battling in the race of the B
division. After the handicap boats had got away,
there was a struggle btween Yeulba and Petrel for
the weather position, and Petrel won. When they
did get going they began to overhaul the long
markers fairly quickly, and the front boats were
overhauling one another, the nutshell Rawhiti 11.
sailing well in the phantom breeze, and Aoma
drawing away from Ranee. But it was not till the
Heads were passed that the scratch boats came up with
Ranee, the tail of the front division. Outside, Aoma be-
gan to close on the little Rawhiti 11., and the scratch
boats closed on all of them. Once really into the
ocean the squadron scattered in different directions
with a view to getting a straight beat for the mark.
That was the decisive part of the race. As they
crawled over the long beat to the turning point it
was evident to those on the steamer that, barring
accidents, it was Yeulba’s race; and this boat led
Rawhiti 11. round the mark by 2min. 27sec. Aoma
was nearly smin. behind the second, Petrel lOmin.
behind the first, and Ranee 2min. later still. Then
they started for home, and Yeulba increased the lead
as she went. It was quite an orderly procession,
Petrel’s overtaking and passing Aoma being the
only change in position. Yeulba won by 9min.
15sec., which was fairly decisive iu that make-
believe breeze.
* * * *
There was a time when the regular ring-
sider at Sydney Stadium could gather during the
progress of the scrap which man was likely to get
the verdict; but those days and nights are past.
Now only fools and drunks venture to prophesy.
Saturday night’s battle between Poesy, of France,
and Hock Keys, of Australia, was a ease in point.
It went the full 20 rounds, and it was not till the
fifth that Poesy got a look in at all. Till half-way
through that round he was banged and walloped and
biffed, just when, where and how Keys liked. He
fought back gallantly, but" Australia’s Old Master
was altogether too skilful; and while he had the
freshness to show his skill at its best, the French-
man was the anvil on which he beat his bruising
masterpiece. In the fifth the Frenchman did cer-
tainly land a few right crosses. But Keys continued
to jab and stab, and in the eighth round split the
visitor’s chin like an apple. Poesy developed a
curious style of defence. He simply let himself go
and whirled in a mass of revolving, coruscating fists,
and tried to smother the Australian, who deftly kept
out of range. It was the ganie to play, for there
was sting in that wild blizzard of wallops. In the
ninth Keys backed on to the ropes. Poesy sud-
denly crowded on to him, and served up some heavy
ones; but Keys managed to duck out, although
somewhat rattled. After that Keys resumed the
offensive, and in the 14th was following up a fast
attack when Poesy sent a heavy right to the chin.
Keys staggered back ; Poesy rushed him and show-
ered blows, beating down Keys’s fainting guard and
knocking him down. He had taken five seconds
when the gong went. When he came up for the
15th he had not properly recovered, and put in the
round sparring for wind. He had to hustle con-
siderably to catch it, though. Of course, that round
was altogether the Frenchman’s. The next two
saw Keys come back in an amazing style. He drove
the left into the Gallic countenance repeatedly, and
made a punching-bag of it. Then his strength again
gave out, and during the rest of the fight things were
just about even, although Poesy was doing most of
the leading. The verdict went to Poesy, presum-
ably on the strength of the narrow escape from the
knock-out. But the point is that during the first
part of the fight Keys was aggressive always, and
landed the blows ; and during the second part, which,
with the exception of about three rounds, was dis-
tinctly defensive, Keys’s defence was too good for
Poesy’s attack. However, the chief consideration
is that it was a good, interesting fight, fought with
scrupulous fairness.
At Brisbane the scrap between Leon
Truffier and 'Joe Russell, for the Australian feather-
weight championship, found the Australian a distinct
favorite among those who back their fancy with
cash. The pair made it very fast and very willing,
and for six tremendous rounds the Gallic whirlwind
fairly smothered Russell. Then his boiler began to
feel the strain, and he easfed up. Russell took a
hand at this stage, and ran baresark for a couple of
rounds; but France came back in the ninth and
10th, only to go to pieces in the 12th, when he
seemed to suddenly lose the power of fighting back.
Before the gong went he suddenly turned, and, limp-
ing to his corner, declared he had been hit low, and
claimed the fight on a foul. The doctor declared no
blow had landed out of bounds, and Russell got the
verdict.
* * * *
Johnson’s best automobile and the gorge-
ous fittings of his amazing cafe have been sold to
satisfy a judgment. This is either evidence that all
the monev has gone, or a dark scheme on the part of
Johnson to convince his tormentors that he has no
more money, in the hope that the pursuit will drop. He
says he is anxious to get back to the ring and earn
something. The first thing that struck him after
that declaration was an order forbidding his entry
to a sporting establishment where he had been
allowed to box right through his troubles. Presum-
ably that club was like the rest of the world, disin-
clined to be too hard on a man as long as he had
money.
The widely-advertised importation of
“Young Jack Redmond, of America” to fight at
Sydney Stadium has ended in a joke. He was to
have left his old Amurkan home on the 22nd of
this month for the wilds of Sydney, a trusting man-
agement passed his photograph to. the press, and
the scribes grew enthusiastic over his record. Then,
despite contracts, business faith, pugilistic integrity
and all the rest, Young Jack Redmond, of America,
cables that he is not coming. And the reason:
“My wife won’t let me!”
Vic. cricketers resumed their devotions on
Saturday, after three weeks given to the serious
business of inter-State scuffles. The games were im-
portant to the flannelled —er —gentlemen, inasmuch
as the leading teams were colliding, and defeat meant
probable outing from premiership chances. The day
being fairly mild —150° in the sun —fully 3000 spec-
tators bestowed themselves in the cool, shady grand-
stand at ’Roy, while their champions chased the ball
that East’s warriors were smacking around. The
latter, however, weren’t any too hefty, only E. V.
Carroll (95) doing anything solid, and the tally was
221. If ’Rov can’t beat this next Saturday it de-
serves to lose. Northcote, against North, began slowly,
but continued firmly, making 123 for two wickets.
’Wood struck disaster in St. Kilda, the State’s only
respectable left-hand bowler, F. G. Moule, going
through the side like a bush fire through a grazing
lease, and taking five wickets for 23 on a batsman’s
wicket, while hisf mates, Addison and Fitzpatrick, got
three for 39 and two for seven. ’Wood’s main hone
of staving off defeat now is bowler Ryder, and as the
Saints had accumulated 111 (Hitchen 62) by six
o’clock, the feat is hardly possible. The collision of
Essendon and Carlton was of no moment, both teams
being thus early outed to all intents; and a similar
remark applies to the P’ran-Richmond bout. ’Varsity,
having the bye, indulged in a little recreation with
Coburg, of tlie Sub-districts ; the latter went in first
and made 155, which score is nothing out of the
common, and only notable because of the uprising
of that reliable old slogger Sundries, who compiled
36, mostly behind the wicket. His score was 10
better than the best* of his mates. *
The Sheffield Shield motoh between South
Aus. and the Ma State, at Sydney on Friday and
Saturday, was remarkable for the old brigade’s re-
turn to form. Trumper, the “champagne of cricket”
of old, sent his crowd drunk with enthusiasm by the
stvle in which he whacked 201 and carried his bat.
He was ably backed up by C. G. Macartney, who
made 125 before Bridgeman caught him off Craw-
ford; and when South Aus. got to the wickets, it
was the ancient warhorse, Clem Hill, who carried
138 to the scoring board. And in the second innings
Macartney made top score (96), while Hill once
more showed Sydney a taste of his brilliant hitting.
The brilliant feature of S. Australia’s
fight against N.S.W. in the first innings was the
wicket-keeping of' Campbell. In the Ma State total
of 513 (put together in 347 min.), the S.A.
“stumper” guarded his sticks so carefully that only
10 sundries were included in the total, in addition
to which he caught and stumped two of N.S.W.’s
warriors. Campbell looms up as the best South Aus.
“keeper” since the days when A. H. Jarvis was
half a team in himself.
There were four beautiful catches in the
early part of the S.A.-N.S.W. match. Bridgeman,
of South Oss., grabbed two tremendous sky-crackers
on the edge of the track; and Bardsley, of N.S.W.,
took a cannon shot from Crawford and a star-punc-
turer from Hill in “the country.” Good deeds like
these shine like virtue in a sinful world.
More startling cricket from Bathurst
(N.S.W,). Playing for the Lagoon Club against
Dunkeld, on Saturday, C. McSpedden did it quite
alone. In the first innings he bagged three wickets
for four runs; in the second, five for one run, in-
cluding the hat-trick. So his total tally was eight
wickets for five runs. Despite this demonstration,
Lagoon managed to scramble through with only 36
to the good.
* » * *
N.S.W. Country team,' with hayseed in
its hair and burrs in its socks, descended on Sydney
last week, but were outed by seven wickets.
The scoring on both sides was poor, country making
92 and 203, and the Metropolis 187 and 116 for
three wickets. The general lack of form on the
part of the Country suggests that cricket has been
neglected in the wild pursuit of the pumpkin.
All the “retirement” guff so liberally scat-
tered by Dick Arnst not so very long ago can now
be measured at its real value. Richard notifies that
he contemplates once more taking part in the rush
for the loot. There’s a gilded offer from the U.S.A.
to show the Yankees how to do it, and talk of going
on from there to shake up Barry once more. With
proper training, Arnst no doubt could shake up a
lot of them very much indeed ; but they do say that
he has taken a violent dislike to hard training.
There certainly isn’t much fun in it.
James Paddon, the new star on the
N.S.W. sculling horizon, is a burly Australian
indeed. Fined down, he kicks the beam at list.,
is 6ft. 41in. high, and has a chest measurement of
42in. When he stands up and makes a cross of
himself he stretches 7 ft. from finger-tip to tip; so
he has the reach which is supposed to give swing to
his abnormal strength. On February 15, he will
show what he can do against Alf Felton on the
waters of the classic Parramatta.
* * * *
Longworth’s winning of the 100yds. State
Amateur Championship on Saturday was a mighty
close thing. About 3000 people were crowded into
Sydney’s big Domain baths to watch the tussles, and
the race for the championship was alone worth the
money. Lovely woman was there in a multitude, and
she who loves a Roman flavor in her sport got it.
At 60yds. Longworth was right in front, swimming
like a machine, and an apparent cert. But at 75yds.
E. Wickham spurted on the opposite side of the bath,
and shooting past the opposition, seemed to draw
level with the streaking Longworth. They were prac-
tically level, but Longworth’s arm flashed out first,
and he won by the touch. It was a well-contested
race. Longworth won it in 57 4-ssec. ; the last of
the bunch of five lost it in 59 l-ssec. This is pro-
bably the only occasion in which all the competitors
in a race have covered the 100 in less than the
minute. Longworth came forth once more for the
Half-mile, and therein he had a comparatively easy
time. His nearest competitor was Smith, of Coogee,
who was suffering from a cold, and was playing the
goat by swimming against his doctor’s orders. As he
was 27yds. behind at the finish, it is doubtful if the
affair could be styled a race. There should be some
medical inspection of the racers before they start;
a reckless stunt like that of young Sam Smith’s
might easily end in something unpleasant. The
Roman streak came in at the high diving display,
when McCarthy landed on his back from a height
of 60ft. and injured his spine, and Stevenson came
down on his diaphragm, and “hurt hisself all over.”
McCarthy had to be removed to the hospital in an
ambulance. It is feared he will be laid up for some
time, having sustained a severe twisting wrench of
the spine. Stevenson recovered very quickly.
On Monday night Longworth was again in evi-
dence in the Mile Championship of the State. It was
a mere swim-over for him, as there was nobody about
to dispute his right. He had only two opponents,
and he beat the better of them, young Adrian, of
Manly, by about 75 yards. Brady, the other man,
was hull down on the horizon.
The G.-G. and his lady and local Gov.
Fuller gave their patronage last week to a Melbourne
race meeting in aid of the V.R.C.’s Distressed and
Disabled Jockeys’ Fund. Habitual race-goers, as a
rule, have no consideration to spare for the per-
formers who fall by the way in the dangerous game
they bet upon. There ought to be homes for impov-
erished jockeys and trainers, not forgetting the
widows thereof, corresponding to the homes for de-
cayed mummers. What’s the matter with the idea
of taking up a collection on each of the big race-
courses on the biggest Cup day of the year in aid
of such refuges?
* * * *
“Bloggs” : A cable message last week rah
as follows: —“The post-office at Wye yesterday was
flooded with hundreds of meaningless telegrams, sent
with the purpose of effecting a starting-price job.
The idea was to prevent the starting-price bookmak-
ers from transmitting money betted to the racecourse,
and thus shortening the odds.” It casts a humorous
sidelight on the way the wire is so often employed
for the purposes of racing swindles in the Cold
Land. In England 1000 people bet for every one
sportsman who goes racing. Wye is a paltry little
provincial course, where rather inferior steeplechasing
rages all the winter. But a red-hot good thing savors
as sweet, financially speaking, at Wye as at Liver-
pool. In this case, doubtless, a gang of “heads” had
a certainty which they had backed in London at what-
ever the starting price should be on the course. The
starting price is made partly by the wagers laid in
the ring, but in an infinitely greater degree by the
bets which come over the wires. The less money
there was for the hot thing, the longer, obviously,
would be its price. By clogging the Wve office with
wires relating to politics, domestic events and so
forth, the conspirators would have a likely chance
of sending their choice out at 33 to one against, or
some satisfactory figure like that. The cutest swindle
ever worked on a trusting peneiller was brought off
bv the agency of the wire. One villain called at a
telegraph office with a vast sheaf of wires, got them
all marked, say, 2.45 p.m., and started putting them
through. At 3.7 p.m. his confederate dived in with
the news that Blight had won the Cup at 3 o’clock.
A vast wager on Blight was promptly despatched to
a bookmaker of standing, under the glozing caption
2.45 p.m. He paid unsuspectingly, and he and his
fellow books continued to do the same, under simi-
lar outrageous conditions, for some time.
The sport of going racing without budging from
one’s own fireside, so to speak, flourishes in London.
It is best enjoyed in a club (the Gazing Room of the
Raleigh, for example) or in the offices of a prosper-
ous firm of bookmakers. Here, with good servants,
drinks and cigars handy, the “lover of horse-flesh”
sits in a deep arm-chair, over against a gracious fire,
and watches the tape-machine. Five minutes pre-
viously he has backed his fancy in an adjoining
office. He sees the field finishing—that is, he sees
the names of the first three horses being spelt out
slowly on the thin strip of paper which coils tire-
lessly into the adjacent w.p.b. He gets all the ex-
citement, all the financial gain or loss of genuine
racing, and none of the noise and bustle and wet feet
and rheumatism. The phrase, “ruined on the turf,”
is largely a misnomer nowadays. Ruined on the
Russia leather, or on the washable chintz covers,
would express the situation of the up-to-date plunger
more accurately.
* * * *
J. Scobie’s luck has not been too good of
late in big races, but with two in each event he
should, if numbers count for anything, have some
sort of mortgage on the Oakleigh Plate and New-
market Handicap at the coming V.A.T.C. and V.R.C.
meetings.
* * * *
Manly, Sydney’s favorite seaside village,
began its week’s carnival on Saturday; and all the
world and his wife have been there to bathe and
frisk round in light attire. And there came, also,
all the “blue-bottles” in the Pacific, they and their
wives and their little ones. When the visiting
humans trod on the blue-bottles, there were wild
yelps and roars for a doctor. Some of the stings
were particularly virulent, and a few patients, show-
ing signs of collapse, had to be given medical atten-
tion. Two of them had to have strychnine in-
jected. Medical advice is, when introduced to a
bluebottle, to shake it off immediately, and rub
the spot with a handful of wet sand. Ammonia can
be used afterwards; but wet sand is the first aid.
The sooner the sand is applied, the shorter the
agony—and it is agony.
* * * *
C. Mundy, one of the first finishers in
the recent Motor-Cycle Reliability Test, having de-
termined to ride back to Melbourne, decided to have
a shot at the record established by N.S.W. rider J.
Fair, whose figures were 29hr. 43sec. Mundy set
himself a schedule which would, if maintained, have
landed him in Melbourne in 25hr. ' But he struck
trouble on the rough road between Gundagai and
Germanton, and instead of reaching Albury 6.18
а. Wednesday, did not arrive there till 11.50 a.m.,
and then he took another lOmin. for food and fuel.
However, he made up an hour on the run to Benalla,
which he reached at 3.45. Seymour was passed at
б. and the home wharf at Melbourne G.P.O. tied
up to at 9.40 p.m. He had not kept to his schedule,
but he had chipped 13min. off Fair’s record. He
rode a 34-h.p. Rudge machine shod with Continentals
in both the Reliability Test and the Record Run,
and got through without any troubles at all.
Horse reporters comment on the recent
epidemic of exciting dead heats and half-dead victories
in the racing around Melbourne as though it were
due to the excellence of the handicapping and the
vigilance of stipendiary stewards in promoting earnest
endeavor among the jockeys. As a matter of fact,
the clusters of nags that could be covered with the
proverbial tablecloth when they pass the post, are
very second-rate animals, not thoroughly wound up ;
and the jockeyship, as a rule, is so poor that any-
thing may win. All the conditions tend to close
finishes. They almost justify Moses in bawling, “Two
to one on the field, three to one bar one, four to one bar
two and six to one bar three,” in a field of 10 pos-
sible winners. If the punters are ready to accept
short prices about several of the runners, why offer
tempting odds against the unbacked ones that are
quite likely to win ? The Tote may commit such follies,
but not a level-headed bookmaker. His long prices
exist only in newspaper reports of the betting, and
9-Pply only to losers. For instance, the rank outsider
Positus nearly won the Standish Handicap at Flem-
lngton on New V ear’s Day. His starting price was
50 to one m a newspaper report. A punter, pushing
his way around the betting paddock, would have had
hard work to get 20 to one about Positus, although
there were 19 starters for the race, and the first
favorite was backed at threes. Bookmakers prefer to
have the unfancied horses running for their book
when they can “get round” on the volume bv laying
on es. That’s how it comes"to pass
Moses wins so much larger a percentage of the
money he takes than the totalisator does. He
aS „ laid A a verv , lcm £ P rice against the occasional
turn-up. As a rule he has not written its name.

“ Jeames” discloses some secrets :
Sydney Daily Telegraph, discussing the new play,
“Milestones,” remarks sadly that “Mr. Preece’s ex-
perience as a member of the House of Commons had
not polished him sufficiently to enable him to avoid
an oath in the presence of a lady.” The oath which
scandalises the Telegraph is “By God!” Speaking
as one who has seen service in some of the best
families in England, and who, but for the demon
Alcohol—but enough. Speaking, I say, as one with
personal knowledge of the subject, I am able to
state confidently that to the average British Parlia-
mentarian of birth, such an expletive as “By God!”
would appear ridiculously inadequate to meet most
of life’s more exasperating situations—and that
whether ladies were present or not. Lord Lonsdale,
the Kaiser’s intimate friend, and a persona grata at
the Court of our own gracious King and Queen, is
a notorious hard swearer. As master of some of the
post fashionable packs of foxhounds in the United
Kingdom, his language to those who were guilty of
such offences as heading the fox, jumping on or near
the hounds, riding kickers and so forth, has at all
times been absolutely lurid. It would not have been
tolerated for an instant, even in the case of the
butler himself, in any genteel servants’ hall. For
that matter, it would have got his Lordship arrested
had he used it in a police-frequented street. But
the ladies present didn’t mind, and neither did the
gentlemen. The old Duke of Cambridge habitually
used oaths that made my blood curdle when I heard
them ; and he paid not the slightest attention to the
presence of ladies when enraged to cursing point.
Our own King can swear more than a bit, on occa-
sion, as those who were privileged to meet him when
he was here as Prince of Wales 12 years ago will
readily testify. To sum up, polish does not prevent
the modern Society man from using oaths in the
presence of ladies. The only social circles in which
the practice of saying nothing stronger than “blow,”
“dash” and what not, in the presence of the “fair
sex ” still obtains, are those which inhabit such
suburbs as Balham and N. Hampstead. It is a sad
admission for one who loves the upper classes to be
compelled to make, but the truth will out.

POST HOLE DIGGER
The “Iwan” is the original Post
Hole Digger, and a success because it’s
built right.
Light, rigid, durable, and perfectly
balanced, has no adjustable parts to
wobble about or stick.
Bores holes in any ground, cuts the
hole in a fraction of time taken with
bar and spade, empties with a shake,
and leaves you little ramming. No man
need rack his back with old-fashioned
methods of fencing, when the “Iwan”
does the job so easily, and costs but a

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.

THE MOVING SKULL.
In relating grim and ghastly yarns
My father was far from Dull
And one of his marrow freezers was
The yarn of the moving skull.
Though years have passed since he spun the
yarn
One night in the dear old Home
On the sexton grim and gruesome skull
I pen the following poem.
In an old burial grounds
A sexton Brave
Unearthed a skull while
Digging a grave,
He glanced at the skull
Worm-eaten and grim
Which twisted around and
Advanced towards him,
With a smile the old sexton
Undaunted and rough
Exclaimed alas poor yorrick
And picked the head up, •
When from without two moles
Speedily glided out
And the horrid skull ceased
To move about.

C. J. Hatton supplies the other side of the
bacon: —
There is not a word of truth in the paragraph
by ‘Fax (B. 19/12/’l2), re the bacon business of
Victoria. The Dandenong Co-operative Co. spent
the money it received from the trustful farmers
in putting up a great factory, capable of handling
the bacon trade of Australia, and forgot that some
money would he required to run the business. That
is exactly the position it is in to-day, but it is ap-
parently using every means to impress the farmers
that the fault was not the directors’—if “Fax”
speaks for them. So far as his statement goes about
Private Enterprise crushing the farmers, the reverse
may be said, inasmuch as the farmers have been
getting prices during the past 12 months which
hardly any other part of the world ever sees for
bacon pigs. For the six months from January to
June the average price this (Melbourne) branch of
J. C. Hutton Pty., Ltd., paid for pigs landed
at our Preston (Vic.) works was 5.29 d. per lb.
Usually that period represents the most productive
season of the year, when pigs are at the cheapest point.
If the farmers cannot make it pay at those prices
in the flush season—well, they had better give up the
business. Since June the following prices have been
paid:—
July average . . . . 5.66 d. per lb.
August „ . . . . 7.72 d. „ „
September ~ .. .. 8.22 d. ~ ~
October ~ .. .. 6.79 d. ~ ~
In November (the month in which the Dandenong
factory apparently found itself in trouble with its
stock, and because it had made no provision for
carrying on its business) the following prices were
paid:—
For week ending 2nd .. 6.89 d. per lb.
„ ~ „ 9th .. 6 88d.
„ „ „ 16th . . 6.91 d. „ „
„ „ „ 23rd .. 7.14 d. „ „
„ „ „ 30th .. 6.96 d. „ „
This does not indicate in any way that at the time
mentioned in “Fax’s” paragraph, “the bottom had
fallen out of the market,” owing to the co-operative
co. having been forced to stand out of the market.
It is very apparent that some people wish to cover
their own unbusiness-like methods, and make it ap-
pear that the greatest enemies of the farmers are the
men who have built up the bacon-curing trade
throughout Australia, and are still doing better for
them than any co-operative co. can hope to do; for
co-operative concerns in Australia, so far as the
bacon trade is concerned, are the weakest possible
sellers, and any co. or organisation that will break
prices cannot be any great friend of the farmers, for
bad selling of products must mean low prices for
pigs. The sooner that fact gets into the minds of the
farmers the better for their banking accounts.
“Chasuble” barks at the shepherds:—
The Bush Brotherhood of the Good Shepherd, work-
ing from Dubbo (N.S.W.), has had praise from The
Bulletin, and has deserved it, from the days of
Matthews, who wrote the book, onwards. The North
Queensland kindred body has acquired a new chief
one Halse. This is through a marriage. Celibacy
is the rule. It would be less objectionable if taken
ala Rome for life. What Ido object to is men
going in for five years’ bush work, with an under-
standing that afterwards they are to splice and go
to luxurious livings. Take your wives into the bush.
Marry bushwomen ! Don’t be a quasi-toff, waiting to
bloom into full toffdom. Make the bush your worthy
life work, satisfied that God made the country, man
the town. Otherwise you impair your influence. You
are good, but not so good. Away with this English
prunella, and the principle that labor is dishonorable
and work disgraceful.

The Inky Way
“E.O’F.”: One day lately I picked up my daily
paper and glimpsed the stirring headlines —“THE
LAST SHOT: A DESPERATE STRUGGLE.” With
a grateful sigh of thanks, I settled down and prepared
to read about a wounded policeman and a dead bur-
glar. I discovered instead that the whole beautiful
column was about a game of giant marbles, or bowls,
played by men who have lost the spring of their
tennis days. Seems to me the daily press is too
feverish these times. It has, or- it pretends to
have, an unlimited capacity for excitement; the
sporting specialists are allowed to rave as long as
they like ; and the most sedentary pastime is written
up as if it were a cross between a bull-fight and an
eruption. There is surely something idiotic in
describing a noiseless game of giant marbles (and
whisky) as “a desperate struggle” or “a fight to a
finish” —phrases that suggest kicking and throttling,
and pools of blood and corpses in heaps. Any writer
who applies hot and rebellious language to the slow,
green killtime seems to me to be working on the
wrong lines: it demands, rather, a grave devotional
style and plenty of rounded periods. Of course, this
is only personal opinion. I don’t perform the rink
devotions, and for all I know it may be a perfect
furnace of excitement. All I can say is it has not
got any of the usual symptoms.
“J.F.H.” : The lady journalist is a power here in
London. She has a society numbering 400 members,
a well-appointed club-house off Fleet-street and a fort-
nightly paper of her own —the Woman Journalist.
The society has just held its annual dinner at the
Criterion —the house where Spiers and Pond scored
their first big success after their migration from
Melbourne to London. Lord Northcliffe, the head of
the Harmsworths and the principal proprietor of the
Daily Mail, was the chief guest. He confessed that
he had been “happily associated” with lady journal-
ists ever since his earliest adventure in Fleet-street,
and now employed 300 of them in the production of
newspapers and magazines. They were loyal, sym-
pathetic and courageous comrades and ideal journal-
ists. Their powers of quick observation greatly
exceeded men’s, and there was a pertinacity about
them which made them highly successful inter-
viewers. When Li Hung Chang came to London, he
emphatically refused to admit a male journalist into
his presence. Yet the Daily Mail published an in-
terview with him, and a very good interview, too.
A woman did the trick. The boss of the Harms-
worths thinks few people realise the extent of the
revolution brought about by the advent of the she-
journalist. The coming of the woman writer in her
hundreds, he says, has brought out the woman reader
in her millions, and the woman reader has enormously
expanded the area of advertising; and that again
has enabled the present-day newspaper to do very
expensive things which it could not otherwise pos-
sibly attempt.
THE SYDNEY “DAILY TELEGRAPH’S"
SKYSCRAPER.
THE FACE AT THE THIRTEENTH-FLOOR WIN-
DOW “ Ah! This is something like a
building. Now I can look around without
getting my whiskers tangled up in the
traffic."
“Billy” Moore, of the Melbourne Inky Way (Bo-
hemian side), recently blossomed into a lecturer
before the Colonial Institute. “Australian Art and
Literature” was the title of the discourse, and the
Pie Commissioner was in the chair.
N.S.W.’s Comptroller-General of Prisons has just
started a weekly newspaper for his boarders. Hither-
to, the gaol bird has led an unnaturally cloistered
life. He has known nothing of the wars and revolu-
tions, the tragedies and other pleasantries, that were
occurring in the world outside. As one result, when
he emerged, it took him months to pick up the thread
of life in a proper fashion. The convict’s total ignor-
ance of international and other politics often caused
him to be mistaken by an importer, which annoyed
him and was awkward and undesirable in other
directions. The Compendium will obviate such con-
tretemps. It proposes to give the fallen brother,
week by week, a summary of the world’s most im-
portant news. However, for the good of the male-
factor’s soul, all such news items as murders, suc-
cessful burglaries, sensational divorces, breach of
promise actions, and so on, will be left out. Also,
there will be no blood and thunder serial, with a red-
hot interest sizzling through it. in fact, the
journal w’ill be something like a whisky and soda
with the whisky eliminated and the soda gone a
shade flat. But when one is in gaol one accepts
little crosses like these without a sigh.
Fred J. Ironside, the small man with the tall
hat, who wrote so much about cricket in Sydney,
was a son of James Ironside, a Sydney bill broker ; he
had a sister of some fame, Adelaide Ironside, poet-
ess and painter, who died in Rome, in 1867. The
late Fred Ironside was first employed on the S. M.
Herald under the original Fairfax; from there he
went to the Government Printing Office, where he
put in many years, and became chief reader —a post
from which he retired on pension in 1896.
* * * *
The staff of Brisbane’s recently-launched Labor
paper, the Daily Standard, was combed from
Melbourne (editor), Sydney (sub-editor, chief of staff,
commercial editor, and three others), Maoriland
(sporting editor and another), Brisbane (cable editor
and lady inky-fingers), Townsville (one), Lismorc
(one) and Darling Downs (one). Australasian
“skeletons” should be a fairly easy business for the
telegraph sub., with that lot at his call.

from the use of infected cow’s milk.

demonstrate that a considerable
amount of the tuberculosis of child-
hood is to be ascribed to infection
transmitted to children in meals
consisting largely of the milk of
the cow.”-

IN BANANA LAND
THE TRUTH, THE WHOLE TRUTH, etc.
THIEF ARTIST : “Oh, well, old boy, so long—
must get back to my ' crib' now."
Up at Chillagoe, the other day, the local
branch of the A.W.U. created a sensation
by organising a boycott against all pubs em-
ploying Chinese. But what about beginning
the patriotism nearer home? Hughenden,
Longreach, Charleville swarm with Chinese
tradesmen —and unionist customers !
George Cowlishaw, a dominating figure in
Brisbane journalism, passed in his checks a
tew days ago. He came of a Sydnev family
hnf iv S fu architect, by profession;
but. liking the smell of printers’ ink, he
secured with his brothers the control of the
Brisbane Telegraph some 35 years ago The
organ was in very low water at the time-
but under his capable fist it took new life
Griffith to h ° F r uf ars , the backbone of Sam
Griffith s big Liberal party. To-day its poli-
tics are pure h at. Cowlishaw died a wealthy
man. with the reputation of being a not
unkindly one when distress appealed to him.
THE TRIUMPHANT ASPIRATE.
THE IMMIGRANT: "You talk erbout cricket! Why, we could 'are taken hon the Haustra-
lians and the Hafricans with our Haitches honly. There's Ohh. an' Ayes an'
'ltch an' 'Ayward, an' the two 'Earnes, an' lrst an' Aigh an' 'Umphrns an'
'Utchings an' 'Ardstaff-and Hold Hingland for hever!"
Brother Binglii can be a snob, too. One
ot him, of the Ipswich district, wanting to
a Christmas treat on his kinsmen at
the Deebing Creek mission station, bought
a bottle of rum for them—and hired a motor-
car so that he might take it to the station
in a manner that would sufficiently impress
the poor niggers inside with the donor’s mag-
nificence. Unfortunately, something went
wrong with the works afterwards, and the
beautiful outburst ended in a week’s gaol.
Warwick, on the Darling Downs, is at
present just about as short of the wet stuff
it breaks down its whisky with as Yassberra
will be when Kink O’Malley has his capital
there. The town drainage goes into the
same chain of waterholes as the drinking
supply is drawn from, and the holes have so
little except drainage in them just now that
the local council warns the townspeople not
to drink the water, etc., without first boiling
U- Representations are now being made to
the Railway Department, which is going to
institute a pumping plant from the Conda-
mine, to increase the capacity so that the
town may get a drink, too.

Quoth Governor Macgregor, at the War-
wick Caledonian Society’s annual celebra-
tion :— :
Some one had said they could be just as good
soldiery in trousers as in kilts, but he was not at all
sure .of that. He thought it was well to maintain cer-
tain national characteristics. It would gratify him and
other Scotsmen to return to the kilts, and he did not
think it would be the worse for anyone else.
Well? There is no law in Bananaland to
prevent the Macgregor getting back to the
kilt as soon as he chooses, and if his Ex
can induce Donald Macintosh, M.L.A., to
don the petticoats wherewith he frightened
Arthur Morgan 40 years ago, and accom-
pany the representative of his Majesty (also
in full Highland fig) down Queen-street
daily, the spectacle will certainly gratify a
great many more people than the two Scots-
men themselves.
His Ex. Macgregor, reminiscing at the
toll-abolishing ceremony at the Burnett
Bridge—the only bridge in Australia where
dues were collected—remarked wistfully that
the clan Macgregor had in former days taken
a great deal of toll on the highways. This
recalls the case of another Queenslander.
Jack” Hamilton, M.P. for the Far North
for many years, and champion revolver-shot.
•Somebody said Jack’s prowess with pistol
and gun was something to be proud of.
‘Well, I dunno,” said Jack. “You see I
come of a border family that had to shoot
quick.”
Died the other day, in Brisbane, Tom
Carrol, one of the brothers in the firm of
Birch and Carrol, Queensland theatrical
managers. He was one of the best fellows
in the profession, and only those inside know
the number of lame dogs he helped over the
stile. He was only 31.
“P.F.": A sidelight on Wages Boards’
awards. A.* boy of 16, employed as a Bris-
bane carter’s helper, has his wage fixed at
dos. a week! Australia wants the highly
skilled tradesman very badly • but
there are few boys who will apprentice
themselves to trades at a low wage
tor three or four years while temp-
tations of this kind are waiting those
who won tbe apprenticed. A living wage is
good ; but one that attracts the youngster to
unskilled labor is a curse in the long run to
himself and the country.

The Blessing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The wedding was going to break down the in-
sular prejndiee of the English race, and tend to the
' of the two nations. — Canon Hues, marrying
a Chinese to a white girl in a fashionable Melbourne
church.
W hen British girls will marry Fang and
Foo,
In spite of all their proper kith and kin;
W’hen the Yellow and the White are joining
hands
In the game at which the Yellow’s bound
to win ;
When the service in the church is newly
read,
An mu tlle , couple in the kirk are du ly wed,
lheres a parson singing gay—
“ ’Tis a truly happy day,
An d I feel inclined to raise a hip hurroo!
When the Mongol weds the White
Pis a sign that all is right—
Here’s a blessing on the Blending of the
two —Hip hurroo !
Here’s a Blessing on the Blending of the
Two!”
I'or the death of racial enmity is near
When we see our laughing daughtens wed
With the children that old Asia sendeth
here
To compete with Aryan toilers for their
bread ;
A ud parson, if he had a daughter, too,
W ould give her like a good ’un unto l 00,
I or he hails it as a sign
Of a coming day divine
W hen the white shall freely wed the black
and blue ;
For in unions such as this
Dies the racial prejudice—
Here’s a Blessing on the Blending of the
Two—Hip hurroo !
Here’s a Blessing on the Blending of the
Two.
So there’s hope for the aspiring Hottentot.
He may wed the Canon’s lamb without a
taint;
And the parson will be glad to tie the knot,
b or he s not afraid of savages or paint;
He will wed your gentle Mary to a Lapp,
lo a Javan or Beluchee or a Jap,
And he’ll beam upon the pair.
ake an An § e l> and he’ll swear
They were meant for one another, as he
knew;
When the yellow weds the white,
And the hybrid is in sight,
Here’s a blessing on the Blending of the
Two —Hip hurroo !
Here’s a Blessing on the Blending of the
Two!
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 11, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Excellency Macgregor, with Aide Foxton
and Colonel Lee, is expected on Monday at
Southport, where the Ohiel will inspect the
military camp. There is no information to
hand as to whether he will wear the ballet
skirt of Caledonia, but 1 hae ma doots,
despite his note of admiration for it when
he bearded the Douglas and others in their
hall at Warwick.
Premier Denham and his wife spent the
New Year afloat on the waters of Moreton
Bay, using the Lucinda as houseboat. Yes-
terday they left for Sydney, whence they g 0
on to Melbourne and Adelaide.
The two clever Denham sisters are at pre-
sent in London, and are staying with the
Agent-General Robinsons. Next month
they are due at Stuttgart, Germany, where
a flourishing trade is done in polishing Eng-
lish and American brains and their various
imitations.
An English soldier man, Lieut.-Col. Har-
rison, of the Yorkshire Hussars, with his
wife and another lady, stepped ashore here
last Sunday, from the Kumano Maru. After
a cursory glance round, they departed south.
The party left England in October, and
went through Siberia, Korea, Manchuria,
Japan, Hongkong and Manila. After visit-
ing N. S. Wales, Victoria, Tassy and Maori-
land they will streak back to England by
way of Uncle Sam’s principal villages.
Under instructions from Cupid, dress-
makers have been working overtime of late
to build the glad marriage rags of handsome
Nellie Lawrence, who for many years has
taught the young and frivolous to two-step
and heel-shuffle in the most approved man
ner. The contract is to be signed during
February. Incidentally, Mr. Ted Oxley,
well known amongst tennis gladiators, is to
be the other party.
Bert Walter, well known as an amateur
mummer and a fine singer, was married at
All Saints’ on Thursday last to pretty Eva
Begg, of Kelvin Grove.
Two other recent starters in the matri-
monial stakes are Jack Surgeon, of Burde-
kin Downs station, and Miss Topping, of
Melbourne, a member of the erstwhile Merry
H idow Co. The risk was taken in Mel-
bourne.
Another wedding that will have lost the
first bloom of its novelty by the time this is
in print, is that of Charles Thorold, the
good-looking teacher at the Anglican High
School, at Southport, and Miss Jeffery, an
English girl. They are to be joined in the
firm, determined bonds of matrimony at
Melbourne on Wednesday (the 15th).
Gentle Mrs. Stevens, wife of the manag-
ing director of the local Courier, went to
her long rest this week, leaving six daugh-
ters and two sons to mourn. She arrived in
this State many years ago as a bride. Ada-
vale station, on the Warrego, was named
after her.
Another passing hence was that of Mrs.
Hipwood, Mayoress in 1886 and 1887, and
wife of Mr. J. Hipwood, founder of the firm
that bears his name. She was a Maori-
lander by birth, but had enjoyed about 40
of the best years of her life in Bananaland.
The Courier, in a breathless par., this
week stated that some person or persons
(unnamed) had seen a lion strolling down
the boulevard near Townsville. Probably
it was only the Anti-Sosh tiger practising
on the hard road it will have to travel pre-
sently.
Southport, the largest and most conveni-
ent watering place near Brisbane, has long
been unnerved by a pier that trembled
undei toot and shuddered in the sea breezes
as if tired of life. When the Maid of Sker,
a vessel somewhat smaller than the Titanic,
hit the spavined structure, it wobbled more
despairingly than ever, and the local coun-
cil hurriedly railed it off as a promenade,
but continued to allow boats to take up and
lower their burdens at the steps. Now it
strikes the council that the bridge might
topple over and ruin a good boat, or that a
good boat might butt into a bad bridge*
and far around with fragments strew the
sea. So a new pier is to be built and im-
provements are to be made generally.
Under-Secretary Boyne Hall, of the De-
partment of Justice, is sampling southern
brands of weather just now, and M.L.C.
h owles and wife are snowballing at
Katoomba.
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.

COUNTERFEIT COINAGE PLANT.
£lOO Reward.
HEREAS certain imitations of silver
v » coins have recently been passed into
circulation at Sydney, in the State of New
South Wales : Notice is hereby riven that a
reward of One hundred pounds will be paid
by the Government of the Commonwealth
for information leading to the apprehension
of the coiners and the discovery of the plant
used in the manufacture of the coins referred
to. The Commonwealth Government will
request the State Government to extend a
full pardon to any accomplice who will give
the desired information. This offer will
remain open for thre months from this
date
ANDREW FISHER.
Treasurer.

ESTABLISHED 30 YEARS.
The Australian is still a casual cuss in
pretty frequent spots. Last year his Dead
Letter Office dealt with 1,662,897 missives,
packets, etc., whose owners could not by any
ingenuity be discovered. This was over half
a million more than in the previous year.
There were valuables in 3176 of these letters
—£17,392 17s. sd. worth of valuables. The
harassed officials did their best to discover
the owners, but as 82 of the letters, contain-
ing £709 6s. 4d., had no address either out-
side or in, and, nobody made any immediate
claim or complaint, they had poor luck. Yet
the chances are that each of those 82 con-
signors blamed and still blames the Depart-
ment for the loss, and sighs for the good old
days of State control when a penny stamp
cost tuppence, so to speak.

DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve c o m-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
ion from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
i?
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they ai;e never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

A MOTHER’S EVIDENCE.
“My little girl, through falling on a sharp
tin, received a nasty gash on the back of the
head,” writes Mrs. J. Barron, 139 Alma Lane,
Rockhampton, Q. “It was about two inches
long, and deep, and it bled most profusely and
was very painful. I really thought I would have
to get it stitched. Luckily a neighbor
gave me some Rexona Ointment, and 1
applied it to the wound. It relieved the
pain and stopped the bleeding. (iradu =
ally the gaping wound commenced to
knit and heal up, and in about a week
the wound was completely cured.
I had also a little boy suffering

being so bad he had to remain home
from school. I applied the oint=
ment to that also, and a couple
of applications cured it. 1 would
strongly advise all parents to keep
a pot of Rexona in their homes.”
